CREDIT AND PROJECT SUMMARY
BgrF-oww-ert:     Socialist Republic of Viet Nam.
Beneficiaries:    Ministry of Education and Training.
Credit Amount     SDR 49.6 million (US$70.0 million equivalent)
Terms:            Standard, with 40 years' maturity.
Cofinancie=:      None
Obiect"v 1:       Major project objectives are:  (a) improve the quality and
relevance of primary education; (b) rehabilitate existing
infrastructure and extend access; and (c) strengthen the
management of primary education at the school, district,
provincial, and national levels.
DSscW2p12o:       To improve the guality and relevance of 9rima2y eduaction,
the project would: (a) strengthen the capacity of the
Education Publishing House to manage the development,
printing and distribution of textbooks and provide access to
textbooks for all primary children; (b) provide simple
teaching aids to all primary schools; (c) provide teaching
aids and other needed inputs to all primary teacher training
schools; and (d) support adoption of a uniform curriculum
length for the primary level and the phased introduction of
multi-grade teaching practices and bilingual education in
schools catering to ethnic minority children. Zg
rehabilitate existing grimary-education infrastructure and
extend accaes, the project would: (e) repair dilapidated
and dangerous classrooms; (f) construct a limited number of
new classrooms (to overcome problems associated with triple-
shifting, incomplete schools, and lack of access); and (g)
provide water supply, latrines, and furniture. These
interventions would be targeted in three phases: (i) five
largest cities, (ii) eleven provinces of the Mekong Delta,
and (iii) eight mountainous provinces. To strngthen
management ot the primarv education system, the project
would support: (h) software and hardware for developing an
education management information system and training local
education officials in its use; (i) study tours and
fellowships for selected education managers to learn from
counterparts overseas; (j) in-country seminars to
disseminate these experiences; (k) studies related to the
rationalization and development of the education system; (1)
an education monitoring and evaluation system; (m)
establishment of a Project Coordination Unit within the
Ministry of Education and Training to improve the
implementation of education projects; and (o) preparation of
future education investment proposals.



- iv -
n&fitLj:          The project should provide gains in efficiency and equity
for both the education system and the society at large. All
education inputs are aimed at reducing wastage (repetition,
dropout) and improving throughput. Better facilities and
access to a full set of textbooks and classroom learning
aids is a crucial step toward a better--and therefore more
efficient--educational environment. The project would
provide resources and strategies for reaching and keeping in
school hard-to-reach and hard-to-teach groups while
addressing systemic distortions in the provision of primary
education, thus reaching the poorer segments of the
population. Other specific project benefits would include:
(a) a streamlined curriculum (as reflected in the new
textbooks); (b) a more cost-effective and financially
sustainable system for producing and printing textbooks,
deriving in part from the introduction of competitive
bidding procedures; (c) an improved system for judging
classroom needs, prioritizing resource allocation, and
empowering district education authorities (through
introduction of the school mapping approach); (d) stronger,
more durable, and ultimately less costly classrooms in the
Mekong delta (through introduction of improved construction
techniques for building typhoon- and flood-resistant
schools); (e) improvements to health conditions at primary
schools (through provision of latrines and drinking water);
and (f) stronger education management (through improved
measures for monitoring education programs, gathering and
analyzing educational statistics, and conducting national
education assessments).
Niaka:            Primary risks stem from:  (a) the transitional economy and
Government agencies' unfamiliarity with multilateral
financing practices and procedures; (b) assumptions about
the direction and pace of change to ZPH operations; (c) the
impact of declining teacher remuneration levels on the
quality of education delivered; (d) the timeliness and
sufficiency of the counterpart funding, in particular the
local (school or district level) contribution; (e) the
capacity of the MOET to implement the project, optimize
education management inputs, and institute new evaluation
and assessment procedures; and (f) the ability of local
(school and district) education authorities to adopt
different processes (i.e. formalize local financial
contributions, institutionalize school mapping procedures,
and develop school plans as a basis for construction
investments). The project has been designed to minimize
these risks.

SOCIALIST REPUBLIC OF VIET NA.
PRIMARY EDUCATION PROJECT
I. THE CONTEXt
1.1         Viet Nam stretches over 1,600 km. from its northern border with
China to the Gulf of Thailand in the south. Hills and mountains cover up to
three-quarters of the land area. The two densely populated river deltas, that
of the Red Rivrer in the north and the Mekong River in the south, are 1,000 km.
apart and linked by a narrow (40-60 km. wide) land strip defined by the
mountainous border region to the west and the South China Sea to the east. In
1993 there were 53 provinces (of which three were city-provinces), nearly 500
districts, and almost 10,000 communes.
1.2         By the early 1990s, Viet Nam had 70 million people, making it the
thirteenth most populous country in the world. The population grew around
2.2% per year, about the same as neighboring countries. Although 80* of the
population was rural, three cities--Ho Chi Minh City (formerly Saigon), Hanoi,
and Haiphong--had over one million residents each.
1.3         With a GNP per capita of tess than US$200, Viet Nam is one of the
poorest countries in the world. Economic isolation, war, and inappropriate
economic policies had led the economy to the verge of collapse. In 1989, the
Government embarked on a course of radical economic reform (referred to as vkL
m2i, "renaissance" or "new life") which reduced the role of centralized
economic management and allowed modified market mechanisms to function. The
progressive removal of subsidies to public enterprises was a key aspect of the
reform program. While the reforms produced immediate positive results (e.g.,
increased agricultural and industrial output; lower inflation) and set the
stage for improved economic performance, the transition to a market-oriented
economy has not been easy. Even with the most optimistic growth scenario, the
country will continue to face severe financial constraints over the next few
years.
II. THE EDUCATION SECTOR IN VIET NAN
A. Overview of the Education Sector
Organization and Structure
2.1       Formed in 1989 with the merger of two ministries, the Ministry of
Education and Training (MOET) is now the major agency responsible for policy
making, guidance, and supervision of education and training programs. It
develops national curricula, prepares model designs for classrooms, produces
standard textbooks and teaching aids, and administers teacher salary scales.
It is under the leadership of a Minister, a First Vice-Minister, and three
Vice-Ministers, each responsible for several Departments and related agencies
(See Chart 1: Organizational Chart of the Ministry of Education and Training).



- 2 -
2.2       The general education system consists of three cycles:  a five-year
gilmarv cycl (Grades One through Five), a four-year lower-secondary cycle
(Grades Six through Nine), and a three-year upper-secondary cycle (Grades Ten
through Twelve).   The first nine years of formal educacion are considered by
the Vietnamese to be "basic" education. Although the Government has been
trying to separate primary from lower-secondary education, both pedagogically
and physically, they continue to occupy the same premises in a declining but
substantial proportion of schools.y
2.3       Technical and vocational education is offered in three-year courses
following lower-secondary education.' "Professional" education (e.g.,
primary teacher training) Is offered in three-year courses following lower-
secondary education or in two-year courses following upper-secondary
education. Com2lementary education (a four-year upper-secondary equivalency
course for working adults) is offered for those who have completed lower-
secondary education. University courses of three to six years are offered to
those who successfully pass the required examinations after the upper-
secondary course. Continuing education (three- to five-year courses for
adults) is offered for those who have completed upper-secondary education.
Post-graduate courses of two or three years are available to qualified
candidates following university and continuing-education courses.
2.4       The university system in Viet Nam is still largely the
responsibility of the national government, which covers expenditures on
professors' salaries, maintenance and operating costs, and capital investments
(construction and new equipment). Secondary education and primary teacher
training institutions are the responsibility of Viet Nam's 53 provincial
governments, which finance both recurrent and capital costs. Primary schools
are the shared responsibility of Viet Nam's 500 district governments (which
pay primary teachers' salaries), 9,600 commune governments (which contribute
to operating and capital costs), and local Parents' Associations (which
finance most construction and maintenance costs).Y
V         In the 1991-92 school year, for example, some 16,400 schools
offered basic education. Of these, 27% (about 4,400 schools) offered primary
education only, 18% (about 2,900 schools) offered lower-secondary education
only, and the remaining 55% (about 9,100 schools) offered both primary and
lower secondary education.
I         Other ministries, particularly the Ministry of Labor, Invalids, and
Social Affairs, as well as provincial and city governments offer technical and
skills training courses of varying duration.
1         The trend, however, seems to be to re-consolidate both revenues and
expenditures from the district to the provincial level. Within the last three
years, some provinces, particule - in the South, have taken over the
responsibility of paying primary teachers' salaries from the districts--and
have also taken over the responsibility of collecting taxes which earlier were
collected by the districts.



-3-
2.5       The language of instruction is Vietnamese, although ethnic minority
languages are used de facto in a few areas until the children learn Vietnamese
as a second language. Beginning in 1954 (in the North) aud in 1976 (in the
South), all private schools were brought into the public system and required
to teach the national curricula, with teachers being paid by the Government.
With dol-moi, the Government also reviewed its policies toward private
education (particularly at the secondary, vocational, and higher levels) and,
from 1989 onwards greater flexibility has been allowed, particularly at the
higher levels.
B. Primary E_ucation
2.6       In the school year 1991-92, nearly 13,500 schools offered primary
education. In these institutions there were 268,686 classes (an average of
nearly 20 classes per school) and 263,215 teachers, over three-fourths of whom
were women. The total enrollment in primary education was about 9.1 million
children, giving an average of neirly 34 pupils per class and 34.6 pupils ner
teacher. These aggregate statistics are misleading, however, as class size
and teacher- -upil ratios varied considerably--from a handful of pupils per
class in sparsely-populated mountainous districts to 60 or more per class in
densely populated urban areas. Additional statistics on primary education are
given in Annex 1.
Strengths of the Primaty Education System
2.7        The existing education system has a number of strengths, all of
which are particularly evident at the primary level.  These include:  (a) a
cultural context which values and respects literacy and education at all
levels, (b) a society that supports equal access to education opportunities
for both males and females, (c) a highly decentralized administrative
structure, (d) a high degree of control and responsibility at the local level,
and (e) strong involvement of the parents and the larger community in
education, particularly its financing. Some of these strengths are facets of
the same phenomenon, but for clarity's sake, they will be discussed separately
in the following paragraphs.
2.8       ResRect for Education.  As in many East Asian societies, respect
for education and reverence for the teaching profession are deeply ingrained
in the social fabric of Vietnamese culture. The country's first institution
of higher education--which prepared officials for service in the royal court--
was established over a thousand years ago. Vietnamese parents, social
organizations and government officials annually make an enormous effort to
enroll children in school, with the result that, by 1991 the net primary
enrollment ratio (or percentage of the age cohort enrolled) was 87%. Other
education indicators are equally positive. By the late 1980s, the adult
literacy rate was 88% and education attainment rate was 57.3%, which compared
favorably with more economically advanced Asian countries.,,
I         The education attainment rate is a combination of the adult
literacy rate and mean years of schooling.



- 4 -
2.9        aual Accles Across Segxes.  Generally speaking, Vietnamese society
values education for girls as highly as for boys, and Viet Nam has achieved
basic parity in terms of education attainment between the sexes.  In 1988,
female attainment was 94% of that of males for primary and 93% for secondary
education. The educational system Is co-educational at all levels, and overt
discrimination on the basis of gender has been minimized. Girls represent
48%, 56%, 46% and 42% of the primary, lower secondary, higher secondary, and
university enrollments, respectively. Such problems as do arise tend to
coexist with conditions of poverty or the relative isolation of the population
cohort. In the mountainous regions, for example, participation rates are much
lower for girls than for boys, and dropout rates are higher. Over twice as
m&ny females are illiterate in these regions. Moreover, throughout Viet Nam,
poor families may withdraw daughters from school more readily than sons, since
the former are perceived to be more useful around the house.
2.10      Decentralization.  Although control over the economy and the
political system has been highly centralized, t facto control over the
education system has been quite decentralized from both financial and
administrative standpoints. Even though the MOZT has a wide range of
responsibilities, all of these national norms can be and are substantially
affected by the local conditions and resource envelop. This decentralization
is partly the result of an explicit policy designed to give greater
flexibility to the sub-national level; but it is also the result of a tight
national budget constraint which leaves much expenditure responsibility to the
provincial level. Viet Nam's poorly developed transportation and
communications networks also inherit the center's inability to exercise
effective control over the grassroots level.
2.11      Local control and resRonsibility.  Complementing this decentralized
structure is the influence that local authorities exercise in the classroom.
Provincial governments and wealthier districts supplement nationally-
determined teachers' salaries with their own resources. The only national
examination is given during the final year of upper-secondary, so most
examinations are generated at the school, district, or province level (with
the attendant strengths and weaknesses such a system provides). Model
classroom designs developed nationally also are routinely modified and
simplified. Since teaching aids are often unavailable, teachers innovate. At
the individual school level, the principals are very much in charge, having a
major say in hiring and firing of teachers and identifying facilities for
expansion or repair. They also oversee the school budget, which is typically
augmented by fees or afintributions from parents and can be used for a range of
school-sponsored activities.
2.12      Involvement of the parents and the community in grimary education.
A corollary of the historic respect for education and the decentralized
administrarion is the active involvement of parents and communities in school
activities, particularly in the financing of primary education. Primary
schools frequently have active Parents' Association which meet regularly and
raise money for the school. Virtually all construction or repair of primary
schools in recent years has been financed largely by the Parents'
Associations, usually with some contribution from the commune People's
Committees (and occasionally from the district or province). Parents'



- 5 -
Associations also routinely finance operating and maintenance costs and may
supplement teachers' salaries.
Main Problems of the Primary Education Subsector
2.13      Notwithstanding the efforts to enroll children in primary school,
the subsector currently faces serious problems, as evidenced by high wastage
rates. While the causes of repetition and dropout are complex and not fully
understood, dimensions of the problem include: (a) generalized poverty; (b)
poor quality caused by inadequate financing levels and intra-sectoral
allocation patterns; (c) inadequate and/or inappropriate educational inputs;
(d) weaknesses in primary education management and administration; and (e)
shortcomings in curricula and pedagogy.
2.14      Repetition and Dropout.  Children are supposed to enter primary
school at age six and complete by the time they turn eleven. Although the net
enrollment ratio is almost 90%, the gross enrollment ratio (or total
enrollment divided by the number of children aged 6-10) was 105%, indicating
that about 20% of the enrollment was younger than 6 or older than 10. Recent
statistics indicate that the majority are over-age students who have repeated
one or more grades. In 1990-91, repetition rates ranged from 5 to 12%, while
dropout rates ranged from 7 to 27% by grade. W7hen repetition and dropout are
combined, the total wastage rate is substantial. It is estimated that, out of
every 1,000 students enrolling in primary, about half complete the cycle, and
of these, 300 complete the cycle in five years. These statistics compare
unfavorably with other countries in the region (e.g., 640 of 1,000 completing
primary in Thailand and 800 of 1,000 completing primary in Indonesia).
2.15      Poverty.  For poor families, there are both direct and indirect
costs related to keeping a child in school. Direct costs include expected
contributions to Parents Associations, textbooks, uniforms, transportation,
and materials. Although primary education through Grade Three is technically
free, cultural norms mandate the annual purchase of new textbooks and
contributions to local Parents' Associations. Some parents cannot afford
these expenditures, and their children frequently remain without the full set
of textbooks. And children without access to textbooks are among the most
likely to fail, repeat, or drop out.
2.16      More insidious are the indirect costs (or opportunity costs)
associated with schooling. Children are frequently taken out of school when
they are considered old enough to be productive members of the family unit.
This is a problem which is not amenable to education solutions alone and is
typically overcome when thie general level of income rises to a point where
families can dispense with child labor. Even so, education investments can
positively influence parental decisions, either by making the learning
environment sufficiently attractive that parents are willing to forego
potential income and invest in their children's education, or by reinforcing
parent's perceptions that potential benefits of schooling outweigh the costs.
2.17      As in many developing countries, poverty in Viet Nam is still a
predominantly rural phenomenon. Poverty also has a strong regional dimension,



- 6 -
with north and central provinces generally poorer than southern provinces.
Nevertheless, there is great variation within individual provinces, which
makes targeting interventions doubly difficult. Mountainous regions are
clearly the most disadvantaged in terms of availability of infrastructure and
social services and their remoteness from markets and information centers.
Viet Nam's ethnic minorities (which comprise 13% of the total population and
reside largely in mountainous areas) pose a particular challenge since they
are amongst the poorest and least educated people in Viet Nam.
2.18      Financing.  Expenditures on formal education represented only 2% of
GDP in 1990, compared with an average of 3% of GDP for other countries in the
region. Even though the allocation for education in the Government's
consolidated budget increased from 6.9% in 1986 to 8.1% in 1989 and 12% in
1991, education's share was still modest in comparison with the allocation for
education in many other developing countries, which allocate about 15-20% to
education. Because of insufficient resources, the Government has been unable
to sustain its achievements in the education sector, much less keep pace with
population growth, improve the system, or expand it to those areas (such as
the Mekong Delta and the mountains) and groups (particularly ethnic
minorities) less well served.
2.19      One aspect of the financing issue is the ineguitable distribution
of resources among regions and social groups. Because 80% of the budget for
primary education derives from local sources, actual per capita expenditures
on primary vary dramatically among provinces as well as within them. For all
the advantages of the decentralized system, it preserves and even exacerbates
regional disparities and inequitable access to education opportunities.
2.20       Education Inputs. Inadequate and/or inappropriate educational
inputs frequently stem from the lack of resources and include: a
deteriorating and inadequate infrastructure; insufficient and poor-quality
textbooks; a lack of equipment, furniture, and teaching aids; a poorly paid
and decreasingly motivated teaching corps; and an inability to develop (and
finance) solutions for the obdurate and special problems of ethnic-minority
children.
2.21      One of the most serious problems is posed by deteriorating and
insufficient Dhysical facilities. Over the course of project preparation,
existing primary schools in Viet Nam were divided into three categories: (i)
those in good condition, needing only minor repair; (ii) those requiring major
repair or rehabilitation; and (iii) those which were so dilapidated or flimsy
that they needed to be replaced altogether. Unfortunately, most facilities
fell into the second or third category. In the cities, many primary schools
were built before Independence and have become seriously dilapidated and even
dangerous. In the Mekong Delta, many are so-called "temporary" schools, built
by local communities of branches, palm leaves, and thatch on mud foundations.
These are highly impermanent structures which suffer major damage during the
annual rainy season and must be replaced every second or third year. Schools
in mountainous areas are typically built of branches and mud and are dark,
unstable, and unsuitable for effective teaching and learning. Moreover, most
schools do not provide adequate sanitary facilities.



- 7 -
2.22      Limited access to education is also a factor:  in ethnic minority
areas where schools may not exist; in remote, rural, and Mekong Delta areas
where schools may not offer all five grades; and in cities where schools may
be severely overcrowded. Although each commune is supposed to have a primary
school, small communities in the sparsely settled mountainous regions may not.
In such a setting--where there may be only 15 to 20 primary aged children--the
traditional solution of building a classroom and providing a teacher for each
grade level is both impractical and costly. In the past, the practice was to
bring such children to boarding schools in the lowlands. Evidence suggests
that this approach was expensive and not particularly effective in keeping
young children in school.
2.23      In some areas, existing schools do not have enough classrooms for
all children in the catchment area, and the pattern is to have a "central"
school (offering all five grades of the primary cycle), surrounded by one or
more "affiliate" schools which offer the lower grades only. Such facilities,
designated incomplete affiliate schools, may be located from one to fifteen
kilometers away from the central school. Once the grade(s) offered by the
affiliate school have been completed, the child is expected to travel the
additional distance to the central school. For many children, this additional
distance is too great.
2.24      Most primary schools use a double shift, each classroom being used
by one class in the morning and another in the afternoon. However,
overcrowding is so severe in the large cities and some areas of the Mekong
Delta that classrooms may be used by as many as three classes each day. This
practice, known as triple-shifting, has many disadvantages. The school day
starts early and ends late. One of the three sessions is invariably shorter
than the other two and is of insufficient length for the already tight
curriculum. Children in this session are short-changed in terms of contact
hours with the teacher and the material, with the result that they do less
well and are more likely to repeat or drop out.
2.25      The quality of education delivered is negatively impacted by
inadequate and insufficient textbooks and deficient learning materials. It is
customary for students to try to purchase a full set of textbooks every year.
Textbooks are produced by the Education Publishing House (EPH) under the MOET.
EPH faces a number of problems. Some relate to publishing: arbitrary
selection of authors; poor editing services; low-quality manuscript designs;
poor illustrations and page layout. Others relate to printing: arbitrary
selection of printers; poor-quality printing services; and low-quality, high-
priced paper. EPH has not been concerned about the books' poor physical
quality since they are not expected to be used more than one school year.
Still others relate the distribution system: inadequate packaging leaves
books vulnerable to transportation, handling, and storage damage; poor
scheduling; and inadequate storage in provincial warehouses.
2.26      EPH financial management practices need improvement.  Textbooks are
sold at a quarter or less of their true manufacturing cost, partly due to the
Government's attempt to keep textbooks affordable to the largest number of
children, and partly because EPH obtains some inputs at subsidized prices.



- a -
Although EPH is expected to rely on the substantial inputs and the book sales
to break even, it is able to recover only a portion of its costs. As a
production unit, it is chronically short of cash and in debt to its creditors,
since it borrows money to produce books, then pays it back little by little,
as it is recovered from schools throughout the country. Deiays ir cost
recovery, exacerbated by inflation, mean that EPH never quite recovers the
price at which the book was valued when it was printed. This makes for
inefficiency in both economic and educational terms. The production calendar
is dependent upon financial resource availability and the pace of cost
recovery. In consequence, production capacity is not used optimally
throughout the year. Furthermore, because of its financial constraints, EPH
produces the number of textbooks it estimates can be sold, rather than the
number required to provide all children with books.
2.27      EPH's problems are exacerbated by curriculum variation and having
to produce many textbooks for each grade. At present, three curricula exist:
i) the standard curriculum (of 165 weeks total, or 33 weeks per school year,
taught in the majority of lowland schools); ii) the abridged curriculum (of
120 weeks total, or 24 weeks per school year, taught in isolated ethnic
minority areas); and iii) the abbreviated curriculum (of 100 weeks total,
taught to youth and young adults pursuing evening primary-equivalency
courses). Although there is merit in adapting curriculum to local
circumstances, the Government simply cannot afford to produce different
textbooks for each curricula. There are also too many textbooks for each
grade; there are ten for Grade One alone. It is estimated that only 60% of
the primary students have a full set of textbooks geared to the standard
curriculum; the remaining students try to cope with partial sets.
2.28      Until 1989, the Government attempted to supply a complete set of
textbooks free of charge to all primary-school children. When it became
apparent that financial constraints would preclude achieving this objective,
the Government shifted to a policy of selling textbooks, albeit at highly
subsidized prices. Children who could not afford to buy were supposed to be
able to rent or borrow textbooks from the school library. This system did not
work because parents and pupils were still oriented toward trying to buy
books, the books themselves were insufficiently durable to last beyond one
year, and schools rarely had sufficient funds to finance the rental and loan
scheme.
2.29      Given the inability to provide all primary children with textbooks
of low quality at subsidized prices, it seems inevitable that Viet Nam must
move toward a system in which all children have access to higher-quality
textbooks, most use them for a year and return them to the system and a
buy them outright (at a price closely reflecting production costs). A cost-
effective, equitable, and sustainable system that ensures all primary children
timely access to a full set of textbooks is necessary. EPH needs to be
improved both technically (i.e. textbook production aspects) and financially
(i.e. operations management).
2.30      The project would streamline and enhance EPH operations in several
ways. First, it would strengthen textbook production management by: (a)
establishing a uniform curriculum; (b) consolidating the number of titles for



- 9-
each grade and reducing the amount of material in each textbook; (c) improving
editorial guidelines and procedures; (d) introducing competitive procedures
for selecting authors, procuring paper and printing services, and shipping
textbook to distribution points; (e) improving scheduling of textbook
publishing, printing, and distribution; (f) improving packing and warehousing
of textbooks during distribution; (g) improving mechanisms for textbook field
testing and revision; and (h)--probably most importantly--fostering an
effective textbook "recycling" system (i.e. using textbooks by different
students for more than one year). Second, it would develop and produce
textbooks and learning materials for bilingual education and multi-grade
teaching to meet the special needs of ethnic minority children. Thlrd, it
would improve textbook content and appearance (format, layout, graphics), and
durability (covers, paper, binding), extending the effective life of textbooks
to three to five years. The Government has confirmed its intention to
progressively reduce subsidies to EPH and Introduce a uniform 165-week
curriculum.
2.31      Classroom equipment, furniture, and teaching aids, where they are
available at all, are often 20-30 years old and in poor repair. Many rural
schools do not have desks; often the only teaching aid other than textbooks is
a blackboard. Although teachers display enormous ingenuity and creativity in
making their own teaching aids, some very simple equipment (i.e. globes, maps,
simple weighing and measuring devices) would facilitate teaching.
2.32      The poor guality of teaching is also a problem.  This may stem from
faulty qualifications (since only about one-third of primary teachers have
proper credentials) or poor quality teacher training. Primary teacher
training schools are supported by the provinces, and although facilities are
generally adequate, resources vary considerably from school to school.
Moreover, all lack the capacity to expose teacher trainees to actual learning
conditions in primary schools. Another factor is low remuneration levels for
the teaching profession. Teachers earn about 0.8 - 1.2 times the per capita
GNp. This figure was very low in comparison with those of other Asian
countries, where primary teachers' salaries average 2.6 times the per capita
GNP. Low salaries have led many teachers to augment their income through
moonlighting. In the early 1990s, it was estimated that nearly two-thirds of
all teachers worked overtime, many in non-teaching jobs.
2.33      Viet Nam's ethnic diversity has complicated its attempts to achieve
universal primary education. Of the 54 recognized ethnic groups, ethnic
Vietnamese (Kinh) represent about 87% of the total population. Of the
remaining 53 groups, 2 have populations in excess of 1 million and another 12
have populations ranging from 0.1 to 1 million. The smallest groups may
comprise as few as 100-200 people. Regardless of size, each group has its own
language, dialects, and culture. Most groups live in mountainous areas where
difficult access combined with unique cultural conditions make it hard for the
Government to supply any education at all, much less one of an acceptable and
appropriate standard. Therefore, even if the full "package" of primary-
education inputs--classrooms, water supply, latrines, basic furniture,
textbooks and teaching aids--were available (rarely the case), it would be
insufficient to attract and retain ethnic minority children in school. Even



- 10 -
where education facilities exist, they may not be used optimally since, as
noted, the school year or school day (or both) have been shorter than those
for the standard curriculum (para. 2.27).
2.34      Not surprisingly, one problem is language.  Children coming to
school for the first time may encounter an ethnic Vietnamese teacher speaking
and using textbooks written in a language that they have never heard before.
Although the Government has experimented with textbooks in various minority
languages, it has never had the resources to generalize bilingual education
and produce the full range of textbooks in the major minority languages. One
solution, namely recruiting ethnic minority youth and training them as
teachers, is hampered by two difficulties. First, few suitable candidates
exist (i.e., ethnic minority youth who have sufficient educational background
to be trained as teachers). Second, in some areas, ethnic minorities are so
mixed that several different languages may be found in a single classroom.
2.35      Weaknesses in the management and administration of the primary-
education system. In comparison with many other systems, primary education in
Viet Nam is relatively well managed because school principals have
considerable authority and autonomy. Principals are carefully selected,
undergo training prior to assuming their posts, and tend to be accountable as
educators and responsible as school leaders. Nevertheless, problems persist
because those in a position to allocate resources at higher levels often lack
the information they need to target special efforts or redress distortions in
provision of education.
2.36      Education statistics (on pupils, their parents, their educational
progress, school enrollments, school and classroom facilities, attendance
month by month, teaching and administrative personnel, school revenues and
expenditures, etc.) are collected at each primary school and transmitted to
the relevant District Education Office. Although aggregate figures on
enrollments and physical facilities are amalgamated (usually by hand) at the
district level and forwarded to provincial authorities, most useful
information stays at the district level. The Provincial Education Service
does its own amalgamation and transmits these totals to the MOET. The process
is slow and laborious, and errors creep in.
2.37      Critical information--such as the number of school-aged children
no in school in a given catchment area, drop-out levels during the school
year, and repetition rates from year to year--never reaches education managers
at the provincial and national levels.1 The practice of presenting education
statistics in aggregate makes it impossible to monitor actual repetition and
dropouts or allow education managers to take prompt corrective action and
target financial assistance appropriately.
2.38      In general, education management could be improved through:  (a) a
better knowledge base on which to make decisions about rationalizing the
F         By contrast, District Education Services frequently have accurate
and detailed knowledge regarding children not in school because such
information is obtained during annual summer enrollment campaigns.



- 11 -
existing system; (b) more systematic collection, storage, processing and
utilization of enrollment statistics, education finance data, and location
data needed for school mapping; and (c) an improved project implementation
capacity, including enhanced ability to prepare sound education programs and
undertake more systematic monitoring and evaluation of government programs in
primary education.
2.39      Inappropriate curricula.  A long term reform process was begun in
the late 1970s, and, by September 1981, a new curriculum was introduced in the
first grade. By September 1992, the process was completed through the last
year of secondary school. Although representing a substantial improvement,
the Government recognizes that the present curriculum has problems. In
particular, primary children are expected to learn too much given the length
of the school year. Such "overcrowding" of the curriculum is hypothesized to
be one of the factors contributing to repetition and dropout. Recognizing
this, local authorities have begun to revise the curriculum again, and the
first changes will be introduced in the first grade in 1995 or 1996.
C. Government's DeveloRment Strategy
2.40      Overview.  The broad outlines of a development strategy for Viet
Nam are clear. Given the country's location and human resource base, it has
the potential to follow the export-led approach pursued by other East Asian
countries. To a large extent, the economy is already on this path. The
Government's development strategy has five specific objectives: (a) maintain
macroeconomic stability; (b) make a rapid transition to a market system; (c)
improve basic infrastructure; (d) alleviate poverty and reduce regional
inequalities; and (e) preserve the nation's natural resource base so that
economic achievements can be sustained over the long term.
2.41      Education and Training Strategy.  Education has a high intrinsic
value in Vietnamese society, and the Government views education as a directly
productive input. The Government consistently has placed the highest priority
on providing basic education for all and education/training beyond the basic
level to those meeting the entrance requirements.
2.42      The main elements of the strategy for primary education, covered in
the Government's report "Basic Education in Viet Nam through the Year 2000"
include: (a) universalization of primary education; (b) reduced illiteracy;
(c) provision of special support to disadvantaged groups (such as ethnic
minority children); (d) consolidation of the primary teacher training system;
(e) enhancing educational quality through improved learning materials and
textbooks; and (f) strengthening the administration of primary education.
2.43      Recent laws demonstrate Government commitment to education.  In
1991, the National Assembly approved laws on the Protection, Care, and
Education of Children and on Universalization of Primary Education. The
former establishes the right of all children to primary education and their
responsibility to complete it. The latter makes primary education (Grades One
through Five) compulsory for children between the ages of six and fourteen,
and stipulates that the State shall allocate an appropriate amount from its
budget for the purposes of universalizing primary education. Government



- 12 -
budgets also increasingly reflect the priority given to education. The July
1991 Party Central Committee session voted to increase the education budget by
508. Approved budgets for 1992 and 1993 have been a substantial increase over
those of preceding years, both in real terms and as a percentage of total
expenditures. The proportion for education and training (14% in 1992) is to
increase annually over the next five years, reaching at least 15% and perhaps
as much as 18% by 1995 (see Annex 2 on Education Finance).
D. Bank Grow, Strategy
2.44      Ovrview.  The Bank's goal in Viet Nam is to promote broad-based
economic development and combat poverty. The overall country assistance
strategy emphasizes: (a) working closely with the IMF to strengthen the
Government's macroeconomic management; (b) supporting a rapid transition to a
market system through development of key institutions; (c) taking the lead
among donors in assisting the Government to plan and finance infrastructure
development; (d) helping the Government to develop and implement poverty
alleviation and human resource development strategies; and (f) helping the
Government to develop and implement a strategy for sustainable use of natural
resources.
2.45      Education Sector. The Bank's objective in the education sector is
twofold. First, it seeks to support Viet Nam's desire to achieve qualitative
and quantitative improvements in its education system so as to ensure an
adequate and appropriately trained supply of labor for a growing market
economy. Second, it seeks to address the more egregious manifestations of
poverty through direct interventions designed to improve the quality of life
for the poor, and in particular women and children, who frequently are the
last to benefit from the early fruits of rapid economic growth. The education
project would also have specific components targeted to particularly
vulnerable groups -- in this case, ethnic minority children living in Viet
Nam's mountainous provinces.
2.46      Viet Nam's long term efforts to improve overall education will
depend critically upon building a solid foundation at the primary and
secondary levels. Thus, IDA's first operation will be to strengthen primary
education, to some extent building on and multiplying successful approaches of
other international agencies long resident in Viet Nam. Anticipated support
from the Asian Development Bank in secondary education would complement this
intervention. The Bank is also discussing with the Government ways of
assisting reform of the higher education and vocational training systems so as
to make them demand driven.
2.47      The project evolved in the context of a major Human Resources
Sector Review (conducted by UNESCO and financed by UNDP), in which the Bank
Group participated as an "Associated Agency." Assisted by international
experts, Vietnamese working groups examined all aspects of the education and
training system and addressed the problems facing the sector candidly. They
developed a comprehensive report which described and analyzed the problems,
proposed alternative strategies for improving the sector, and put forth
specific investment proposals.



- 13 -
E. Experience with_Past LAnding
2.48      To date there have been no Bank Group-assisted projects in the
education and training sector in Viet Nam. In fact, the only operation has
been an irrigation project, for which an IDA Credit was approved in 1978. The
Project Performance Audit Report for this project offered some lessons useful
in preparing the education project:
(a) The Government has little familiarity with competitive
bidding, and virtually none with international competitive
bidding (ICB);
(b) The Government is more familiar with labor-intensive
construction techniques than with machinery-intensive ones;
and
(c) The project design should be sufficiently detailed to give a
Borrower agency unfamiliar with IDA a clear blueprint of how
to proceed with project implementation.
2.49      These lessons have been taken into account in the design of the
project, as follows:
(a) In 1992, IDA carried out a procurement seminar in Viet Nam, to
which MOET was invited. Furthermore, the candidate chosen for
the post of Assistant Project Manager for Administration and
Finance would be internationally recruited and trained in Bank
Group procurement procedures prior to assuming the post.
Local competitive bidding procedures relevant to the project
would be codified.
(b) All civil works under the project would be small-scale and
would use labor-intensive construction techniques presently
used to build and repair primary schools.
(c) Detailed working papers have been prepared for each aspect of
the project, in order to give the Borrower a blueprint for
implementation. Papers not annexed to this document are
available in the project files. In addition, the Supervision
Plan (Annex 24) programs intensive supervision at project
inception and during the first two years of implementation.



- 14 -
III. SHiE RRDECT
A. Project Objectives
3.1       The project's objectives are to:
(a)  improve the _Ualitv and relevance of primary edfcation by
providing essential inputs needed (textbooks, teaching aids,
better trained teachers, and the specialized approaches
required for ethnic minority children) and supporting
introduction of a uniform school day and school year for all
regular primary classes;
(b) rehabilitate existing infrastructure and extend access; and
(c)  strengthen the management of Drimary_edMcation at the school,
district, provincial, and national levels by increasing and
enhancing information available to education managers and
their ability to use it.
B. Project Components and Activities by Obiective
Objective One:  Improve Oualitv and Relevance
3.2       To address causes of repetition and drop-out, the project would:
(a) Strengthen the EPH's capacity to manage the development,
printing, and distribution of textbooks and learning
materials;
(b) Provide simple teaching aids to all primary schools;
(c) Provide teaching aids and other inputs to all primary teacher
training schools (in order to improve the quality of training
imparted and enhance trainee's awareness of conditions in the
primary classroom); and
(d) Introduce multi-grade teaching practices and bilingual
education in remote schools catering to ethnic-minority
children.
3.3       Textbook Development. Printing, and Distribution (Annex 3).  During
NsUUiLtiUGl, the Borrower gave assurances that it would maintain EPH with
functions, powers, responsibilities and funding satisfactory to the
Association, staffed by competent staff in adequate numbers. However, to
ensure that EPH begins to put itself on a stronger financial and managerial
footing, the agency would need to prepare an Annual Business Plan which would
set forth the strategy and goals for the coming year, in particular the
financial, physical, and production/distribution targets. During

8PdsikatiQa. the Government provided assurances that EPH would: prepare and
submit a plan of acceptable scope to IDA by October 1 of each year, commencing
on October 1, 1994; review the plan with IDA by December 1 of every year,
comencing on December 1, 1994; and thereafter, taking into account the
Association's comments implement the plan in a manner acceptable to the
Association. Preparation and review of the EPH Business Plan would be
conducted in the context of the project's Annual Work Program (see para.
5.30).
3.4       Under the project, equipment would be provided to enable EPH to
improve graphic design, page layout, manuscript design, typesetting, and other
activities relating to textbook quality.f  Short term consultants would be
hired to provide in-house training for EPH staff (in competitive procurement
procedures, accounting, textbook authorship, manuscript preparation, textbook
illustration, page layout, graphic design, use of desk-top publishing
equip; nt, and textbook production). 2  Short-term training abroad, to be
carried out mostly in nearby countries (Hong Kong, Malaysia, Thailand,
Singapore, and the Philippines), would permit key EPH staff to observe
textbook production, editing, management, and distribution in countries which
rely on market mechanisms and free competition to achieve better quality at
less cost.
3.5       IDA Credit proceeds would be used for the production of new
textbooks, including standard and ethnic textbooks, workbooks, and standard
and ethnic teachers' guides1'. The major components of production costs would
be publishing (design, lay-out etc.), paper, cover, printing, and binding.
New textbook production is expected to commence in the second project year for
grades 1 and 2, in the third year for grades 3 and 4, and in the fourth year
for grade 5. Textbook print quantities over the project period have been
determined based on the projected numbers of pupils and teachers and the fact
that the books would be more durable and therefore reusable for a 3-5 year
period. Print coverage will be 100% in the first year, 50% each in the second
and third years and 40% in the fourth year for the respective grades. Given
that workbooks and teachers' guides will not be recycled, annual print
quantities for those items would cover 100% of the annual need.
3.6       As noted, textbook production would be limited to only one
curriculum (the standard 165-week cycle). The number of standard textbooks

the ethnic language textbooks. Basic assumptions underpinning the textbook
component (regarding numbers of titles, size of cohort, print quantities,
production costs and distribution patterns) are given in Annex 3, Appendix 1.
3.7       A system for allocating textbooks would be Introduced incorporating
a mix of selling (at full cover price), yearly rental (for a fee about one-
third of the cover price), and lending (for free). EPS would continue to
distribute textbooks through its subsidiary companies. It is proposed that
the local distribution agencies take the lead in fostering and organizing
textbook delivery and recycling (para. 2.30). Such mechanisms already do
exist in Viet Nam, although on a fairly small scale. For example, the
district textbook and school supply distribution company in Danang has already
established a flourishing trade in secondhand and reconditioned textbooks, and
this system could be adapted by others. Provincial school libraries have also
been found to play a key role in managing equitable book distribution. During
the first year of the project, EPH would sponsor a national workshop to
explain the policy changes being undertaken and their implications for
textbook distribution at the local level. Subsidiary companies, as well as
district education authorities, school librarians and representatives of
Parents Associations would be invited to attend. Among other topics, the
workshop would address: (a) technical and managerial aspects of secondhand
book selling; (b) balancing equity and cost recovery considerations in
establishing a book distribution system; and (c) the potential role for
private companies, school authorities, and Parents Associations in making such
a system work. At Negotiations, the Government gave assurances that EPH would
by September 1, 1994, furnish to the Association for its review and comments,
a plan for textbook distribution and thereafter, taking into account the
Association's comments, implement such plan in a manner satisfactory to the
Association.
3.8       The cover price of textbooks is assumed to comprise production,
distribution, and overhead costs. These costs (estimated at present
Vietnamese prices), and consequently the cover prices, will inevitably rise as
a result of the reforms described earlier. It is assumed that, as cover
prices rise and the new distribution system takes effect, fewer children will
buy books, but more will rent or borrow. Hence, the initial sell:rent:lend
ratio of 35%:45%:20% would shift to 20%:45%:351 in project year five and
beyond.
I'        Ethnic minority language textbooks will supplement the standard

although the number of titles may increase as the ethnic minority textbook
content is elaborated and developed.

3.9       Cost recovery would be effected on standard textbooks and
workbooksW. For standard textbooks, it would be on sales and rentals, and
for workbooks, it would be on sales only, since it is presumed that workbooks
are not reusable. It is proposed that the full cover price be recovered. On
rentals, the recovery would be spread over a (conservatively estimated) useful
life of three years.
3.10      Over the seven year implementation period, sales and rental
revenues would vest EPH with a significant cashflow, enabling them to: (a)
develop manuscripts for new textbooks (particularly ethnic language and multi-
grade texts); (b) cover textbook distribution and overhead costs wholly or in
part; and (c) produce textbooks after IDA financing ceases. Without affecting
these activities, EPH could use the balance of the revenues to: (a) produce
minimum quantities of old textbooks (not exceeding 30% of the respective
enrollment) until the new textbook production commences; and (b) provide
flexibility with regard to the mix of textbooks sold:rented:lent.u'
3.11      Based on reasonable assumptions, EPH's cashflow scenario (as
projected in Appendix 2 of Annex 3) shows a respectable cumulative net inflow
of US$ 9.8 million over a seven year period. Included in these calculations
are: (a) US$ 12.4 million (representing total distribution and overhead costs)
and (b) US$ 3.3 million (representing partial recovery of distribution and
overhead costs). If the textbook component is managed efficiently as
intended, EPH would have adequate seed capital to continue the new textbook
production and distribution system with minimum levels of subsidy from the
Government.
3.12      Provision of Teaching Aids to Primary Schools (Annex 4).  A fixed
list of simple equipment and teaching aids would be provided to all primary
schools. The list includes, inter alia, maps, charts, a globe, measuring and
weighing devices, and a set of basic reference books (an atlas, a dictionary,
a simple picture encyclopedia) and is estimated to cost US$250 equivalent.
3.13      Viet Nam has a long tradition whereby teachers develop and produce
their own teaching and learning aids. Efforts to encourage this practice have
included competitions, workshops, and incentives for teachers. The project
also would reinforce the tradition by supporting exhibitions of teacher-
produced learning aids. Two exhibitions would be organized during the project
period at local and national levels. At the provincial level, the school
whose teacher developed the best aid would receive a prize (consisting of a
set of "ideal" teaching aids and equipment). Winners of provinci&l
computitions would exhibit their entries in the national competitions.

3.14      Provision of Inputs to Primary Teacher training Schools (Annex 5).
The project would provide low-cost but critical inputs to primary teacher
training institutions to improve conditions in the schools and encourage
awareness and analysis of actual conditions in the primary classroom: each
primary teacher training school would receive a standard package of inputs
including: (a) grants to cover transportation, housing and per diem to enable
second-year trainees to visit schools in their provinces and observe primary
classrooms firsthand; and (b) typewriters and simple equipment to enable
trainees to write up and disseminate the findings of their visits. Each
institution also would be given a budget of US$3,000 equivalent and asked to
draw up a list of equipment, facilities, reference books, or other teaching
aids deemed most needed. Lists would be reviewed and approved by the respec-
tive Provincial Education Service and MOET's Department of Teacher Training.
3.15      Introduction of Multi-Grade Teaching and Bilingual Education for
Ethnic Minorities (Annex 6). To ensure that ethnic minority children have
access to high quality, relevant primary education, a comprehensive package of
integrated assistance is needed which includes specialized learning materials,
teacher training in improved pedagogies, and changes to existing curriculum
policies. Where the number of children in a given catchment area is
insufficient to justify having a teacher for each grade level or the children
do not speak Vietnamese, multi-grade and bilingual teaching practices should
be introduced. Teachers and administrators at such schools would require
training and special support from supervisors. Finally, to ensure that
exist-ng classrooms and teachers are used optimally, that EPH is not
overburdened trying to supply textbooks for two curricula, and that all
primary-aged children throughout the country receive a similar quality of
education, a single standard for the school day and the school year would
prevail (para. 2.30).
3.16      Eight ethnic minorities have been selected to participate in the
project: (Thai, Kuong, Nung, Hmong, Jarai, Bahnar, Cham and Sedang).W  The
component was designed in consultation/collaboration with representatives of
target populations, local experts, and two international agencies long active
in Viet Nam, UNICEF and World Vision.  UNICEF has done a great deal of work in
the areas of multi-grade teaching, and the project would build upon this
experience. UNICEF would remain actively involved in project implementation
in an advisory capacity, providing support and guidance from a resident
education specialist. World Vision, an international non-governmental
organization (NCO), has had considerable experience in mother tongue literacy.
At the request of the MOET, World Vision agreed to supply two internationally-
recruited technical specialists (an Applied Linguistics Advisor and an Ethnic
Minority Literacy Advisor) to reside in Viet Nam for the duration of the
project.
3.17      Under the project, IDA would finance: (a) 115.25 staff months of
international and 560 staff months of local expertise to inter alia, support
J2          These eight groups represent about 45% of the total ethnic
minority population of Viet Nam. Except for the Cham, all live primarily in
the mountainous regions.



- 19 -
implementation of multi-grade teaching, study ethnic minority languages and
develop children's dictionaries, standardize scripts for languages already
written, and develop writing systems for those that are not; (b) workshops for
teachers in multi-grade pedagogies, teaching of mother-tongue literacy, and
teaching of Vietnamese as the national language; (c) development of learning
materials suitable for multi-grade schools;2 and (d) development of learning
materials in the eight selected ethnic minority languages. Learning materials
for some languages (Thai, Nung, Jarai, and Cham) have been developed but need
to be updated. Similar materials need to be developed in the other selected
languages (Muong, Hmong, Bahnar and Sedang).
Objective Two: Rehabilitate Infrastructure and Extend Access
3.18      Viet Nam's attempt to universalize primary education is
increasingly threatened by severely decaying infrastructure and problems of
access. In fact, the enrollment ratio has actually declined in the recent
past because investments have not kept pace with population growth and the
country risks losing much of its previous investment if long-delayed
maintenance and repairs are not undertaken urgently.
3.19      The objective would be supported through: (a) rehabilitation and
repair of dilapidated and dangerous classrooms; (b) construction of a limited
iiumber of new classrooms based upon specific criteria; and (c) provision of
water supply, latrines, and furniture for these classrooms. In addition,
technical assistance would be provided to help the MOET develop appropriate
school construction and maintenance techniques. Three geographical areas have
been identified as the most needy. The five major cities (Hanoi, Ho Chi Minh
City, Haiphong, Danang, and Hui), where classrooms are very old and
dilapidated, would be supported during a first phase of the project. Selected
districts in eleven provinces of the Mekong Delta, where schools are built of
impermanent materials and problems of triple-shifting, incomplete affiliate
schools, and limited access are widespread, would be addressed during the
second phase of the project. Finally, selected districts in eight mountainous
provinces, where the poverty of the sparsely settled population (often ethnic
minorities) has limited the quality and availability of facilities, would be
the focus in the third phase of the project.W
3.20      As noted earlier, the problem of insufficient capacity has three
manifestations: limited access (para. 2.22); incomplete affiliate schools
(para. 2.23); and triple-shifting (para. 2.24). Under the project, population
clusters with primary school-aged children but no school within a "reasonable
distance" (about five kilometers) would be eligible for additional facilities.
Where problems of triple shifting and incomplete affiliate schools are not
1         Including payments to authors and testing, printing, and
distribution of materials.
W         Of the 53 provinces, 3 were city-provinces, 11 were lowland
provinces of the Mekong Delta, 3 were lowland provinces of the Red River
Delta, and 36 were partially or wholly mountainous provinces.



- 20 -
amenable to resolution through better scheduling or multi-grade teaching,
additional classrooms would be supplied based on dimensional considerations
outlined In Annex 7. It is important to note that the project will not supply
additional facilities basec upon normal population growth, but only against
the specific criteria described above.
3.21      School iapng (Annex 8).  During project preparation, local school
authorities, in cooperation with the MOET's Department of Planning and Finance
and the Research and Design Institute for School Construction (RDICS), became
familiar with school mapping as a tool for determining classroom needs. A
school map shows the distribution of school-age children in relation to
existing facilities in a given area. It is useful in determining whether
problems like triple-shifting can be resolved through improved scheduling or
other means. As it inventories existing facilities in relationship to
existing demand, it is also useful in setting priorities in a resource-
constrained environment. The capacity to set priorities is crucial in the
project, given that the Credit would be able to assist only a fraction of the
total anticipated need.
3.22      School maps have already been prepared for most districts in the
five major cities and for many in the Mekong Delta and the mountainous
provinces. These maps will need updating, and the school mapping exercise
should be extended nationwide. Technical assistance will be provided to
assist with this exercise. School mapping activities should be coordinated by
the respective Provincial Education Services under the guidance of the MOET.
The Government would prepare and furnish to IDA the outlines of a phased
school mapping program for all districts in Viet Nam. The program should
specify the schedule of mapping requirements for districts included under the
project, as follows: Phase I (Five Largest Cities) maps were completed prior
to October 1, 199a; Phase II (the Mekong Delta) maps to be completed by
October 1, 1994; Phase III (the Mountainous Areas) maps to be completed by
October 1, 1995. This program should be incorporated into the AnnialMWork
Frggrar and its progress should be reviewed annually with IDA (para. 5.30).
D*urngNeggtiatlons, assurances were given that MOET would implement a phased
program of school mapping covering all of Viet Nam, acceptable to the
Association, to be completed by December 31, 2000.
3.23      Phase One Construction:  Selected Communities in the Five Largest
Cities (Annex 9). Because dilapidation and triple shifting are most acute,
and because civil works can be accomplished more easily in the cities than in
other parts of the country, urban areas will be targeted first. This would
allow the Borrower to meet high-priority needs while gaining useful experience
in implementing IDA-financed construction. The first priority in the five
cities would be repairing or replacing seriously dilapidated schools. The
second priority would be constructing additional classrooms where existing
facilities are insufficient and problems cannot be relieved through better
scheduling or rationalization of existing enrollment patterns.
3.24      It is proposed that, in the five cities, 665 classrooms would be
repaired and 486 would be replaced/expanded. This figure represents about 20%
of the total identified need in each category for each city. In addition,



- 21 -
1,438 latrines and 72 water systems would be supplied (about 25% of projected
need). Technical assistance would be provided to advise on typhoon-resistant
structures and sanitation and develop a maintenance manual appropriate for
urban schools. Finally, each school benefiting from civil works under this
component would receive the furniture (desks, chairs, blackboards, and storage
cupboards) needed to function properly.
3.25      Phase Two: Selected Communities in the Mekong Delta (Annex 10).
About one-fourth of all Vietnamese live in the Mekong Delta, making it one of
the most densely populated regions of the country. Construction choices
would be based on criteria agreed with IDA during preparation and presented in
the Annex. About 2,000 classrooms would be repaired and 3,000 would be
replaced/expanded, which represents 18% of total need for repair and
replacement/expansion in each project province in the Mekong Delta. Flood-
resistant classrooms would be provided, along with technical assistance to
develop a maintenance manual appropriate for the schools. Suitable furniture
(including desks, chairs, blackboards, and storage cupboards) would be
provided to project schools where it does not already exist. Since most
schools in the Mekong Delta are located in the midst of areas of standing
water during the rainy season, special attention has to be given to health and
environmental considerations. Technical assistance would be provided under
the project to help design environmentally appropriate technologies and
proceduros for: (a) construction and maintenance of about 5,000 suitable
latrines (18% of need), (b) provision of safe drinking water at about 500
sites (about 18% of need), and (c) disposal of garbage. All schools
benefiting from civil works under the project also would be provided training
in their maintenance.
3.26      Phase Three:  Selected Communities in the Mountain Areas (Annex
11). Viet Nam has 36 provinces regarded as wholly or partially mountainous.
Although sparsely populated, these provinces cover the bulk of the geographic
area. Since it would be impossible to cover such a wide area in the current
project, eight provinces have been identified to participate in the project.
These provinces (Son La, Hoa Bitih, Lang Son, Yen Bai, Lao Cai, Gia Lai, Kon
Tum, and Ninh Thuan) were selected because they contain a large number of
ethnic minority inhabitants and UNICEF has already been active in these areas.
Criteria used to identify proposed sites were developed and agreed during
preparation and are presented in Annex 11.
3.27      During project preparation the importance of linking physical to
pedagogical improvements in ethnic minority areas was discussed, and the
outlines of a pilot program was agreed upon. Project-supported pedagogical
interventions (multi-grade teaching, mother tongue literacy, and teaching of
Vietnamese as the national language) supported under Objective One would begin
during the second year of the project in at least 40 schools (five in each of
the eight selected provinces) identified for physical upgrading under
Objective Two. During the third year of the project, additional schools would
be added. These pilot schools (which should number at least 200 by year
three) would be closely monitored, and during the fourth year of the project,
the performance of their students would be evaluated against control groups
where no pedagogic interventions had taken place. Based upon the
recommendations of this pilot program, the remaining schools scheduled for



- 22 -
rehabilitation in the mountainous areas would receive pedagogic interventions
during the final years of project implementation.
3.28      In total, the construction component for the mountainous areas
would comprise: (a) repair of about 1,550 existing classrooms and
replacement/expansion of about 2,700 dilapidated classrooms (about 30% of the
total need per participating province); (b) provision of 5,670 suitable
latrines to schools (40% of needs); (c) provision of suitable furniture where
it does not already exist; (d) provision of water systems at some 570 school
sites (also about 40% of needs). All schools benefiting from civil works
under the project also would be provided training in the maintenance of the
facilities.
Objective Three:  Strengthen the Management of Primary Education
3.29      To improve primary education management the project would support:
(a)  Rationalization of the primary education network;
(b)  Development of an education management information system; and
(c)  Improved implementation, monitoring and assessment capacity.
3.30      Rationalization of Primary Education Network.   Technical
assistance and training would be financed under the project to enable
education managers to undertake activities and carry out studies related to
the rationalization and development of primary education. A total of 23 staff
months of experts would assist with school mapping and education planning,
while a substantial training program would expose primary education managers
to conditions in other countries. This training activity is considered
essential for laying the basis for sound development of the subsector. Study
tours to industrialized countries (for selected national and provincial
managers) would demonstrate recent developments in education management theory
and practice, while study tours to neighboring countries would allow
administrators from the MOET, Provincial and District Education Services, and
a few primary schools, to observe and learn from experiences in nearby
countries. Annex 12 provides details concerning: (a) duration and purpose of
the different types of study tours and training courses; (b) qualifications of
candidates; (c) language requirements for candidates; (d) post-tour
obligations of candidates. The outlines of the training program were
discussed with MOET, and assurances have been obtained that study tours and
fellowships for education managers would be carried out in accordance with a
program acceptable to IDA.
3.31      DeveloRment of an Education Management Information System (EMIS).
The project would support the acquisition of both computers and software
programs appropriate to the development of an Education Management Information
System. The expertise necessary to adapt the software to Vietnamese
conditions and train local authorities in its use would be provided under the
project. An initial list of the software to be acquired has been reviewed and
approved by IDA. Microcomputers, other office equipment, and a limited number
of vehicles would be provided under the project to the main departments of the



- 23 -
MOET, the 53 Provincial Education Services, and selected District Education
Services.M An initial list of hardware to be acquired (Annex 13) has been
reviewed and approved by IDA.
3.32      Improved ImDlementation. Monitoring and Assessment Capacity.
Several activities are included. First, a Project Coordination Unit would be
established. A limiteo number of vehicles, some office equipment (including
computer hardware and software), incremental operating costs (including funds
for internal travel to supervise project implementation), and one expatriate
specialist in project administration (para. 5.3) would be provided under the
project. The structure, function and responsibilities of the Unit and the
provincial, district, and school network that would work with it has been
elaborated (paras. 5.1-5.7 and Annex 18). Second, to enable education
managers to monitor and evaluate the progress of government primary education
programs (including those supported under the project), about US$0.5 million
equivalent would be made available for technical assistance, printing, and
testing associated with a national sample-based education assessment. This
exercise would yield reliable data for monitoring and evaluating the
educational impact of the project and introduce a valuable, long-term
monitoring device for improving the education system. Annex 14 elaborates the
basic requirements for such an assessment and its component tasks (assessment
development, sample selection, assessment administration, scoring, preparation
of the data base, data analysis, and reporting of findings), as well as the
scheduling and budgeting for such an exercise.
3.33      Finally, an amount of US$1.23 million equivalent would be provided
to prepare pre-investment studies for potential investments in primary
education. Several tentative proposals have been put forward, including
assessments of recruitment, training, and remuneration policies and practices
that affect primary teachers and a study of curriculum development. During
Deg2tiions, assurances were obtained that all studies would be carried out
in accordance with terms of reference and timing acceptable to the
Association.
Summary of Project-Supported Technical Assistance
3.34      The project would provide a total of 1,094 staff months of
technical assistance, of which about 177 staff months would be internationally
recruited. The largest amounts of locally-recruited technical assistance
would support the ethnic minority inputs (560 staff months), the national
sample based assessment activity (150 staff months), and school mapping and
civil works (198 staff months), all of which require extensive data collection
and/or analysis over the course of implementation. Terms of Reference for
specialists financed under the project are presented in Annex 15. A breakdown
of technical assistance by input/sub-component, staff months, and unit costs
is presented in Annex 16, Detailed Costs.

IV. PROJECT COSTS AND FINANCING PLAN

net of taxes, with a foreign exchange component of about US$31.4 million
equivalent (or 40% of total project costs). Taxes (limited to those levied
internally on local purchases) were calculated at about US$2.19 million, for a
total project cost of US$80.19 million equivalent. These taxes have been
reflected in project costs but would not be financed from the proceeds of the
IDA Credit. Project costs are summarized by component in Table 4.1 and by
expenditure category in Table 4.2 below and are presented in a more detailed
format in Annex 17.
