4.2         Unit Costs (Annex 16).  Unit costs for civil works are based on
construction of primary schools in Viet Nam during 1992. The average unit
cost for repairing an existing classroom has been estimated at US$1,588,
US$1,091 and ITS$1,053 equivalent in the five cities, the Mekong Delta, and the
mountainous provinces, respectively. The unit cost for building a new
classroom has been estimated at: US$3,175 in the five cities for 40-student
classrooms; US$2,182 in the Mekong Delta for 32-student classrooms; and
US$2,105 in the mountain areas for 30-student classrooms. Average unit costs
for providing latrines and water cisterns have been estimated at US$100 and
US$1,000 equivalent, respectively. An amount equivalent to US$500 per
classroom has been provided for furniture for each classroom repaired or
replaced under the project.
4.3       Estimated costs of teaching aids, equipment, and vehicles are based
on contracts for similar purchases made recently by the MOET. The standard
package of teaching aids to be provided to every primary school has been
estimated at US$300 equivalent; the budget for inputs to be provided to every
primary teacher training school has been established at US$3,000. Costs for
internationally-recruited technical specialists include the cost of salaries,
allowances, and airfares. Many are expected to be recruited from nearby
countries. Fellowships abroad for short-term study trips are estimated at



- 26 -
US$6,000 each for 1-2 month visit to developed countries, and at US$540 per
week for visits to neighboring countries; in-country seminars and workshops
are estimated at US$42 per person-week. Textbook production costs have been
based on current Vietnamese prices in the first year, and increased by about
17% each successive year to cover the anticipated price increases resulting
from the free market reforms. Textbook overhead and distribution costs have
been estimated at 42.8% of production costs (see Annex 3 for further details).
4.4       The foreign exchange comRonent has been estimated as follows:
civil works, 30%; furniture for primary schools, 10%; equipment, 64%; textbook
printing and shipping, 40%; overseas fellowships and study tours, 95%; in-
country training, workshops, and seminars, 20%; international specialists'
services, 90%; local specialists' services, 10%; and studies, 70%.
4.5       Contingency allowances have been estimated as follows:  physical
contingencies have been estimated at 10% of the base cost for all categories;
price contingencies have been calculated on the basis of an average annual
international price increase of 2.8% over the project implementation period.
Local rates of inflation are difficult to estimate, but since the rate of
exchange is adjusted frequently, international rates of inflation have been
applied to both foreign and local costs denominated in US dollars. Aggregate
physical contingencies represent 10% of total baseline costs; aggregate price
contingencies (in US$) represent about 9% of total baseline costs and physical
contingencies.
4.6       Recurrent Cost Implications.  As the projact Is oriented toward
rehabilitating the present system--rather than significantly expanding it--the
recurrent cost burden of project inputs should not be a major problem for
either the Government or the individual communities. Generally speaking, the
improvements to the quality of primary education are expected to lead to
increased internal efficiency and decreased pupil unit costs; the improvements
to the administration of primary education are expected to have a similar
impact.
4.7       Recurrent costs associated with the construction component should
be minimal. Most civil works would be the repair or replacement of existing
classrooms, which should be cheaper to maintain than the existing highly
dilapidated classrooms. Particularly in the Mekong Delta, where temporary
structures are the rule, it would be less expensive to maintain a permanent
structure than to rebuild a temporary facility every two to three years. The
recurrent cost implications on the human resource side are similarly small.
Where classrooms are rebuilt or replaced, or where new classrooms are built to
relieve triple-shifting, no new teachers would be hired, since the stock of
teachers is sufficient. New teachers would be hired only where entirely new
classrooms are to be built, and most of these would be teachers starting at
the bottom end of the salary scale. Furthermore, among the responsibilities-
of the individual School Committees (para. 5.7) would be: (a) estimating
probable recurrent costs resulting from project-supported activities
(including salaries of any new teachers required), (b) proposing methods for
financing additional recurrent costs generated, and (c) securing commitment
from the local People's Committee and the Parents' Association to provide the
additional funds required.



- 27 -
4.8       Progressive reduction of subsidies plus the quality improvements
envisaged would initially result in increased textbook production and
recurrent costs. However, the increase would be more a matter of unit costs
than net recurrent cost outlay. The inflow efficiency would be better as more
efficient cost recovery (including distribution and overhead costs) is
contemplated for the textbooks sold or rented. The outflow volume and values
would be lower for several reasons. EPH would have the benefit of the
substantial seed capital resulting from retention of revenues earned through
sale and r ntal of IDA-financed textbook production. Production quantities
would be lower than under the old system (due to reduction in the nuiber of
curricula and titles ivad the improved physical quality and lifespan of the
books themselves). The change in the distribution policy whereby, the
textbooks rented out or lent would return to circulation for a period of about
3 years, would warrant a smaller annual print run.   Furthermore, if textbook
quality is good, a secondary market of used textbooks originally sold at full
price could easily result, especially in the urban centers, which would reduce
necessary print runs even further. Finally, over the long term, as free
market systems take root in the economy generally, the production and
distribution costs may well come down.
B. Financing Plan
4.9       The Government's contribution of 10% of the project costs (net of
taxes) would include financing from the national, provincial, district, and
commune levels, as well as from primary school Parents' Associations. It is
important to recognize the sense of financial responsibility already ingrained
in the local communities and to build upon it under the project. The
Government supports the notion that a local contribution would be required at
every construction site. Therefore, it is expected that approximately 10% of
the financing plan for civil works at each school would be a locally-based
contribution (from, inter alia, the Parents' Association or local People's
Committees). 1
4.10      The project financing plan is given in Table 4.3.  The IDA Credit
of SDR 49.6 million (US$70.0 million equivalent) represents about 90% of the
total project costs net of taxes.
IF        It was suggested originally that this contribution be 10% of the
cost of the works at each site. The current scheme requires a local
contribution at each site, but allows the amount of this contribution to vary
from school to school, depending on how much each community is able to
contribute. Education authorities at the district and provincial levels
(drawing on their annual allocations from their respective People's
Committees) would "top up" the local contribution, as necessary to reach 10%
of the total cost at any one site.

V. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
A. Proiect Management
5.1       The structure, functions, and staffing of project management have
been discussed with the Borrower and are detailed in Annex 18 and Chart 2:
Project Coordination Unit.' These arrangements were designed to reflect:
(a) the Government's unfamiliarity with IDA-financed projects; (b) the
decentralized nature of education management in Viet Nam; (c) the importance
of the school or classroom as the unit of account; (d) the importance of
parental/community participation in the financing and implementation of
various project activities; and (e) the importance of establishing ex auto
lines of authority/responsibility for general administration (procurement,
financing, reporting), construction, training, textbook production, ethnic
minority education, and project monitoring and evaluation.
5.2       The project management structure reflects and to a great extent
replicates extant primary education management structures. Functions,
responsible parties, and reporting mechanisms have been developed at each
level (national, provincial, district, and school). At the national level, a
National Project Director, assisted by a National Project Manage. assigned to
the project full-time, would coordinate the implementation of all project-
related activities. The National Project Director would receive advice and
assistance from a newly-formed Consultative Council, comprising
representatives of agencies outside the MOET such as the State Planning
Committee (SPC), the Ministry of Finance (MOF), and the State Bank of Viet
Nam. He/she would be assisted by a National Project Management Committee
composed of directors of all NOET departments participating in the project.
I'        Implementation arrangements for the Ethnic Minorities Component are
discussed separately in Annex 6 and Chart 3.



- 29 -
5.3       The full-time National Project Manager would be responsible for
day-to-day oversight of all aspects of project implementation. He/she would
be supported by seven Assistant Project Managers and three Deputy Assistant
Project Managers responsible for implementing various aspects of the project.
An internationally-recruited Assistant Project Manager for Administration and
Finance would oversee (inter ali) project accounting and auditing, reporting
requirements, procurement, and withdrawal applications. The National Project
Manager and the Assistant Project Managers would together constitute a Project
Coordination Unit (PCU). Although the PCU would initially coordinate the
implementation of only the IDA-assisted project, the MOET's Intention is that
this unit would eventually be responsible for overseeing implementation of all
foreign-assisted education projects.
5.4       Project activities at the provincial level would be coordinated by
a Provincial Project Coordinator attached to the Education Service who would
be relieved of other responsibilities for the duration of the project and be
assisted by a Provincial Management Committee. Duties would include: (a)
undertaking ex post review of School Committee plans; (b) ensuring delivery of
nationally-procured equipment to districts and Primary Teacher Training
School(s): (c) supervising (through the Provincial Education Service's
Construction Unit) construction activities in the province; (d) ensuring
timely provision of funds from provincial budgets for the project; (e)
ensuring the flow of funds from the national to the district level; and (f)
following agreed criteria, selecting staff from the Provincial Education
Service to participate in project-sponsored training.
5.5       Project activities at the district level would be coordinated by a
District Project Coordinator attached to the Education Service who would be
relieved of other responsibilities and be assisted oy a District Management
Committee. Duties would include: (a) supervising civil works in the district
(if any); (b) reviewing/approving School Committee plans; (c) ensuring
delivery of equipment to the district's primary schools; (d) ensuring timely
provision of funds from district budgets for the project; (e) ensuring flow of
funds from the provincial to the school level; and (f) following agreed
criteria, selecting staff from the district's Education Service and primary
schools to participate in project-sponsored training. The District Project
Coordinator would be assisted by a District Management Committee composed of
school principals involved in project implementation and other appropriate
officials of the district, including a representative of the District
Financial Service.
5.6       At the school level, each school would have a site manager, which
would be either the principal or head teacher. This individual would
represent the school during project implementation in order to: (a) minimize
the impact of construction on the learning environment; (b) receive, inspect,
and accept shipments of books, materials, and furniture on behalf of the
school; (c) schedule staff training (especially in multi-grade or bi-lingual
teaching techniques); (d) interact with provincial, district, and local
authorities to maximize impact of inputs on education activities; and (e)
collect school-level data for use by Assistant Project Manager for Monitoring
and Evaluation.



30
5.7       Principals would be assisted (as is presently the case) by a School
Committee chaired by the principal and including representatives of the
Parents' Association and the local People's Committee (commune People's
Committee, in rural areas; the neighborhood People's Committee, in urban
areas). Where sufficiently large population clusters exist without a school,
local initiative would form an appropriately-constituted New School Committee.
Each School Committee (whether existing or New) would prepare a plan for the
repair, rehabilitation, or construction to be undertaken under the project
which would include designs, estimated costs, a proposed implementation
schedule, and assurances regarding recurrent cost financing. Plans for
schools in ethnic minority areas (and mountainous areas with sufficient
population and no school) would be prepared by the existing or New School
Committees with the help of district education authorities and would include
the following additional elements: (a) number of primary-age children in the
catchment area by gender and ethnic affiliation; (b) proposals for multi-grade
teaching and resulting number of new classrooms needed (if any); and (c)
proposals for mother-tongue literacy and for teaching Vietnamese as the
national language.
5.8       During project preparation, IDA and UNICEF worked closely with
RDICS staff to develop cost-effective designs for schools and latrines, and
these designs would become the basis for the new construction. Technical
assistance would be obtained to further refine designs in light of local
conditions. In the case of Phase Three civil works (in mountainous areas),
these plans must also reflect the phasing of additional pedagogic inputs which
would precede construction (para 3.27). All such plans would be submitted to
the respective District Management Committee for review and approval; in
particular, the District Management Committee would ensure that the proposed
designs conformed to national norms. Following approval, copies of the plans
would be submitted to the respective Provincial Management Committee and the
Project Coordination Unit (either the head office in Hanoi or the branch
office in Ho Chi Minh City) for ex Dost review and eventual review by IDA
supervision missions.
5.9       During Negotiations, the Borrower gave assurances that it would:
(a) maintain the Project Coordination Unit in the MOET with functions, powers,
responsibilities, and funding satisfactory to the Association, staffed by
competent staff in adequate numbers; and (b) ensure that at all times during
project implementation, each project province shall maintain a Provincial
Project Coordinator and a Provincial Project Management Committee, and each
project district shall maintain a District Project Coordinator and a District
Project Management Committee, all with functions, powers, and responsibilities
acceptable to the Association.
B. Flow of Funds. Accounting. and Auditing
5.10      Flow of Funds.  In October of each year, the MOET would request the
MOF to include in the expenditures budget for the forthcoming year the full
amount of funds necessary to cover national contributions to project
implementation expenditures. The MOF would also include in its revenue budget
the amount expected to be drawn down from the IDA Credit during that year.
After January 1, the MOET would be authorized to start making expenditures



- 31 -
according to the approved budget allocations, drawing on its account ln'x the
State Bank of Vietnam in Hanoi. Funds needed for expenditures at the lower
levels which are to be financed by the national government would be
periodically transferred to the accounts of the respective Provincial
Education Services in the banks in those provinces, using the normal budgetary
transfer procedures. Funds needed for expenditures at the school level would
be periodically transferred, first, to the accounts of the respective District
Education Services in local banks in those district, and then to the accounts
of the respective primary schools in the same banks, also using the normal
budgetary transfer procedures.
5.11      In order to facilitate the flow of funds to each construction site,
each School Committee would open a project account at a local bank in the
district capital.  The Parents' Association and the local People's Cormittee
would be required to make a deposit into this account (normally equivalent to
10% of the total estimated financing plan). Some contribution would be
required, no matter how small. The-Provincial Peoples Committee would deposit
an additional amount in each account to bring the total deposit in each case
to 20% of the estimated financing plan. A letter to the Assistant Manager or
Deputy Assistant Manager for Construction certifying that the 20% has been
deposited would be required to obtain authorization to sign a contract with a
local rontractor and initiate construction. Thus, construction would begin
w.ch 20% of the total estimated budget available for use.
5.12       When funds equivalent to 10% of the estimated costs had been drawn
down, school committees would present supporting documentation to obtain
reimbursement of amounts eligible for financing. The Project Coordination
Unit would reimburse the percentage of the amount eligible for financing from
a Special Account, then request reimbursement from IDA, following normal
reimbursement procedures. This process would continue until the full amount
of the IDA contribution had been disbursed to each school in question. Except
in the poorest areas, where parents could not afford the full counterpart
contribution, the 10% initially advanced by the Provincial People's Committee
would be returned to them by the time the civil works are completed.
5.13     Accounting and Auditing.  Appropriate accounts for the project
would be kept. Standard formats have been developed and are presented in
Annex 19. All project related expenditures should be fully documented and
aggregated quarterly.1' A system will be developed whereby copies of
supporting documentation will be consolidated at the project office in Hanoi.
This will facilitate auditing. At the national level, accounts would be
audited annually by independent auditors acceptable to IDA. During
Negotiation, assurances were received that: (a) project accounts would be
audited on a yearly basis by auditors acceptable to IDA; and (b) certified
audit reports would be furnished to IDA no later than nine months after the
close of the fiscal year.
it/       It is recommended that copies of documentation be retained with
District Project Coordinators.

C. Proeure*nt
5.14      The procurement of goods and works will be carried out in
accordance with the "Guidelines for Procurement under IBRD Loans and IDA
Credits," May 1992 (the Guidelines). In the procurement of goods through ICB,
goods manufactured in the Socialist Republic of Viet Nam may be granted a
margin of preference of 158 (or the applicable import duties, whichever is
less) for purposes of bid comparison. In the procurement of goods and
services through ICB, the standard bidding documents issued by IDA will be
used. In such cases where these do not exist, bidding documents based on
other internationally recognized standard forms agreed with IDA shall be used.
Local procurement procedures have not been adequately standardized or
codified, and IDA proposes to assist the Government in doing so. For the
purposes of this project, however, local competitive bidding (LCB) procedures
acceptable to IDA were discussed during the technical discussions (see Annex
20) and agreed during Negotiations.
5.15      Civil Works (total estimated cost:  US$28.1 million equivalent,
including taxes and contingencies). Civil works envisaged under the project
would be small, simple structures dispersed throughout Viet Nam, typically
costing under US$200,000, and would not be attractive to foreign bidders. In
urban areas, civil works would be procured under LCB procedures acceptable to
IDA (Annex 20), except for contracts estimated to cost US$50,000 up to an
aggregate of US$0.7 million equivalent, which may be procured under prudent
shopping procedures acceptable to IDA.J' In rural areas, civil works would
be procured on the basis of prudent shopping practices acceptable to IDA.
.'his procedure would provide sufficient competition and is appropriate because
of the scattered nature of the sites and the small amounts involved at any one
site. Where prudent shopping procedures are being employed, School Committees
would undertake procurement of civil works. A copy of the bid evaluation and
the contract would be sent to the Assistant Manager for Construction in Hanoi
or to the Deputy Assistant Manager for Construction in Ho Chi Minh City for
review and would be retained for random sampling review by IDA supervision
missions. In procurement involving LCB, the Assistant and Deputy Assistant
Managers for Construction would be responsible for establishing bid evaluation
and award committees for the North and South, respectively, (comprising inter
Al", other members of the PCU, Provincial Project Coordinators, and local
RDICS staff) and thereby administering the procurement of civil works
contracts (Annex 18). Appropriate aggregate figures for LCB and prudent
shopping would be US$3.0 million and US$25.1 million equivalent, respectively.
5.16      School Furniture (total estimated cost:  US$6.1 million equivalent,
including taxes and contingencies). School furniture would be simple and is
manufactured locally at points of use. The type of furniture and the overall
e         All references herein to "prudent shopping procedures acceptable to
IDA" should be taken to mean: (a) the solicitation of price quotations from
at least three qualified and eligible local contractors or suppliers,
excluding any who are meubers of the selecting committee referenced, and (b)
the awarding the contract to the lowest-priced qualified contractor or
supplier.



- 33 -.
scale of procurement make it unlikely that foreign bidders would be interested
in bidding. In urban areas, school furniture would be procured in bulk by the
District Management Committee, on the basis of LCB procedures acceptable to
IDA (Annex 20), except for contracts estimated to cost US$25,000 or less, up
to an aggregate of US$0.1 million equivalent, which may be procured under
prudent shopping procedures acceptable to IDA. In rural areas, school
furniture would be procured through prudent shopping. Where prudent shopping
procedures are being employed, furniture would be purchased by each School
Committee. In procurement involving LCB, the District Management Committee
would be responsible for establishing bid evaluation and award committees and
overseeing procurement (Annex 18). Appropriate aggregate figures for LCB and
shopping would be US$0.5 and US$5.5 million equivalent, respectively.
5.17      Teaching Aids. Equipment. ad Vehicles (total estimated cost:
US$9.5 million equivalent, including taxes and contingencies). Primary school
teaching aids valued at US$5.6 million equivalent and miscellaneous items
valued at US$2.5 million equivalent (including office equipment, computers,
and vehicles) would be procured in bulk by the Project Coordination Unit (the
Assistant Project Manager for Administration and Finance) on the basis of ICB
in accordance with the Guidelines (Annex 18). Equipment and teaching aids for
primary teacher training schools estimated to cost US$100,000 or less, up to
an r,gregate of US$1.4 million equivalent, would be purchased by each primary
tea.-her training school through prudent shopping procedures acceptable to IDA.
5.18      Textbook Printing and Shipging (total estimated cost:  US$29.1
million equivalent, including taxes and contingencies). EPH would have the
responsibility for awarding contracts for new textbook development as well as
textbook printing and shipping.N Printing contracts (valued at US$26.2
million equivalent) would be awarded by EPH on the basis of ICB procedures in
accordance with the Guidelines. Shipping contracts (valued at US$1.4 million
equivalent) and printing tryouts for textbooks (up to US$50,000 per contract
and not exceeding US$1.5 million in aggregate) would be awarded by EPH on the
basis of prudent shopping procedures acceptable to IDA.
5.19      Overseas and In-Countrv Training (total estimated cost:  US$3.0
million equivalent, including taxes and contingencies). Overseas training
(US$1.5 million equivalent) would consist of study tours and placement of
candidates in short-term courses. Overseas study tours would be planned by
the Project Coordination Unit (the Assistant Project Manager for Human
Resources and Training) on the basis of the relevance and usefulness of the
proposed visit to the work of the candidates proposed, costs, and willingness
of host country institutions to receive groups of education authorities from
Viet Nam. The placing of candidates in specific institutions would be made on
the basis of the relevance and quality of the programs offered, costs, and
prior experience. In-country training (US$1.5 million equivalent) would be
organized by the MOET.
v         Textbook development, overheads, and distribution (typically via
district or provincial warehouses owned by EPH) is estimated to cost US$12.40
million equivalent for the project period.



- 34 -
5.20      Consultant Services (US$4.5 million equivalent, including taxes and
contingencies). This would include the services of local and international
specialists (valued at US$3.1 million equivalent) as well as the preparation
of studies (valued at US$1.4 million equivalent). Consultants would be
selected and employed by the Project Coordination Unit in accordance with the
"Guidelines for the Use of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers."
5.21      Prior review by IDA would be required for:  (a) all contracts for
teaching aids/equipment/vehicles valued above US$200,000 equivalent (covering
about 85% of the value); (b) all textbook printing contracts valued above
US$200,000 equivalent (covering 100% of the value); (c) all civil works,
shipping, and furniture contracts valued above US$200,000 equivalent; and (d)
all technical assistance and studies contracts. In addition, notwithstanding
the contract amount threshold, bidding documents for the first procurement of
civil works or goods would be subject to prior review by IDA. IDA has
reviewed and approved the list of teaching aids and equipment for primary
schools and the list of equipment and vehicles for the MOET, the Provincial
Education Services, selected District Education Services, and the Project
Coordination Unit. The individual equipment lists for the primary teacher
training schools would be reviewed and approved by the respective Provincial
Education Service and by the MOET's Department of Teacher Training; the lists
would be retained for ex Rost review by IDA supervision missions. Since the
civil works envisaged consist mainly of rehabilitation and repair of existing
classrooms and/or replacement of a few existing dilapidated classrooms, it is
believed there would not be any contracts valued above US$200,000 equivalent
for civil works or furniture, but these would be subject to post-award review
of a random sample covering no less than 10% of all contracts awarded through
LCB and shopping.

D. Disbursements and ImpleRmntation
5.23            Disbursement Percencages.  The proposed IDA Credit of SDR 49.6
million (US$70.0 million equivalent) would finance about 90% of the total
estimated project cost net of taxes. Disbursement percentages for various
categories of expenditure are presented in Table 5.2.
5.24            A Special Account in U.S. dollars would be established and
maintained to facilitate implementation. An initial deposit of US$5.0 million
would be deposited in a bank in Hanoi acceptable to IDA. This bank should
have a branch in Ho Chi Mlnh City to facilitate the transfer of funds.
Applications for replenishment would be submitted on a monthly basis, or when
the amounts withdrawn were equal to 30% of the initial deposit, whichever
occurred first. The minimum application size for payments directly from the
credit account or for issuance of special commitments would be US$0.5 million
equivalent.
5.25            Reguired Documentation.  Withdrawal applications for civil
works, furniture, equipment, teaching aids, vehicles, textbook printing, and
textbook shipping with a contract value of US$200,000 or more would be
supported by full documentation, as would all contracts for consultants'
services, studies, and overseas training. Disbursements under contracts for
civil works, school furniture, equipment, teaching aids, vehicles, textbook
printing, and textbook shipping with a value below US$200,000 as well as
disbursements against any expenditures for in-country training would be made
on the basis of SOEs, for which supporting documents would be retained in the
Project Coordination Unit in Hanoi and made available to IDA for review during
the course of normal project supervision. The Assistant Project Manager for
Administration and Finance would be responsible for aggregating the eligible
expenditures under each heading and for preparing withdrawal applications for
submission to IDA.
5.26        Disbursement Profile.  (Annex 21) There is no disbursement profile
for Viet Nam. Although the possibility of scheduling project implementation
over five years was discussed, a more conservative approach of a seven-year
implementation-cum-disbursement period was agreed upon, since Viet Nam is for
all practical purposes a new Borrower. The fact that some counterpart funds
would be provided at the local level (by Parents' Associations and local
governments) reinforces this conservative approach. Finally, the proposed
profile closely follows that established for other Bank-financed education
projects in the East Asia Region.

5.27      Implementation Schedule.  (Annex 22) Project implementation is
estimated at seven years, with a completion date of December 31, 2001, and
Credit Closing Date six months later (i.e. June 30, 2002). Construction and
furnishing of primary classrooms is scheduled to be implemented in three
phases, beginning with the major city schools during the third quarter of
FY94. Most technical assistance inputs (i.e., for ethnic minority education,
textbook production, and various education management activities) will be
provided during the first fire years of implementation. Major changes
affecting EPH and its practices also will be introduced during the first five
years.
E. Monitoring. Reporting. and Supervision Requirements
5.28      Monitoring and Assessment.  The project would reinforce periodic
monitoring of project targets as well as support a sample-based educational
assessment exercise. A matrix for monitoring and evaluating project



- 38 -
implementation and impact was developed during preparation and discussed
extensively with the Government. This matrix presents project activities
according to sub-objective, agency responsible for implementation and key
monitoring indicators (Annex 23). While key indicators would monitor progress
toward achieving quantitative and qualitative targets of the project, sample-
based assessments would provide useful information about educational outcomes,
especially in ethnic-minority areas (see Annex 14 and para. 3.32).
5.29      SuPervision. (Annex 24)  The project would be supervised by IDA
according to an agreed plan. IDA would supervise the project tri-annually
during the first two years of implementation and bi-annually thereafter
(barring unforeseen implementation problems). Particular attention would be
paid to supervision of textbook activities. From the second year of the
project onwards, EPH would be using competitive bidding procedures to procure
inputs, and printing and shipping services. By the end of the project, the
system would have changed radically, with EPH not only out of debt but having
the working capital to sustain the production system after project completion.
IDA will need to closely monitor this difficult but necessary transition, and
will do so through: (a) providing technical oversight (textbook production
specialists and financial analysts) during the course of supervision and (b)
mandating an annual review of the EPH's Annual Business Plan as part of the
Annual Work Program exercise (see paras. 3.3, 5.30).
5.30      Annual. Mid-term. and Final Reviews. (Annex 25)  Implementation
progress would be monitored on the basis of work plans formulated annually and
reports submitted quarterly by responsible entities. During Negotiations,
assurances were received that the Government would: (a) by October 1 of every
year, commencing on October 1, 1994, prepare and furnish to IDA an Annual Work
Program for the following calendar year; (b) by December 1 every year,
commencing on December 1, 1994 review that program with IDA; and (c)
thereafter, taking into account the Association's comments, implement the
program in a manner satisfactory to the Association. For the review taking
place in 1997, the Borrower gave assurances that it would undertake with the
Association a Mid-Term Review of project progress and prospects, commencing on
October 1, 1997, and furnish to the Association a comprehensive report of the
activities undertaken under the project by December 1, 1997. The Annual
Review would discuss progress of project implementation over the preceding
twelve months, propose the work plan for the next twelve months, and monitor
progress in resolving teacher remuneration issues.   UNICEF and World Vision
representatives would be invited to discuss the Annual Work Program each year
prior to its finalization.
5.31      Under the general direction of the National Project Manager, the
Assistant Project Manager for Monitoring and Evaluation would coordinate the
preparation of the Annual Work Program and organize the annual reviews.
He/she would be responsible for &11 evaluation-related activities under the
project, including: (a) monitoring key project indicators; (b) collecting
information on project achievements; (c) consolidating information received
from the Provincial Management Committees and from other Assistant Project
Managers; (d) summarizing information quarterly for presentation to the



- 39 -
National Project Management Committee, IDA, UNICEF, and World Vision; and (e)
organizing the sample-based assessment exercise (paras. 3.32 and 5.28)
5.32      For the project monitoring and evaluation system to work well,
cooperation would be needed at all levels, down to the school level. The
Assistant Project Manager for Monitoring and Evaluation should prepare a plan
which would include the proposed arrangements for: (a) standardized data
collection according to key monitoring indicators; (b) computerizing project
statistics; and (c) undertaking statistical analyses. This plan should be
incorporated into the Annual Work Program and reviewed with IDA.
F. Status of Proiect Preparation
5.33      During project preparation, MOET produced standard classroom
designs which could be modified to correspond to local conditions and
available resources. Similarly, local education authorities supplied IDA with
very detailed information on construction needs in the five largest cities,
the Mekong Delta provinces, and the eight mountainous provinces to be included
in the project. Such proposals were reviewed by the appraisal mission and
subsequent missions.
5.34      Since, to the extent possible, civil works in the mountainous
provinces would follow the pedagogical aspects, it is important that these
activities also begin early. UNICEF is presently engaged in training teachers
for multi-grade teaching and would continue to do so under its existing five-
year work program. The IDA-financed project would expand this program to
additional schools in the eight provinces in which UNICEF is already active.
One specialist in ethnic minority languages financed by World Vision began
working with the Research Center for Ethnic Minority Education in early 1993.
5.35      Other components are in a state of readiness.  The standard list of
basic teaching aids and equipment for primary schools has been prepared by the
MOET's General Company for Educational Equipment and was reviewed and approved
by IDA. The preparation of individualized lists of inputs by each primary
teacher training school is underway. The list of equipment to improve the
administration of the primary education system has also been prepared,
reviewed, and approved by IDA. A fellowship and training program for
education administrators has been prepared and reviewed. Where major changes
to present practices are proposed (namely, to the EPH textbook production
system), appropriate assurances have been sought and conditionalities
established. The present system of producing textbooks would not begin to
undergo major changes until the second year of the project. Finally, most
Project Managers have been identified, including the probable National Project
Manager.
5.36      In addition the Government has given assurances with regard to the
following:
(a) the contents, scope, timing, and responsibilities for
preparing Annual Work Programs and EPH Business Plans (paras.
3.3 and 5.30 and Annexes 3 and 25);



- 40 -
(b) the proposed work program for the first year of the project;
(c) outlines and timetables for a phased school mapping program
(paras. 3.21-3.22 and Annex 8);
(d) the timetable for establishing and staffing the Project
Coordination Unit (5.1-5.9 and Annex 18);
(e) mechanisms for forming New School Committees, undertaking
school planning at proposed construction sites, and mobilizing
local financing in a timely manner (paras. 5.11-5.12);
(f) codification of LCB procedures for IDA-financed procurement
(paras. 5.14-5.18 and Annex 20).
VI.  SUSTAINABILITY. ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACT, AND GENDER ISSUES
6.1       Proiect Sustainability.  Project benefits are regarded as
sustainable for several reasons. First, the society and the Government place
a high priority on education, both intrinsically and as a "value added"
dimension to the productive factor of labTior (paras. 2.8, 2.41-2.43). Within
the education sector, the highest prior-ty is accorded to primary education
and to literacy. Both public and priv be (household) budgets reflect the
importance attached to education, as evidenced by the high enrollment levels,
the increasing budget allocations, and the community support in filling the
Government's funding gaps at the primary level (paras. 2.12, 2.43). Second,
except for the textbook and ethnic minorities components, the project is not
introducing major alterations to the policies or practices governing primary
education. In cases where changes are being proposed, affected beneficiaries,
agencies, and the Government have been involved and a high degree of consensus
has been achieved.
6.2       Third, all of the quality improvements included in the project are
intended to reduce repetition and dropout in Viet Nam's primary education
system, increasing internal efficiency and lowering the cost of producing a
primary-school graduate. The training of primary education administrators
supported by the project has no immediate salary implications. It is expected
to enable administrators to rationalize primary education and manage the
system better and thus enhance efficiency (para. 3.30). The textbook
component has been designed to make it both more equitable and efficient,
enabling EPH to sustain its operations while allowing a greater number of
children access to textbooks (paras. 3.3-3.11, 4.8).
6.3       Finally, the recurrent costs generated by project-financed inputs
(particularly the civil works component) should not be much greater than they
are today; in fact, in the case of some schools, they may be lower, since the
buildings would be of a more permanent nature and would require less
maintenance. Among other things, School Committees would be responsible for
estimating probable recurrent costs resulting from project-supported
activities (including salaries of any new teachers required), proposing



- 41 -
methods for financing additional recurrent costs generated, and securing
commitment from the local People's Committee and the Parents' Association to
provide the additional funds required (para. 4.7). The package of teaching
aids received by any one school is estimated to cost the equivalent of US$300;
as items are lost or broken or wear out over a period of years, it is
considered well within the capability of local Parents' Associations to
replace them.
6.4       Environmental lmpact. The impact of the proposed project on the
environment would be limited. Construction is small scale and limited mainly
to the repair or rehabilitation of existing classrooms. The fact that all
schools under the project would receive suitable latrines and training in
their maintenance is expected to have a positive impact in both the
environmental and public health areas. New textbooks to be provided under the
project would include environmental concepts, as appropriate, and the fact
that the textbooks themselves would be recycled would encourage awareness of
such issues. The EPH's Editorial Board would review new textbooks to ensure
that ecological and environmental issues are covered.
6.5       Gender Issues.  As noted earlier, Vietnamese society values
education for girls as highly as for boys, and the statistics available
indicate that the ratio of girls to boys in primary education (91 to 100) and
secondary education (90 to 100) was the highest among a group of comparator
countries of similarly low income per capita. To the extent that a gender gap
exists in certain areas, exacerbated by poverty or isolation of the population
cohort, the project would likely impact girls to a greater extent than boys,
given that: (a) it will provide sanitary facilities at school sites
(frequently noted as a facility beneficial to keeping girls in school); and
(b) it will ensure that a full set of textbooks reaches all primary aged
children. Furthermore, new textbooks to be provided under the project would
be expected to be free of gender bias and would be reviewed by the EPH's
Editorial Board during the course of project implementation to ensure that
they were.
VII. PROJECT BENEFITS AND RISKS
A. Benefits
7.1       The project should provide gains in efficiency and equity for both
the education system and the society at large. All education inputs are aimed
at reducing wastage (repetition, dropout) and improving throughput. If
children can move through primary education in less time than they are
currently taking, unit costs would go down. Better facilities and access to a
full set of textbooks and classroom learning aids is a crucial step toward a
better--and therefore more efficient--educational environment.  To the extent
that the school environment is improved, children should learn more.
7.2       Viet Nam has made great progress toward universal primary education
but has reached the point where further progress would become increasingly
difficult, as the Government attempts to attract and retain in school the
harder-to-reach and harder-to-teach groups of children (e.g., ethnic



- 42 -
minorities; children living in isolated or flood-prone areas; very poor
children in urban areas). This difficulty would be exacerbated by a
constantly growing school-age population and, at least in the madium term, by
Viet Nam's transition to a tarket economy. The project would make available
the resources and strategies for reaching and keeping in school these hard-to-
reach groups during this difficult period and would address systemic
distortions in the provision of primary education, thus reaching the poorer
segments of the population. Specific project activities with a poverty-
reduction focus include: (a) a more equitable textbook system (based on a
standard curriculum, provision of ethnic language minority materials as
appropriate, and a more equitable approach to distribution); (b) support for
more culturally-sensitive pedagogies and mother-tongue literacy, which should
help to level the educational playing field for ethnic minority children; and
(c) targeting of schools in the most dilapidated condition (and therefore,
likely to be in the poorest areas) for rehabilitation. The direct result of
these inputs would be higher attendance in primary school, better educational
performance, and a higher attainment rate among the most vulnerable groups.
7.3       Other specific project benefits would include:  (a) a streamlined
curriculum (as reflected in the new textbooks); (b) a more cost-effective and
financially sustainable system for producing and printing textbooks, deriving
in part from the introduction of competitive bidding procedures; (c) an
improved system for judging classroom needs, prioritizing resource allocation,
and empowering district education authorities (through introduction of the
school mapping approach); (d) stronger, more durable, and ultimately less
costly classrooms in the Mekong delta (through introduction of improved
construction techniques for building typhoon- and flood-resistant schools);
(e) improvements to health conditions at primary schools (through provision of
latrines and drinking water); and (f) stronger education management (through
improved measures for monitoting education programs, gathering and analyzing
educational statistics, and conducting national education assessments).
B. Risks
7.4       Primary risks stem from:  (a) the transitional economy and
Government agencies' unfamiliarity with multilateral financing practices and
procedures; (b) assumptions about the direction and pace of change to EPH
operations; (c) the impact of declining teacher remuneration levels on the
quality of education delivered; (d) the timeliness and sufficiency of the
counterpart funding, in particular the local (school or district level)
contribution; (e) the capacity of the MOET to implement the project, optimize
education management inputs, and institute new evaluation and assessment
procedures; and (f) the ability of local (school and district) education
authorities to adopt different processes (i.e. formalize local financial
contributions, institutionalize school mapping procedures, and develop school
plans as a basis for construction investments). Each of these risks is
discussed below, with reference to the specific project input or corresponding
conditionality designed to address it.
7.5       Transitional Economy and New Borrower Status.  Viet Nam is in
transition from a command to a market-based economy. Moreover, for all
practical purposes, Viet Nam and the World Bank Group have yet to forge an



- 43 -
operational relationship around financing for a specific project. It is
likely that there will be macro-economic changes and a period of adjustment as
the two entities become familiar with the other's way of working. Although
the Government is firmly committed to the direction of changs as its policies
amply demonstrate, implementation of the project may be adversely affected by
the rest of society's need to adjust to the new signals. For example, the
local banking system is still in transition and has yet to establish
mechanisms to facilitate flows of funds from central to local levels. The
lack of experience with market mechanisms and competitive bidding procedures
could complicate implementation, as could the lack of familiarity with Bank
Group procedures and requirements (regarding disbursements, financial
reporting, auditing, and project monitoring and evaluation).
7.6       The project has taken these risks into consideration as follows:
(a) development of user-friendly local accounting formats in collaboration
with local authorities (para. 5.13 and Annex 19); (b) elaboration of Local
Competitive Bidding practices for IDA-financed project items (paras. 5.14-5.18
and Annex 20); and (c) preparation of Annual Work Programs for the project
acceptable to IDA (para. 5.30-5.32 and Annex 25). In addition, IDA has
proposed intensive supervision during the first two years of the project
(para. 5.29 and Annex 24).
7.7       Changes to EPH Production and Business Practices.  As noted, EPH
will need to introduce drastic revisions in the way it operates, an inherently
risky proposition for any organization. The Government has indicated its
support for these changes. Key policy changes will be: (a) phased reduction
to input subsidies; (b) conversion to a system which fosters textbook
recycling; (c) institution of competitive bidding practices; and (d)
production of a single set of textbooks conforming to the 165 week curriculum
(para. 3.6 and Annex 3). In addition, EPH would be required to develop Annual
Business Plans which would be reviewed with IDA in the context of the Annual
Work Program (para. 3.3). Finally--as adequate recycling mechanisms will be a
crucial factor in the successful implementation of the new scheme--EPH will
host a national workshop and develop acceptable textbook recycling mechanisms
(para 3.7).
7.8       Teacher Remuneration.  There is a risk that the educational impact
of inputs provided under the project would be blunted by a decreasingly
motivated teaching force stemming from declining salaries. This issue is
being handled through: (a) the Annual Work Program which obligates the
Government to review primary teacher remuneration issues and discuss them with
IDA (para. 5.30); and (b) a project-financed study of primary teacher
recruitment and remuneration issues (para. 3.33). In the meantime, the
project would support crucial inputs to primary teaching and teacher training
schools (paras. 3.12-3.16 and Annexes 4 and 5).
7.9       Adeguacv of Counterpart Funding and Local Contribution.  IDA is
financing 90% of total project costs net of taxes. The Government and local
communities would finance the remaining 10%. The bulk of Government
counterpart financing is for the textbook component, primarily attendant upon
operating and distribution costs of EPH, which it already subsidizes. Thus,
no significant additional outlay on the part of Government will be required to



- 44 -
implement the project. The local contribution would be exclusively for
counterpart funding of school construction, but in communities too poor to
meet the requisite 10% share of local financing, District and Provincial
Education Services would have already provided the required amount as
described in paragraphs 5.11-5.12.
7.10      MOET Managorial Capacity.  As noted, MOET departments have been
actively involved in all aspects of project preparation; some heads of these
departments have been provisionally identified as Assistant Project Managers
for various components. The detailed structure, functions, and staffing of
project management down to the school level have been discussed and agreed
with the MOET (para 5.1-5.7 and Annex 18), and IDA received additional
assurance from the Government during negotiations (para. 5.9). A Matrix for
Monitoring and Evaluating Project Implementation was also developed in concert
with the MOET authorities, and responsibilities for monitoring and evaluation
in the project are well defined (Annex 23). The project would provide
significant inputs in study tours, fellowships, and local training
opportunities at all levels of the administration (para. 3.30 and Annex 12).
The EMIS system proposed would require software preparation as a pre-condition
for hardware purchase, and substantial technical assistance has also been
provided to this component (para. 3.31, 3.34 and Annex 13).
7.11      New Presses for-Planninf and Eecuting School Imrovements.  In
fact, the civil works component incorporates and depends upon Viet Nam's
existing tradition of local initiative in building schools for which there is
ample evidence of precedent (para. 2.10-2.12). The project would cement and
formalize this commitment, however, through active participation of School
Committees and the development of school plans (para. 5.7) and the
establishment of school-based construction accounts and deposits (paras. 5.11-
5.12). The institutionalization of school mapping procedures (paras. 3.21-
3.23) would solidify a rational basis for making investment decisions and
establishing priorities among them.
VIII. AGREEMENTS AND RECOMMENDATIMOS
8.1       During Negotiations. the Government gave assurance that:
(a) the Project Coordination Unit in MOET would be maintained with
functions and responsibilities acceptable to IDA and competent
staff in adequate numbers; and at all times during project
implementation, each Project Province shall appoint a Provincial
Project Coordinator a Provincial Project Management Committee, and
each Project District shall appoint Management Committee (para.
5.9);
(b) by October 1 in each year, commencing on October 1, 1994, the
following would be prepared and furnished to IDA: (i) an Annual
Work Program for project activities in the following calendar year
and; (ii) an Annual Business Plan for EPH of scope acceptable to
IDA; the Government would review such program and plan with IDA



- 45 -
prior to December 1 each year, and commencing on December 1, 1994,
thereafter implement them In a manmer satisfactory to IDA (para.
5.30);
(c) by December 1, 1997, a Mid-Term Review of project progress and
prospects would be undertaken in place of the Annual Review for
that year (para. 5.30);
(d) by December 31, 2000, the MOET shall have implemented a phased
program of school mapping covering all of Viet Nam acceptable to
IDA (para. 3.22);
(e) all training under the project would be carried out in accordance
with a program acceptable to IDA (para. 3.30)
(f) all studies under the project would be carried out in accordance
with the terms of reference and timing acceptable to IDA (para.
3.33); and
(g) by September 1, 1994 EPH shall prepare and furnish to IDA a plan
for textbook discribution and thereafter, implement as plan
satisfactory to IDA (para.3.7).
8.2       Subject to the above agreements and conditions, the proposed
project would constitute a suitable basis for an IDA Credit of SDR 49.6
million (US$70.0 million equivalent) to the Socialist Republic of Viet Nam, on
standard IDA terms, with a maturity of 40 years.

SOCIALIST REPUBLIC OF VIET NAM
PIMARY" EDUCATION PROJECT
EDUCATION FINANCE
1.          Structure and ComRosition.   Viet Nam's education budget contains
four fiscal transactions by levels of government: the central (trung ugna) and
three levels of local government (dia phbung): provincial, district and
commune. Within this structure, each level of government has its own
independent budget, revenues, and expenditures. Flows within these four
levels are handled through a system of grants. The MOF collects financial
data on expenditures by category from the national, provincial, and district
governments, but not from the commune governments. The budget thus assembled
is called the "consolidated budget." Since the communes do not report their
budgets to the central ministries, a comprehensive database which aggregates
all four levels into a single account of sectoral expenditure is lacking.
2.          Domestic sources of financing historically account for about 80
percent of total expenditure on education and training. The balance is
accounted for by external grants. Within domestic sources, financing of
education is borne principally by provincial governments, followed by the
central government and parental contributions. Government allocations are
raised chiefly through non-tax revenues (i.e. operating surplus, transfers
from state enterprises, and a small proportion of tax revenues from
agricultural and municipality taxes). Provinces receive significant budgetary
support from the central government only for special programs (i.e.
immunization or literacy campaigns). Districts receive most of their support
from transfers from provincial budgets, although they do have their own
revenue sources as well. Since the communes are expected to be basically
self-sufficient, they receive few direct transfers from the consolidated
budget.
3.          Although about four-fifths of the institutions of higher education
are financed from the central budget, the financing of most formal education
at the primary and secondary level remains the responsibility of the
provinces. In the largest cities, Hanoi, Haiphong, and Ho Chi Minh City,
(which have the status of provinces) responsibility is further decentralized
to individual districts within the cities. The provinces (or districts) are
responsible for paying all teachers' salaries. As noted, communes
(neighborhoods in urban areas, villages in rural areas) contribute to
education by providing funds for (a) school construction, maintenance, and
repair, (b) salary supplements for teachers, and (c) purchase of teaching
materials.
4.          The proportion of local to government spending in education varies
considerably across provinces as well as within them. It has been estimated
that public expenditures account for about 70t of the total, and the remainder
is raised through student fees, parents' contributions, and, in some cases, by
revenues from productive activities undertaken by the school or by a parent-



-51-
ANNEX 2
Page 2 of 6
teacher association.Y Revenues from student fees, parents' contributions,
and productive activities are normally used for supplies, equipment, mainte-
nance, and teaching materials, and also for salary supplements. Thus,
although education is publicly-sponsored substantial financial support comes
from individuals, households and communities.
5.          Trends.  The main characteristics underlying recent trends in
public expenditure in the education sector have been:
(a) slow growth in total expenditures in real terms;
(b) the large role of recurrent "autonomous' (salary and repair
and maintenance) expenditures in the budget; and
(c) low levels of investment.
6.          In the twelve-year period 1976-1987, an average of 6-8% of the
consolidated budget was spent on education and training. This peroentage was
low: several other countries similar to Viet Nam in terms of income per
capita (Bangladesh, Malawi, Nepal, Tanzania, Burkina Faso, Zambia, Uganda,
Togo) spend considerably more. Even so, this conclusion should be treated
with caution. Cross-country comparisons of education expenditures are
problematic because most countries have several financing sources but few
aggregate and reflect them all adequately. In some countries, for example,
the national or central government finances most education and training. In
other countries--particularly those with federal structures (e.g., China,
India, Nigeria, Brazil, the United States)--most education and training is
financed by lower levels of government. The role of private contributions
also varies enormously. Thus few countries have reliable data on expenditures
from all sources. (Chart 1 highlights the structure and composition of public
expenditure in education from 1984-1990.)
7.          Shrinking investments resulted in an increasing shortage of
classrooms. This, in turn, necessitated multiple shifts in most primary and
lower secondary schools. Supplies of textbooks, furniture and other teaching
materials also became so insufficient that many students had to share
textbooks and teachers had to share teaching materials. Triple shifting
became common, even in relatively high income areas, such as Ho Chi Minh,
Hanoi, and Haiphong.
8.          The social cost of economic reforms (j i) have been
considerable. Although the Government historically placed a great deal of
emphasis on primary education, the difficult fiscal situation placed
increasing responsibility on parents and communities. In recent years, most
new school construction at the primary level has been financed by Parents'
Associations, often with contributions from the neighborhood or People's
Committee. Such contributions also have been used for maintenance of
equipment and teaching materials when necessary.
1           The Parent-Teacher Association of the National Studies General
High School in Hui, for example, produced wooden furniture which was exported
to the Soviet Union.



-52-
ANNEX 2
Page 3 of 6
9.          In primary education, annual recurrent cost was 34,345 dong per
student (or only US$ 6.80 per student per year at 1990 prices). This was
about 4.2 percent of GNP per capita, compared to higher averages elsewhere in
Asia. This was attributable mainly to low teacher salaries and relatively
high student/teacher ratios.
10.         Teachers' base salaries, whlch were uniform nationwide, remained
low throughout the period. During 1990 and 1991, real salaries for teachers
declined, although there was some recovery in 1992 and 1993. Nevertheless, it
has been noted that real salaries of teachers in Viet Nam are one-half or less
of comparable levels in other low-income Asian countries.
11.         By way of illustration, at the top of the salary scale for
primary teachers, a well-qualified veteran with 30 years experience earned
63,000 dong (US$ 12.50) per month in 1990. This compared unfavorably to the
average wage in the state enterprise sector of 95,000 dong (US$ 19.0) per
month in 1990. In 1991, the salary of a beginning primary-school teacher was
25,000 dong per month (about US$6.40); an experienced secondary-school teacher
or director of a secondary school could receive 65,000 dong per month (about
US$16.70). University professors were also poorly paid: a beginning
professor earning a base salary of 30,000 dong (US$7.70) per month, plus a
supplement of 25,000 dong (US$6.40) per month; an experienced senior professor
earning a base salary of 60,000 dong (US$15.40) pet month, plus a supplement
of 15,000 dong (US$3.80) per month.
12.         Some teachers had additional sources of income other than their
salaries. Schools which could afford it paid teachers supplements of 1,000 to
7,000 dong per month (US$0.25-1.80) in the early 1990s. Teachers with some
knowledge of subject matter for which there was a private demand (like
English) also supplemented their salaries by teaching privately in the
evening. In fact, some doubled their incomes through private teaching. Even
so, the earnings were meager and there was concerns that qualified teachers
would be leaving the profession to seek occupations which paid better.
13.         A second reason for the low unit costs was the relatively high
pupil/teacher ratio. In 1989, pupIl/teacher ratios were 27/1 in primary, 17/1
in secondary, and 8/1 in higher education. The corresponding figures for Asia
were: 33/1, 24/1 and 14/1, respectively, suggesting that there may be room for
efficiency gains, particularly at secondary and higher levels. At the primary
level, larger pupil/tea4her ratios than the current figure of 27 in Viet Nam
exist in some countries, but with some adverse effects on cohort survival
rates. In the coming years, as the primary school-age population increases,
the pupil/teacher ratio is expected to increase unless mitigated by greater
accessibility through more classrooms and multiple shifting where feasible.
The latter is possible particularly in less densely populated areas.
14.         Government Policies.  Until recently, the government's policy was
to try to provide free public education at all levels to all children and
young people. Schools were created throughout the country, financed by the
government, teachers were paid with government resources, and textbooks were
supplied free for all pupils and students (at least in theory: the
discrepancy between demand and supply probably led to some role for money in
the allocation of the supply). Limited government resources have made it
difficult to maintain and operate the education system effectively. The



-53-
ANNEX- 2
Page 4 of 6
result has been a decline in enrollment ratios and a serious deterioration in
the quality of education at all levels.
15.         In view of the lack of sufficient resources to provide free public
education of good quality at all levels, the Council of Ministers recently
promulgated a series of measures designed to establish clearer priorities and
mobilize new resources for the sector, particularly from parents and students
themselves:
(a)     With technical assistance from UNICEF, the government
began experimenting with the privatization of
kindergarten; new kindergartens would be created as long
as parents were willing to pay the costs of operating
them. If the experiment is successful, cost-covering fees
will be introduced in kindergarten classes throughout the
country.
(b)     Instead of trying to achieve universal coverage at all
levels, the government has decided to concentrate its
scarce resources on primary and lower-secondary education.
Enrollments at higher levels of education will be limited
through the use of entrance examinations.
(c)     In September 1989, the government introduced a nationwide
system of limited student fees, from those students who
can afford to pay, to help cover costs. Under this
system, (i) Grades One through Three were still entirely
free, (ii) pupils (or their parents) in Grades Four and
Five paid the equivalent in money of one kilogram of rice
per month (in September 1989, about 500 dong), (iii)
students in lower-secondary school (Grades Six through
Nine) paid the equivalent in money of two kilograms of
rice per month (in September 1989, about 1,000 dong), and
(iv) students in upper-secondary school (Grades Ten
through Twelve) paid the equivalent in money of three
kilograms of rice per month (in September 1989, about
1,500 dong). Pupils and students who could provide a
certification from their village or neighborhood People's
Committee that their families were too poor to pay the
fees are not required to pay. The fees are collected at
the school level and may be used by the school's director
to pay for maintenance and upkeep, to purchase school
supplies and equipment, or to pay limited supplements to
teachers' salaries.
(d)     Also in September 1989, the government introduced the sale
of textbooks (rather than free provision) to students who
could afford to buy them.   Pupils and students who
provide a certification from their village or neighborhood
People's Committee that their families are too poor to buy
the textbooks may borrow copies from their school library.
Prices range from 600 dong (US$0.15) for a first-grade
reading textbook to 1,500 dong (about US$0.40) for a
twelfth-grade physics textbook.

Page 5 of 6
(e)     Private schools, although not encouraged, have been
tolerated, but enroll a very limited number of students.w
Students in 'private" schools pay a part of their
education, but not all; some operating costs were still
paid by the government.
it          About 2% of enrollments at a8l levels in Ho Chi Ninh City, for
example.
