SOCIALIST REPUBLIC OF VIET NAM
PRIMARY EDUCATION PROJECT
TEXTBOOKS COMPONENT
BACKGROUND
1.   As an aid to helping children learn, textbooks are the second most
important factor after the teacher. They contribute enormously to the
transmission of knowledge and to the formation of pupils' personalities.
Evidence from throughout Viet Nam indicates that a lack of the needed textbooks
is the single most important factor contributing to pupil repetition and dropout.
An estimated 40% of Vietnamese primary school pupils do not have any textbooks,
and many of the remaining 60% do not have all the books they need.
2.    Until 1989, the Government attempted to supply a complete set of textbooks
to all primary school children, free of charge. This could never be accomplished
due to financial constraints. In 1989, the Government implemented a policy of
selling textbooks to primary school pupils, albeit at highly subsidized prices.
The pupils who could not afford to buy textbooks were supposed to be able to rent
them for the school year at a lower cost than the purchase price. Those who
could not afford even to rent were supposed to be able to borrow them from the
school library for the school year, at no charge.
3.    In fact, this system never worked.  The schools in poor areas (particularly
rural) did not have the funds required to acquire the books to be distributed on
rental or free-lending basis. So, the pupils who could not afford to buy the
complete set of textbooks ended up with no or incomplete sets of textbooks, and
became prime candidates to repeat or drop out.
4.   The high number of separate textbook titles for each grade is another
aggravating aspect of the textbooks problem. For example, there are ten text
books for Grade 1, while most countries have just two, viz., language and
arithmetic, with basic concepts of social and natural sciences included in the
language book. The present textbooks also contain more material than can be
adequately covered in a school year. Although a complete set of textbooks is
produced for the standard primary school curriculum, there are not full ranges of
textbooks for minority language and multi-grade teaching.
5.    Another cause for the problem is existence of three different curricula for
primary education: (1) Standard curriculum of 165 weeks for a five-year primary
education course; (2) Abridged curriculum of 120 weeks for use in the ethnic
minority areasd; and, (3) Further abridged curriculum of 100 weeks for use in
the accelerated primary-school-equivalency courses for older youth and adults who
were unable to attend or complete primary school when they were children. The
Government tries to produce separate sets of textbooks for every grade, and for
each of the three curriculum.
L1    The rationale is that ethnic-minority children face a number of
difficulties in primary education and therefore need a simplified curriculum if
they are to make it through the primary cycle.



-57-                            ANNEX 3
Page 2 of 19
6.    Textbooks are produced and distributed by the Education Publishing House
(EPH) under the MOET. Its main office is in Hanoi and a large affiliate office is
in Ho Chi Minh City. The EPH is faced with a number of problems. Some of these
relate to publishing: arbitrary selection of authors (rather than competitions
to discover the best authors); no systematic training for authors; poor editing
services; low quality manuscript designs; need for improvement in graphic design
(illustrations) and page layout. Other problems relate to printing: printing is
done by arbitrarily assigning printing jobs to existing printing firms, rather
than through competitive bidding; printing services are not always of good
quality, yet because of the arbitrary and centralized manner in which printing
jobs are assigned, the EPH has little recourse; the EPH buys paper itself, rather
than leaving the job to the printers, and most of the paper is bought from a
government paper mill at an arbitrary price, rather than through competitive
bidding which might result in better quality paper at a lower price. Still other
problems relate to distribution: packing is often inadequate to protect from
damage during transportation, handling, and storage; distribution is not
scheduled evenly; warehouses at the provincial level often do not even have racks
to keep the books away from the dirt and damp.
7.    The textbooks are of poor vhysical quality and insufficient durability.
The paper (mostly of domestic production) is flimsy and tears easily. The paper
is of adequate quality for one-color work, but has excessive show/print-through
in two-color books. The light-weight textbook covers do not resist wear and tear
well. The low quality glue and thread used in binding allows the books to come
apart easily. The EPH has never been greatly concerned about the poor physical
quality of textbooks; since pupils are expected to buy textbooks annually, their
useful life is judged to be only one year, and as such, they do not need to be
more durable.
8.    The sale prices of the textbooks do not reflect real manufacturing costs
and are at least 75% less than they would be in a free market with no subsidies.
The sale price is low partly because of a deliberate government policy to
subsidize the price of textbooks and keep them affordable to a larger number of
children, and partly because the EPH itself can obtain a number of its inputs at
subsidized prices (e.g., paper; rent-free facilities; controlled printing prices;
managerial staff paid by the MOET).
9.    Presently, the EPH is a Government subsidized institution.  It also
recovers part of its costs through textbook sales. The EPH is faced with
constant and acute cashflow problems. It is chronically indebted to its bankers,
having to borrow money to produce textbooks, and repay only in part and mostly in
installments, depending upon the trickle of textbooks sale proceeds from schools
throughout the country. The production calendar depends on the pace of cost
recovery and on the availability of funds, rather than on optimal use throughout
the year of production capacity. The financial constraints dictate that EPH
plans production and distribution based on funiding availability and sales
potential, rather than the need to make a complete set of textbooks available to
the pupils in a timely fashion.
10. As an initial step, a system must be put into place which ensures that all
primary school pupils receive the full set of textbooks they need. It is not
enough, however, for children simply to have access to a certain number of text-
books. The textbooks need to be adapted to the children's capacity to learn and
to the reality of their everyday life. In addition, the textbooks must provide
children with the knowledge they need to be able to live in the modern world,



-58-                              ANNEX 3
Page 3 of 19
e.g., environmental concepts, health and nutrition, and technological concepts.
Textbooks should be free of sex bias and racial bias and discriminatory language
anid images.
11.   Viet Nam has alread: made an enormous effort in making textbooks available
to primary school pupils: some 60% of primary school pupils in Viet Nam have
access to textbooks which are adapted to the current curriculum and which show
major improvements in quality over textbooks used some years ago. Efforts are
needed, however, to improve the capacity of the textbook publishing and
distribution system in order to ensure that all pupils have access to the
required texts; and to sustain this achievement in the coming years, as the
school-age population grows, as more poor and minority children are brought into
and complete school, and as subsidies of inputs are reduced or eventually removed
(as part of the economic reforms currently being implemented by the Government).
Improvements are needed in the following areas: selection of authors; editorial
services; book illustration; book design and page layout; pilot testing of
materials; contracting of high-quality printers and suppliers of materials;
warehouse management; efficient and timely distribution of textbooks; and the
training of teachers in the use of new books. All these improvements should be
achieved at a cost level which will enable the Government of Viet Nam to sustain
textbook provision after completion of the project.
OBJECTIVES
12.   This project component would have three main objectives:
a.   To reduce repetition and dropout and improve learning by providing
all primary school pupils in Viet Nam with the full range of
textbooks they need for the complete primary cycle;
b.    To improve textbook quality in both content and form, at the most
economic cost, by improving:
i.    the quality of manuscript preparation and design,
ii.   paper and printing materials,
iii. the qualifications and skills of EPH staff,
iv.   the EPH's technical equipment, and
v.    the management of textbook publication and distribution; and
c.    To move, through the policy changes and financial interventions of
the project, from a highly subsidized system which is constantly in
debt to a system which will be self-sustaining and increasingly self-
financing.
TE$TBOOK AND RELATED MATERIALS PRODUCTION
13.  Number of Curricula:  Considering the cost implications, logistic hurdles
and most importantly, the fundamentals of actual needs, the present practice of
producing separate set of textbooks for the 165 week and 120 week curricula would
be discontinued. Instead, only one set of textbooks to match the 165 week
standard curriculum would be produced. This means that teachers and pupils of
the other two curricula will have additional material in the new cextbooks.
Teacher training courses, particularly those for the multi-grade classes and



-59-
ANNEX 3
Page 4 of 19
ethnic minorities, will include advice to teachers on how to adapt and make the
best use of the new textbooks.
14.  Number of Titles:  A total number of 21 titles would be produced as new
standard textbooks for the five primary school grades. The distribution would be
4,3,4,5 and 5 between Grade 1-5, respectively. Corresponding Teachers' Guides to
be produced would be 20, the distribution between Grades 1-5 being 3,3,4,5 and 5,
respectively. A total of 9 Pupils' workbooks would be produced for Grade 1-4,
the distribution being 3 for Grade 1, and 2 each for Grades 2-4. For the ethnic
minorities, 8 textbooks and 8 corresponding Teachers' Guides would be produced.
The exact number of titles for each minority would be decided with appropriate
consultations .
15. Standard Te;tbook Development: The preparation of new titles is well
advanced and the EPH is confident of being ready to publish a total of 7 titles
in the second year of the project. The EPH would allocate funds out of the
overhead portion included in the revenue from textbook sales and rentals to
develop and evaluate the new titles. The above allocated funds would be used,
Inter alia, for the following purposes in the process of new titles development
and evaluation:
a.   Provide the opportunity and means to the potential authors and
textbook production staff to visit mountainous and other rural
schools and to discuss the pupils' textbook needs with the
principals, teachers, pupils and educational officials;
b.    Organize competitions and pay fees (or other compensation) to several
different authors to prepare and complete manuscripts for each title,
so that the best among them can be selected for eventual publication.
Authors whose manuscripts are not selected for publication would
receive reasonable compensation (say, half the fee paid to successful
author of the corresponding competition);
c.    Elicit feed-back evaluation information from the teachers, education
specialists, pupils and principals on the proposed and published
titles;
d.    Purchase a wide range of primary school textbooks from several
countries, so that the potential authors can learn from and adapt (if
appropriate, and without infringing on copyrights) such materials;
and,
e.    Translate reputed and useful international textbooks (particularly
those for natural sciences) for use as reference materials, without
infringing on copyrights.
16. Ethnic Textbooks Develonment: Arrangements for ethnic minority language
textbook development would be similar to those for standard textbooks. This
project would concentrate on producing materials in the following eight minority
languages: Thai, Nuong, Nung, Hmong, Jarai, Bahnar, Cham and Sedang. Manuscript
ft   Jthnic minority language textbooks will supplment the standard textbooks.
The lnitial quantity of.8 titles is based on one for each of the ethnic minority
languages. The number of titles may increase as the ethnic minority textbook
content is elaborated and developed.

ANNM 3
Page 5 of 19
development and preparation of page film for the printer will be assisted by
UNICEF, with help from project supported technical assistance. The revenue
generated through the sale and rental of standard textbooks and workbooks would
be used to improve the contents of the ethnic minority language textbooks and, if
necessary, to increase the number of titles and print quantities.
17.   Phaging of New Titles Prtnting:  Logistically, it is not possible to
undertake the production of all the new titles In one go at the commencement of
the project, or for that matter even during the years following the project
effectiveness. Manuscripts have to be developed and tested. Teacher training
has to be imparted. EPH printing infrastructure and technology need upgrading.
Policy changes have to be addressed. The experiences gained have to be
evaluated. However, it is possible to commence the production of ethnic
textbooks and teachers' guides (as these are small in number, and the preparatory
work has covered much ground already), and the pupils' workbooks for Grades 1-4
(as the product content is simple). The EPH hopes to be in a position to produce
the new textbooks and teachers' guides for Grades 1 and 2 in the second year of
the project implementation. Those for Grades 3 and 4 would be produced in the
third year of the project. Those for Grade 5 would be produced in the fifth year
of the project.
18.   Print Ouantities:  The EPH determines the print quantities on an informed
guess basis. The results are as below:

19.  The print run percentage assumes that there is a reverse correlation
between age and wastage. While this may be true, there is no denying that the
percentages are arbitrary and are not backed by reliable statistics. Given the
substantially higher enrollment in lower classes, higher rate of repetition, and
only marginal differences in production costs between grades, there is a strong
possibility that scarce resources are not being used optimally. In the context
of uncontested complaint about lack of the needed books in the country as a
whole, such a casual approach is ill-advised. The extent of textbook surplus or
shortage could be minimized by a more rational approach.

the 1st print year, 50% each for the 2nd and 3rd print year, and 40% during the
4th print year, all of the respective enrollment pupils' population, compounded
at 2% p.a. for annual growth over the 1992 population.Q The approach ls similar
for teachers' guides, except that the calculations are made on teachers'
population. The print quantity for the standard workbooks will be 100% of the

pupils' population calculated as above, for project years 1-5. Ethnic textbooks

be noted that the phased printing plan (para 17) envisages no IDA financed

will be in active use at least for the estimated 3 year life cycle of each book.
In fact, the potential for the books which have been sold for full price
returning to circulation at a reduced resale price cannot be ruled out.
21.   Ouality of Textbooks:  New titles will be produced under improved quality
specification. Two-color textbook titles (those for Grades 1 and 2) will be
printed on 80 g/zO wood-free paper. One-color textbook titles will be printed on
70 g/uP wood-free paper, and workbooks will be printed on 60 g/uP part-mechanical
paper. Textbooks will have four-color printed varnished covers of 200 gIn1
board. All textbooks will be thread-sewn, and all workbooks will be wire-
stitched. Specifications for the old titles will remain unaltered, but with a
better cover, viz. 60 glne text paper and 120-140 g/m' cover paper.
22.   Printing Costs:  The production costs at current Vietnamese prices bears no
comparison to international open market prices for corresponding production. The
average cost at Vietnamese prices works out to the equivalent of US$ 0.14,
whereas the corresponding cost at international prices is US$ 0.37 per title.
The disparity is understandable, as the former is vitiated by macro and micro
economic incongruities. However, at the current state of the economy, it would
be unrealistic to cost the production or charge the user at international prices.
The only viable course is to start with the current Vietnamese prices and
progressively lncrease them over the project life to take into account the
probable impact of the macro-economic reforms that the Vietnamese Government has
embarked upon, (including the progressLvely reduced subsidles to the EPH).
23.   For project preparation purposes, the base manufacturLng costs of
textbooks, teachers' guides and workbooks have been estimated uslng current
Vietnamese prices. However, the combination of policy changes (relating to
procurement methods and practices, subsidies enjoyed or suffered, and cost
recovery) is bound to push actual production costs up. Thus, the current
Vietnamese prices have been compounded by a factor of 17S p.a. (excluding
inflation at 2.8% p.a.), to estimate base prices of textbook production costs
during the successive years of the project. The cost of printing the old
toxtbooks has also been given similar treatment.
24.   Distribution and Overheads:  The Government's attempts to address the
textbook availability issue through a policy of sell, rent for a fee or lend
freely, has not worked to date, though there is nothing wrong with policy Itself.
Some of the major reasons for the continuing failure of the EPH to produce
complete sets of quality textbooks and distribute them in time to the pupils
under an affordable method and price are:
A.    The number of curricula and number of textbooks for each grade are
too many to be handled efficiently within the technical and financial
capabilities of the EPH;
b.    The EPH has no handle on the optimum size of print quantities
required to meet the demand;



-62-                            AM  3
Page 7 of 19
c.    The physical quality is so poor that the textbooks seldom lst beyond
one year; this ruled out the success of rental scheme;
d.    The EPH is permanently cash-strapped due to low government
allocations, poor and delayed revenue collections; thus, the sets for
different grades are mostly incomplete;
e.    Distribution mechanism (scheduling despatches, packaging,
warehousing, sales, rentals etc.) is inadequate.
25.   Thus, pupils frequently lack an essential learning input.  The worst
sufferers are those from poor families who cannot even afford to rent textbooks.
Adequate care has been taken in the project design to avoid, or at least reduce
the adverse impact of these impediments through: (a) reduction of curriculum to
one and reduction in the number of textbooks; (b) improvement of EPH's financial
position by IDA funding of textbook production and by allowing the EPH to retain
the revenue from sales and rentals to meet, inter alia, the distribution and
overhead costs; (c) improvement of the content and physical qualities of
textbooks; and (d) provision of new equipment, training and technical assistance.
26.   As part of the project intervention, IDA funds would be made available to
produce textbooks of improved quality in adequate numbers. Having assured the
production of improved textbooks, the next logical step is to ensure a rational
distribution system. Until a more innovative and cost-effective method is found,
EPH will continue to distribute the textbooks through its subsidiary companies.
Pupils will have the option of either buying the textbooks at full price or
renting for a fee. Those who cannot afford either option would be allowed to
borrow the textbooks free of charge. In the latter two cases, the students are
bound to return the textbooks in acceptably good condition, so that they can be
reused for a period of at least three years. Presenting an acceptable system for
this scheme together with a time table for its implementation will be a
precondition for disbursement of IDA funds for the production of standard
textbooks.
27.   A reasonable estimate of average distribution costs works out to 208 of the
cover price of the textbook. Overhead costs, which represent all costs relating
to the development and evaluation of new titles, are estimated at 10% of the
textbook cover price. Together, the distribution and overhead costs account for
30% of the cover price, which comprises the production, distribution and overhead
costs. In other words, distribution and overhead costs amount to about 42.8% of
the production costs.8
28.   IDA funds would be used fcr defraying the production costs.  When the
textbooks are sold or rented for a fee, the relevant production cost is recovered
as part of the textbook cover price. Such recovered funds would vest with the
EPH and would be used to meet any of the following costs in the order of
preferenceg:
a.   Textbook overhead costs (primarily for textbook development)
iJ These percentages are rough estimates based on inadequate, but best available

29.   With progressive cost escalations, the cover price of textbooks will
correspondingly go up. This will cause sliding movement between the three
proposed methods of distribution. From year to year, some of the pupils buying
books at full price will slip to the borrow-at-a-fee category, and some from the
latter category to borrow-freely category. As a consequence the mass of pupils in
the middle category may be static. Reliable figures are not available of the
present distribution of pupils among the three categories. The financial
organizational state of EPP do not support the indicated composition between
these three categories at 500:35%:15%. Considering these facts, the initial
ratio of textbook distribution between the three different categories is
estimated at 35*:45%:20% and to move to about 20%:45%:35% by the fifth year of
the project. In the case of workbooks (where no rental is possible as the book
is not reusable), the sale at full price is counted at 50% initially, going down
to 35% by the 5th year of the project. As the cost of workbooks is only about
25% of the textbooks, and the numbers are small (between 2 and 3 books per
grade), the higher estimated percentages are reasonable.
30.   EPH's Financial Status: Reliable or complete information is not available
on the financial status of the EPH. Hence, it is not possible to present
projections of EPH's balance sheet, income and expenditure or cashflow. Given
this, IDA should intensively supervise the textbool component during the first
year of implementation. In particular, close supervision will be required to set
the financial, management, procurement and human resources sections of the EPH in
order.
31.   EEL-Cashflow Statement: However, based on the assumptions and estimates

the base year;

in print years 2 and 3 and 40% in print year 4;

35.   Overall, while the project interventions can reasonably expect the
financial viability and self-sustainability of the EPH to improve from the
present precarious state of affairs, the need for speedy and substantial
improvement in the overall efficiency of the EPH cannot be over-emphasized. The
project design takes adequate care to address the latter issue.
TRAINING AND TECHNICAL ASSISTANCE:
36.  Given the primacy of textbooks in primary education, the key role played by
the EPH, and the national and sector policy reforms under way, it appears
imperative that the academic, technical and managerial skills of EPH officials
are upgraded. This goal is intended to be achieved by means of training
workshops conducted with the technical assistance from internationally recruited
short-term specialists, and study tours to neighboring as well as developed
countries. In all these cases, the stated subjects and other parameters are
indicative and by no means rigid or exhaustive.   They can be modified rationally
as appropriate, without increasing the budgeted total costs.
37.   Training Workshops:  Two training workshops of 2 weeks each would be
conducted during each of the first 5 years of the project. The workshops would
be held in Hanoi and Ho Chi Minh City back to back, so that the same visiting
International specialist could handle one after the other. Table 1 provides the
desired specialization of the internationally recruited short term experts and
the duration of their service.

The study tours would be spread over the first 5 years of the project. Base cost
for each staff would be US$ 2,000 per tour.

needs to be provided. The most essential item is the layout equipment, which

46.  While care has been taken to ensure that the proposed equipment to be
procured under the project would be compatible with those that have been or will
be provided by the UNICEF, this important issue to be borne in mind during all
stages of equipment procurement under the project through maintaining a close

47.  The EPH's printing plant is going through a modernization program.  Some of
the equipment stated in the Table 3 may be procured out of funds from EPH's own
or some other alternate source of funds before the procurement under the project
may take place. Moreover, the proposed policy changes in the EPH's procurement
methods and practices may reduce or change the configuration of the equipment
list. Considering all these issues, the EPH is given the flexibility to propose
to the IDA for its consideration and approval at the appropriate stage, the
changes that would be required to the equipment list.
AID TO PROVINCIAL SCHOOL LIBRARIES
48.  Each of the 53 provinces has a central school library, which should house a
wide range of reference books and also the textbooks meant for rent and free
lending. To enable these libraries to effect minor repairs to buildings, to
purchase additional book-shelves as well as additional reference books, a sum of
US$ 4,000 is provided in the under the project for each central school library.

SOCIALIST REPUBLIC OF VIET NAM
PRIMARY EDUCATION PROJECT
BASIC TEACHING AIDS FOR PRIMARY SCHOOLS
Many Vietnamese primary schools, especially those in rural areas, have
virtually no teaching aids; often the only teaching aid other than textbooks
is a blackboard. Where such aids are available at all, they are often twenty
or thirty years old and in a bad state of disrepair. Teaching and learning
suffer, and children who are unable to learn are at high risk of repeating or
dropping out of school before they achieve functional literacy.
Under the project, it is proposed to address this critical quality issue
by providing a standard package of very simple equipment and basic teaching
aids such as maps, a globe, and measuring and weighing devices to every school
offering primary education in the country. Such aids are already being
produced in Viet Nam. The standard package, prepared by the Ministry of
Education and Training's General Company for Educational Equipment and
consisting of 34 items, is estimated to cost about US$250 equivalent.
Viet Nam has a long tradition whereby teachers develop and produce their
own teaching and learning aids. Efforts to encourage this tradition have
included competitions, workshops, and incentives for teachers. This effort
would be further ercouraged under the project. Specifically, the project
would include financial assistance for organizing, at the provincial and at
the national levels, exhibitions of self-produced teaching aids. Such
exhibitions would be held twice during the project period. At the provincial
level, a prize consisting of a set of "ideal* teaching aids and equipment
would be awarded to the school whose teacher had developed the teaching aid
judged best in the exhibition, The winners in the provincial competitions
would be allowed to exhibit in the national competitions.

Note 1.      Viet Ma has 62 institutions uhich train teachers for primary schools. The annual output is about 12,000.  Of these 62 institutfonts  o0 (46
Primary Teacher Training Schools and 14 Junior Teacher Training Colleges) are financed and operated by provitnIfal goverrents; 2 Teacher
Training Colleges are financed and operated by the Rinistry of Education and Training (national governeent).
Note 2.     All of Viet 11m*s current 53 provinees have at least one institution which trains teachers for primary schools.
Note 3.     PTTS a Primary Teacher Trainins School (Trugna C tI   Mae Su MM). These institutions offer the standard teacher training course for
primary-school teachers, which consists of 2 years of teacher training follofing 12 years of general education (primary plus Lower and upper
secondary). Some also offer the alternative training course for primary-school teachers, which consists of 3 years of teacher training
following 9 years of general education (primary plus lower secondary). Graduates receive a certificate.
JTTC u Junior Teacher Training College (Tc=na Cao Dona Su Pla). These institutions mainly train teachers for lower-secondary schools in a
course consisting of 3 years of teacher training folloming 12 years of general education (primary plus lower and upper secondary). Graduates
receive a certificate.  Thirteen JTTCs (those listed in this annex) also train teachers for primary schools (also in a 3-year course
followfng 12 years of general education).
TTC a Teacher Training College (Truor Rdj Hoe Su Pha).  These institutions mainly train teachers for upper-secondary schools in a course           (5
consisting of 4 years of teacher training following 12 years of general educatfon (primary plus lower and upper secondary).  Graduates              w. w
receive a diploma.  Two TTCs (those listed in this annex) also train teachers for primary schools (also in a 4-year course following 12 years
of general education).

THE ETHNIC NINORITIESfMOUNTAINOUS AREAS EDUCATION COMPONENTY'

1.          Viet Nam bas 54 ethnic groups.  Of these, ethnic Kinh represent
about 87% of the total population. Of the remainder, two groups, the Tay and
the Thai, have populations in excess of one million each, according to the
1989 Population Census.  Another twelve groups--the Muong, Hoa, Khmer (Khome),
Nung, Hmong, Yao (Dao), Jarai (Ciarai), Ede (Rhade), Bahnar (Bana), San Chay,
Chai, and Sedang (Xodang)--have populations ranging from almost one million to
around 0.1 million. At the other end of the scale, the smallest ethnic
minorities have populations of only 100-200. Each group has its own language
and culture. The larger groups have many, sometimes scores, of local
subgroups with their own names and dialects. Although many of these ethnic
minorities live in the mountainous areas, some (e.g., the Cham) live in the
lowlands, and some (e.g., the Hoa) are predominantly urban dwellers. Ethnic
minorities live throughout the national territory and are represented in
virtually all of the provinces of Viet Nam.
2.          One of the main objectives of the proposed Primary Education
Project is to improve primary education for ethnic minority children, particu-
larly those who live in Viet Nam's mountain areas. These children face a
number of special problems. Inputs provided under the component would not
address all of the problems. In particular, it would not immediately or
directly improve the gconomic conditions of ethnic minority families, even
though research indicates that (i) high rates of participation in primary
education are very closely linked to high rates of economic growth and (ii)
primary education has a high economic rate of return. Neither would it change
entrenched cultural perceptions as to the value of education, although it
would Improve the quality of the educational services being offered, thus
encouraging a more positive view of the value of education. The component
would provide a comprehensive package of educational inputs to selected ethnic
minority children, most of them living in mountainous areas, and to other
children in the mountainous areas. This proposed package would consist of (a)
gnlicy measures; (b) pedagogical activities; (c) grovision gf phyical
facilities in support of the policy measures and pedagogical activities; and
(d) institution-building.

same title, which is available in the project files.

?roposed Activities
3.          The gglicy measures are intended to ensure that existing primary-
education facilities are used to capacity and that all primary children are
exposed to the same basic curriculum. In this regard, the Government has
indicated its intent to adopt the 165 week curriculum for all regular pzimary
classes.
4.          The pggdg2gical actvitfie  would support three areas of special
concern for ethnic minorities and other populations living in mountainous
areas: (i) multi-grade teaching, for remote, sparsely populated mountainous
areas where there are not enough children in a given school catchment area to
justify having a single teacher and a separate classroom at each grade level;
(ii) mother-tongue literacy teaching for ethnic minority children, and (lii)
the teaching of Vietnamese as the national language, for the same children.
The first of these three areas, multi-grade teaching, is not necessarily
limited to ethnic minority children; nevertheless, the two phenomena--sparse
population and ethnic minority population--often coincide in Viet Nam's
mountainous areas.
5.          The following pedagogical activities would be undertaken:
(a) Development of learning materials suitable for multi-grade
teaching (payment to authors, printing, testing, and
distribution);
(b) Training of teachers in the techniques of multi-grade
teaching for children living in sparsely populated areas;
(c) Revision and/or reprinting of materials that have already
been developed in ethnic minority languages;
(d) Carrying out of studies (with the help of technical
assistants) in selected major ethnic minority languages,
establishment of approved orthographies for each language,
and devclopment of reference materials, to contribute to the
development of appropriate language policies;
(e) Development of learning materials for mother-tongue literacy
teaching in eight selected major ethnic minority languages
(Thai, Muong, Nung, Hmong, Jarai, Bahnar, Chan, and Sedang);
(f) Training of teachers in the techniques of mother-tongue
literacy teaching for ethnic minority children;
(g) Development of learning materials to be used for teaching
Vietnamese as the national language; and
(h) Training of teachers in the techniques of teaching Vietnamese
as the national language to children from ethnic minorities.
6.          Finally, once the policy measures have been initiated and
pedagogical activities are underway, limited phyuical fa.litSes would be



-83-                    ANNEX 6
Page 3 of 21
provided under the project to expand and improve the network, as required.
This would include:
(a) Repair of existing classrooms, replacement of seriously
dilapidated classrooms which are beyond repair, and
construction of new classrooms, if necessary, in sites
selected according to agreed criteria;
(b) Provision of latrines and water systems, as necessary, in all
schools benefiting from civil works under this component; and
(c) Provision of essential school furniture (including desks,
chairs, blackboards, and storage cupboards) in all schools
benefiting from civil works under this component.
7.          Institution-building, would be supported through:  (a) increasing
the capacity of the Ministry of Education and Training (MOET), its Research
Center for Ethnic Minority Education (RCEME), Provincial and District
Education Services, and primary-school teachers to deal effectively with the
educational problems of ethnic minority children and other children in
mountainous areas; and (b) the establishment of a Steering Committee comprised
of the major partner agencies to guide and manage the ethnic minority
component. Assistance would be provided through the establishment of
appropriate management and advisory units, the provision of expatriate
technical assistance, the collection of data, the carrying out of local
seminars and workshops, the provision of material inputs including vehicles
and office equipment, and the introduction of on-going programs to develop
appropriate materials and train teachers.
S.          Proposed sequence of activities.  This annex presents a proposal
for (a) the policy measures required for the component, (b) the implementation
and scheduling of the related pedagogical aspects, and (c) the provisign of
Dhnsical facilities in the mountain areas (quantities are given in the annexes
describing the project's Construction Component). It is proposed that, to the
extent feasible, the building and repair activities and the provision of
furniture foll   the introduction of the policy measures and the successful
implementation of the pedagogical activities: i.e., civil works (repair and
rehabilitation of existing classrooms, construction of new classrooms,
provision of latrines and water systems) and provision of furniture would not
take place until the policy measures had been introduced and until the
pedagogical activities (e.g., research into the ethnolinguistic situation;
development, testing, printing, and distribution of appropriate learning
materials; training of teachers) had been successfully completed. The
districts and villages where the pedagogical aspects had been implemented
successfully should therefore be the first ..reas to benefit from the provision
of facilities. This sequence would ensure that repaired classrooms would be
adequately utilized and would have trained teachers and adequate materials.
Minor repairs of schools could, however, be carried out under the component at
the same time as the development of the pedagogical materials and the training
of teachers. The costs for such minor repairs would be relatively small.
Criteria for the Selection of Ethnic Ninorities



-84-                    AM  6
Page 4 of 21
9.          Given the impossibility of working with all ethnic minorities
under a single project, beneficiary groups were carefully selected based on a
number of criteria. A first consideration was Interest in participating on
the part of the ethnic minority in question. During project preparation
primary schools in several ethnic minority areas were visited, and discussions
were held (through interpreters) with ethnic minority pupils, parents, and
teachers. All groups contacted expressed interest in participating in the
component. Thus, while interest was of primary importance for participation,
it was not a useful consideration In narrowing the focus to a feasible number
of groups.
10.         A second consideration was the potential ipact that could be
derived from a given investment in linguistic research and the development of
teaching and learning materials in a given ethnic minority language. This
consideration intrinsically favored larger groups. On this basis, it was
decided to focus on ethnic minorities with populations of about 100,000 or
more, leaving the remaiuder for a future project.
11.         A third consideration, transferability, is a corollary of the
second consideration: whether work done on one ethnic minority language could
be easily transferred to another, related ethnic minority language. Work done
to develop learning materials in the Nung language, for example, would be very
useful for later work on the Tay language, or vice versa; similarly, work done
on the Hmong language would be very useful for later work on the Yao language,
or vice versa. On the basis of this consideration, it was decided to focus on
Tay or on Nung, but not both, and on Hmong or on Yao, but not both, in the
component.
12.         A fourth consideration was availabllitv, i.e. whether teaching and
learning materials were already available in a given language. It was
determined that a dearth of materials exists for all ethnic minorities. A
fifth consideration was whether a given ethnic minority was already bilingul,
reducing the urgency of providing mother-tongue literacy teaching.
13.         A sixth consideration was the distribution of ethnlc minority
ggpolations. With the exception of the Hoa and the Khmer, who are not
considered "indigenous" ethnic minorities, the six largest ethnic minorities
in Viet Sam are all located in the northern part of the country. It was felt
that some geographical diversity was worthwhile, and it was decided that about
half of the groups to be included in the component should be from the South
and about half from the North.
14.         A seventh consideration was whether the target area being
considered was mountainous. It was felt important to take into account
factors such as remoteness, difficulty of access, and relative lack of
physical Infrastructure as well as presence of ethnic minorities. However,
with the exception of the 11 provinces of the Nekong Delta and 3 provinces
between Hanoi and the sea, afl the provinces of Viet Nam are wholly or



-85-                    ANNEX 6
Page 5 of 21
partially mountainous. This consideration was therefore not very useful in
limiting the scope of the component.2'
15.         An eighth consideration was previous involvement of other
agengies. UNICEF, in particular, has been involved in a basic education
project with some of the largest ethnic minorities in eight provinces of Viet
Nam; its project has focused on the In-service training of teachers to enable
them to teach successfully in a multi-grade classroom, but not on ethnic
minority languages per i in these eight provinces. On the basis of this
consideration, it was felt useful to build on the experience of UNICEF in
these eight provinces.
16.         On the basis of all these considerations, a tentative list of
provinces and ethnic minorities to benefit from the component was drawn up.
The list includes the eight provinces where UNICEF has been active: Son La,
Hoa Binh, Lang Son, Yen Bai, Lao Cai, Gia Lai, Kon Tum, and Ninh Thuan. The
RCEME is presently collecting data on the ethnic minority languages spoken,
and number of speakers, in each of the eight selected provinces. About 45% of
Viet Nam's ethnic minority population are represented by ethnic minority
languages spoken in these eight provinces. The list also includes eight of
the 14 largest ethnic minorities in Viet Nam:  the Thai, the Muong, the Nung,
the Hmong, the Jarai, the Bahnar, the Cham, and the Sedang. Four of these are
major groups in the North of Viet Nam; the other four are major groups in the
South. Furthermore, all are linguistically related to other ethnic
minorities, and it is hoped that the experience gained can be transferred to
other groups, with the lessons learned in the eight selected provinces being
used to expand similar activities to other provinces in subsequent programs.
.articiDating Agencies
17.         Three agencies (in addition to the World Bank) worked together to
develop the package of inputs for ethnic minorities and suggest provinces and
groups to benefit from the project. The first was the RCEME. Staffed largely
by native ethnic minority speakers, its role is to promote research on issues
and problems affecting ethnic minority education and encourage the development
of educational programs providing solutions to these problems.
18.         The second party was UNICEF.  UNICEF's Program Officer for
Education in Viet Nam participated in the World Bank's Second Preparation
Mission for this project (January-February 1991) and every mission thereafter,
until she moved to a new position in early 1993. UNICEF continues to be
involved in a basic education project with ethnic minorities in all eight of
the selected provinces which has been funded with donations from Australia and
Japan. This project, which has focused on in-service training of teachers,
has given UNICEF a great deal of valuable experience in the introduction of
multi-grade teaching -- including experience in the development of appropriate
curricula, the production and provision of suitable teaching aids, and the
V           As it turned out, however, seven of the eight ethnic minorities
chosen for inclusion in the component are mountain-dwellers; only the Cham are
lowland dwellers, albeit in a relatively less densely populated part of the
coast.

training of teachers. UNICEF would continue its basic education project and
to coordinate it with parallel activities being assisted under the proposed
Primary Education Project so that useful innovations may be replicated in as
wide an area as possible. Because of this experience, it is proposed that the
agency play a key role in providing advice and guidance to the Vietnamese
authorities responsible for implementing this component. Specifically, the
Chief of UNICEF's Education Program would provide advice not only on multi-
grade teaching but also on other activities to be implemented under this
component.
19.         The third party involved in developing the component was World
Vision. World Vision, a non-governmental organization (NGO) already active in
Viet Nam, began discussions with the Government of Viet Nam about possible
assistance for ethnic minority education in March 1990 and began discussions
with the World Bank shortly thereafter. World Vision has provided a full-time
expatriate specialist in ethnic minority languages, serving as Aoplied
Linguistics Advisor (a linguist with expertise in developing textbooks) (terms
of reference are attached), and is planning to provide a full-time expatriate
specialist in ethnic minority education, serving as Ethnic Minority Literacy
Advisor (terms of reference are attached). These two specialists would work
closely with RCEME and UNICEF in the implementation of the component.'
Ethnic Minority ParticiRation
20.         A number of activities have already taken place, and others are
under way, to: (a) confirm that the eight selected ethnic minorities were and
continue to be interested in participating in the project and (b) ensure that
these groups participate actively in the development and implementation of the
component.   An Ethnic Minority Literacy Seminar, sponsored by the MOET in
conjunction with World Vision, was held in Viet Nam in March 1992, and
representatives from all eight ethnic groups selected were invited, to
determine the extent of their interest, discuss the proposed component, and
elicit their views and advice on how to proceed. The representatives of all
eight groups endorsed the types of activities envisioned, expressed enthusiasm
at being included in the component, and shared ideas for improving
implementation which have been incorporated into the component's design.
21.         The RCEME has been doing surveys in four selected provinces since
mid-1992 to gather base-line information cn the groups in these areas. The
World Vision-financed Applied Linguistics Advisor has, together with RCEME
staff, developed an instrument for gathering demographic and ethnolinguistic
data. They have been gathering data on the number of native speakers of each
selected ethnic minority language and making an inventory of the linguistic
and educational materials already available in these languages. The resulting
data will be used in further planning and selection of experimental schools in
which the component's pedagogical activities would be introduced.
1           The two full-time specialists are being provided to World Vision
by The Summer Institute of Linguistics, an NGO specializing in ethnic minority
languages and literacy education.

22.         In addition to gathering data available in Hanoi, RCEME and World
Vision have begun to undertake a series of in-depth visits to ethnic minority
areas in the eight selected provinces. These in-depth visits began in March
1993 with a visit to the Nung ethnic minority in Lang Son Province and in
April with a visit to the Hmong in Lao Cai and Son La Provinces. Similar
visits are scheduled to the Cham (in Ninh Thuan Province), Jarai (in Gia Lai
Province), Bahnar (in Gia Lai and Kon Tum Provinces), and Sedang (in Kon Tum
Province). The two remaining visits, to the Thai (in Son La and Yen Bai
Provinces) and the Muong (in Hoa Binh Province), are to be completed in late
1993. These in-depth visits to the eight ethnic minority areas are being
conducted by World Vision and RCEME staff who are native speakers from the
ethnic minority being visited. The main purpose of the visit is to discuss
the proposed project with ethnic minority cowmunities, solicit their advice
and suggestions, and confirm their interest (or lack thereof). Also during
the visits, detailed statistics are collected -- ouch topics as the numbers of
speakers of the ethnic minority language, by district, the number of primary
schools, and enrollments (both total and ethnic minority). In addition, an
inventory is made of any existing educational materials or literature
available in the ethnic minority language. After the first series of visits
is completed, follow-up visits will take place regularly t'uroughout the
implementation of the component.
23.         Presently, MOET, assisted by UNICEF and World Vision, is actively
preparing for two workshops: (a) a workshop on the development of schooling
for Cham children (scheduled for October 1993) and (b) a workshop on the
development of schooling for Hmong children (scheduled for November 1993).
These workshops are additional preparatory steps in the implementation of the
component.
Management and Staffing (See Chart 3)
24.         The implementation of the component would be under the overall
supervision of the Director of the RCEME, who would serve as Assistant Project
Manager for the Ethnic Minorities/Mountainous Areas Education Components and
have day-to-day management responsibilities for component activities in the
northern part of Viet Nam. He would be assisted in his responsibilities by a
Deputy Assistant Project Manager for the Ethnic Minorities/Mountainous Areas
Education Component, who would have day-to-day management responsibilities for
component activities in the southern part of the country. These two
individuals would be assisted by a Steering Committee and various national
staff and expatriate technical advisors, as described below. In addition to
coordinating the activities of all the various expatriate and national staff
working on the implementation of this component, the Assistant Project Manager
and the Deputy Assistant Project Manager for this component would be
responsible for liaising with: (i) the Assistant rroject Manager for the
Construction Component (to ensure that the construction activities are
coordinated with the pedagogical activities); (ii) the Assistant Project
Manager for the Textbook Component (to ensure the timely preparation of

26.         Steering Committee.  A Steering Committee would be formed to
provide advice and guidance in the implementation of the component. The
Steering Committee would be comprised of:
(a) the National Project Manager, Primary Education Project;
(b) the Assistant Manager, Ethnic Minority Component;
(c) an Accountant;
(d) the Primary Education Advisor, UNICEF; and
(e) the Applied Linguistics Advisor, World Vision.
The Steering Committee would meet informally and often, according to
operational procedures agreed during the first six months after Project
signing. The RCEME would function as a secretariat for the Steering Committee
and would continue to provide expertise and overall staff support and
assistance for implementation of the component throughout the Project period.
27.         National Advisory Council for Ethnic Minority Education.  A
National Advisory Council for Ethnic Minority Education (NACEME) would also be
established. This council would provide an opportunity for other institutions
and individuals with special interest in or responsibilities related to the
component to provide guidance and advice to the Assistant Project Manager for
this component and to coordinate their various inputs under the component.
The NACEME would be comprised of at least the following members:
(a) the Assistant Project Manager for the Textbook Component;
(b) the Assistant Project Manager for the Construction Component;
(c) the Assistant Project Manager for Administration and Finance;

North and one in the South.
