31.         Language Teams. Language Teams would be formed for all activities
related to each of the eight languages selected. Each language team would be
advised by a part-time expatriate Specialist in Ethnic Minority Languages and
would include two national Core Language Cadres for each of the eight ethnic
minority languages selected. The Language Teams for the Muong, Hmong, Bahnar,



-92-                     ANNE 6
Page 12 of 21
and Sedang languages would be assisted by part-time national Mother-Tongue
Lexicographers (terms of reference attached in Annex 15).
32.         As described above, four Language Teams would be included in the
Implementation Unit for the North, and four would be included in the
Implementation Unit for the South. Each member of the eight Language Teams
would be responsible for the following activities: (i) assist in the
production of instructional materials in his/her native language, (ii) assist
in the testing and revision of materials, (iii) improve his/her individual
skills in mother-tongue literacy teaching and the teaching of Vietnamese as
the national language, (iv) assume leadership in the Teacher Training Phase,
and (v) monitor and evaluate the effectiveness of the program in the schools
selected to benefit from this component.
33.         Province Advisory Councils.  Province Advisory Councils would be
established in each of the eight provinces selected. These councils would be
comprised of ethnic minority leaders and representatives, representatives of
the Provincial Education Service, a representative from each District
Education Service where project activities are being carried out, a
representative of the province's Primary Teacher Training School, local ethnic
minority specialists from the province, and primary-school teachers with
experience in teaching in ethnic minority areas. These Province Advisory
Councils would meet regularly, at least once a quarter, to (a) help explain
all project activities to' the communities included in the project, (b) serve
as a vehicle for expressing the views of the communities, including the ethnic
minority populations, to the education authorities, (c) review progress of the
component in their respective province, (d) provide advice and guidance to
their respective Implementation Unit, (e) coordinate their inputs to project
implementation, and (f) assist in selecting the schools where the project
would be implemented.
34.         Primarv Teacher Training Schools.  In addition to sending a
representative to its respective Province Advisory Council, the Primary
Teacher Training School in each of the eight provinces would select three
staff to participate part-time in the implementation of the component. Each
of these persons would be-come familiar with multi-grade teaching, mother-
tongue literacy teaching, and the teaching of Vietnamese as the national
language. They would assist in logistical matters and in coordinating with
the teachers and schools in each province and district and would serve as a
link between the respective Implementation Unit and the selected schools.
They would also take the lead in organizing and carrying out the teacher
training to be provided in the Teacher Training Phase.
Phasing of Activities
35.         It is anticipated that the implementation of the component would
be carried out in six phases, as follows:
(a) an Organizational Phase;
(b) a Materials Development. Testing. and Publication Phase;
(c) a Teacher Training Phase;



-93-                    ANNEX 6
Page 13 of 21
(d) a Classroom Imnlementation Phase;
(e) a Construction Phase; and
(f) an Evaluation Phase.
Although these phases would begin sequentially, some of them would overlap in
implementation. For example, the Teacher Training Phase would begin (with
training of teachers for multi-grade teaching situations) before the Materials
Development Phase had been completed. Since, however, the six phases comprise
distinct activities, they are described separately. The remainder of this
annex suggests the tentative content of the six phases. This content should
be reviewed and finalized by the Steering Committee by December 31. 1993.
The Qreanizational Phase
36.         The Organizational Phase has already begun and is being
implemented by RCEME, other units of the MOET, UNICZF, and World Vision. In
addition to selecting eight provinces and eight ethnic minorities, carrying
out surveys, and undertaking community mobilization activities, RCEHE, UNICEF,
and World Vision are preparing a detailed implementation plan for this
component.  This draft should indicate inter-alia:  (i) any changes in scope
proposed since the original discussions, (ii) activities underway or
completed, (iii) the office space to be used in Hanoi and Nha Trang, and (iv)
specifics of the activities to be funded by the Government. As noted, UNICEF
and some World Vision staff are in place. In addition World Vision plans to
provide assistance with some of the following items: (a) two vehicles, one
for each Implementation Unit, (b) microcomputers, and (c) other office
equipment. As noted, terms of reference have been prepared for all national
staff and expatriate technical advisors. Finally, a number of national staff
have already been identified who will participate in implementation.
37.         During the Organizational Phase, the MOET should complete the
selection of those staff to work full-time on the implementation of the
component. The names and profiles of these staff should be sent to the World
Bank for review and approval by December 31. 1993. In addition, the RCEME
should proceed with the establishment of the Steering Committee and the NACENE
and initiate identification of the various expatriate technical assistants.
38.         Also during the Organizational Phase, a part-time national
Educational Materials Researcher (terms of reference attached) would catalog
existing literacy and other instructional materials available in the eight
ethnic minority languages selected. This work, which is expected to take
about four months, would be carried out in Hanoi, Ho Chi Minh City, Nha Trang,
the eight provinces selected, and other areas of the country where such
materials might exist.
39.         After the completion of the ethnolinguistic surveys and an
analysis of the data collected during the surveys, 40 experimental primary
schools would be selected, five for each of the eight ethnic minority
languages included in this component. These schools would have the following
characteristics:



-94-                    ANNEX 6
Page 14 of 21
(a) they would be located within one of the eight provinces
selected for the component;
(b) they would include a very high representation from one of the
eight selected ethnic minority languages;
(c) their facilities would be very rudimentary, particularly
temporary thatch-roofed structures ithout walls;
(d) the population they serve would be in the lowest 10% in terms
of per capita income;
(e) they would consist of a central school and one or more
affiliate schools which were more than 5 kilometers from the
central school and which could not offer all five grades,
either because of insufficient physical space or because of
the absence of a teacher skilled in multi-grade teaching;
(f) they would be accessible by road; and
(g) their staff would be willing to participate in the project.
40.         Also during the Organizational Phase, the 16 Core Language Cadres
would par.l.cipate in workshops on mother-tongue literacy teaching and teaching
Vietnamese as a national language, conducted by the Applied Linguistics
Advisor and the Ethnic Minority Literacy Advisor. The Core Language Cadres
would receive assistance from the eight expatriate Specialists in Ethnic
Minority Languages in planning (a) the preparation, testing, and evaluation of
materials for mothar-tongue literacy teaching and for teaching Vietnamese as
the national language and (b) the preparation of training courses for primary-
school teachers in these areas.
41.         The executive secretaries and the persons trained in statistics
and in the use of a data-processing program would organize semtnars in basic
statistics and data-processing for the staff in the two Implementation Units,
the eight Primary Teacher Training Schools, and the Provincial and District
Education Services who would be responsible for monitoring the project and
preparing the reports. Money would be made available for taking the courses
in basic statistics and data-processing provided by the Computing Center at
the National Institute for Education Science in Hanoi.
42.         The MOET has already undertaken school mapping in a number of
districts in the eight selected provinces, using a methodology introduced
during project preparation. The preparation of district school maps, one for
every district in each of the eight provinces included in this Component,
would continue and would be completed within the first two years of the
project. In each of the eight mountainous provinces included in the project,
at least one District Education Service should complete a detailed school map
before negotiations of the IDA Credit. By January 1, 1996,Y detailed school
maps should have been prepared for the remaining districts of these eight
f           Or about two years after Credit effectiveness.



-95-                     ANNEX 6
Page 15 of 21
provinces. This work should be done with the help of the respective
Provincial Education Service under the guidance of the MOET.
43.         Each district school map would show all schools in the district.
For each school, both the central school and all affiliate schools (if any)
should be shown. The school map should also show the existing distribution of
pupils, classrooms, and teachers at each central and affiliate school, and any
hamlets with a critical mass of primary-school-aged children and no school.
Each school map should be accompanied by related education statistics:
enrollment data by grade and age, as well as information on the number of
primary-school-aged children in the catchment area not in school, for each
ethnic group in the catchment area.
44.         Using this school-mapping methodology already being used by the
MOET, the principals of the 40 experimental schools would also prepare
individual school maps of their schools. The principals would attend a one-
day workshop on school mapping organized by the MOET before beginning this
exercise. In addition to the information customarily shown on a school map,
these 40 maps would show the proposed distribution of pupils, classrooms, and
teachers after introducing multi-grade teaching at the central school and/or
in one or more of the affiliates, and the proposed distribution of pupils,
classrooms, and teachers at the end of the project. The 40 principals would
also complete the education statistics which accompany a school map. On the
basis of the school maps, the 40 principals would each select a teacher in one
of the affiliates who would introduce multi-grade teaching (and, later,
mother-tongue literacy teaching and the teaching of Vietnamese as the national
language) on an experimental basis.
45.         Finally, during this Organizational Phase the Assistant Project
Manager would conduct two one-week seminars, one in the North and one in the
South, with all the key staff who would participate in the implementation of
the component and would assign responsibilities and deadlines.
The Materials Develo=ment. Testing. and Publication Phase
46.         This phase would start during the firsW year of the project,
preferably at the beginning of the school year. Materials would be developed
sequentially, as follows: (a) during the first year of the project:
materials for multi-grade teaching; (b) during the second and subsequent years
of the project: materials for mother-tongue literacy teaching; and (c) during
the third and subsequent years of the project: materials, mostly diglotted,
for teaching Vietnamese as the national language and for the transition from
literacy training in ethnic minority languages to instruction in Vietnamese.
47.         The materials for multi-grade teaching would be supplementary to,
rather than replacements for, the existing textbooks. They are intended to
assist children in learning the curriculum and facilitate the work of the
teacher. They should have (a) explanations which are not in the normal
textbooks but which are necessary for children in multi-grade classrooms, (b)
additional exercises and practice activities which children in multi-grade
classrooms can carry out on their own, and (c) answers and feedback, so that
children know if they are doing the work correctly. It is expected that
children would follow the regular textbooks but would also have these mater-



-96-                     mm  6
Page 16 of 21
ials to supplement and expand on the subject matter presented in the regular
textbooks. The materials for multi-grade teaching, a few lessons at a time,
would be tested and revised throughout the first year of the project. The
revision of each few lessons would provide inputs for tha revision of the next
few lessons. A final check for accuracy and consistency of the entire set of
materials would be done during the first workshop for training teachers for
multi-grade teaching; printing of the final manuscripts would be completed
after the first workshop. Prior to the final printing, limited reproduction
by photocopy would be done until adequate testing and revision had been
completed.
48.         The Chief of UNICEF's Education Program would prepare a very
simple teacher's guide for the organization of classrooms in a multi-grade
teaching setting. The guide would include:
(a) a chapter on how to organize the use of the materials, i.e.,
how to make one of the pupils responsible for keeping the key
to the materials cupboard and for checking when something is
used;
(b) a chapter on keeping records of pupils' progress (work
progress charts);
(c) a chapter on how to get older children to help the younger
ones when they have difficulties;
(d) a chapter on how to plan lessons;
(e) a chapter on how to evaluate children's progress; and
(f) a chapter on how to obtain help from the commuity, with
examples of how teachers in other schools have done it.
UNICEF would distribute this guide to teachers in the 40 experimental schools,
review it with them, and correct it with them.
49.         Some of the materials for mother-tongue literacy teaching and for
teaching Vietnamese as the national language would be developed during
workshops conducted by the 16 Core Language Cadres with assistance from other
speakers of the eight languages selected and under the guidance of the
expatriate advisors, including the Applied Linguistics Advisor, the Ethnic
Minority Literacy Advisor, the Specialist in Curricula for Ethnic Minority
Children, and the eight language-specific Specialists in Ethnic Minority
Languages.



-97-
Page 17 of 21
The Teacher TraininU Phase
50.         Training of teadcers for multi-rade teaching situations.  This
training would be done in three practical Teacher Training Workshops, the
first lasting four weeks, the second lasting one week, and the third also
lasting one week. The first workshop, for one teacher each from the 40
experimental schools, would take place during the second year of the project,
before the start of the school year (about August). The second workshop would
take place three months after the first workshop (about November); the third
workshop would take place at the end of the school year (about May). These
workshops would be held at the eight selected Primary Teacher Training Schools
and would focus 2nls on the very practical things that a teacher needs to do
in a classroom offering multi-grade teaching. They would not deal with
subject matter but only with the simple tasks that need to be done to achieve
success in a multi-grade teaching situation. The series would be repeated,
for 160 teachers, in the third year (first and second workshops) and fourth
year (third workshop) of the project. A short summary of these three
workshops follows:
(a) In the first workshop, teachers would be given all the
materials produced for multi-grade teaching. They would
receive hands-on training to master the use of these mate-
rials. They would also be taught how to undertake a number
of activities designed to help them organize their activities
and monitor children's progress, including (i) lesson plans
for the first month of classes, (ii) an inventory of all the
material received, and space to indicate it is used; (iii) an
example of a pupil Work Progress Chart;!' and (iv) techniques
for encouraging older children in the school or in the
community to come to school every day and help the smaller
children who need assistance with their exercises.
(b) Teachers would bring to the Second workshop a list of the
good things that had happened in their schools, as well as a
list of difficulties encountered, and would try, with other
teachers, to identify the problems that need help from the
authorities, those requiring assistance from the specialists,
and those needing help from the community. Working together,
the teachers would prepare individual plans to obtain such
assistance.
(c) The third workJsh2 would be an evaluation workshop.  Teachers
would make recommendations for how to carry out the work in
new schools implementing multi-grade teaching in the future.
They would review the list of materials they had received and
would suggest which ones were not needed and which ones would
a'          A bulletin board with all the children's names on the left-hand
side and the chapters of the arithmetic and language books at the top. When a
child finishes the exercises in one chapter, he/she puts a dot on the chart in
the appropriate box. This chart permits the teacher to keep track of which
pupils need help.



-98-
Page 18 of 21
be modified.  They would also bring their pupil work progress
charts and compare them with those of the other teachers.
51.         Training of teachers i; the use of mother-tongue literacy
materials. This training, to be given in each of the eight Primary Teacher
Training Schools, would begin in the third year of the project, before the
beginning of the school year, with an initial one-month session for the 40
teachers, one from each of the 40 experimental schools, who had already been
trained in the use of mu'ti-grade teaching techniques and ziterials. This
initial training in the use of the mother-tongue literacy materials developed
during the second year the project would be followed by a one-week upgrading
workshop in each successive year of the project. In the fourth year of the
project, an additional 160 teachers would be trained in an initial one-month
session, again followed by one-week upgrading workshops in each successive
year of the project.
52.         Training of teachers in the teaching of Vietnamese as the national
language. This training, to be given at one site in the North and one in the
South, would take place in the fourth year of the project, before the
beginning of the school year, and would consist of a one-month session for the
40 teachers, one from each of the 40 experimental schools, who had already
been trained in the use of multi-grade teaching techniques and materials and
in the use of mother-tongue literacy materials. This course would include
training in the use of the Vietnamese language learning materials developed in
the third year of the project. Emphasis would be placed on oral speaking,
drills, games, and the situational use of Vietnamese. Attention would also be
given to reading and writing as well as grammar and acquisition of vocabulary.
In the fifth year of the project, an additional 160 teachers would be trained
in an initial one-month session.
The Classroom Imnlementation Phase
53.         The Classroom Implementation Phase would be divided into two
parts: an Experimental Part involving 40 schools, and an Expansion Part
during which the experiment would be expanded to an additional 160 schools.
54.         Experimental Part of the Classroom Implementation Phase.  A total
of 40 classrooms, one in each of the 40 experimental schools, would be
selected and provided with the materials for multi-grade teaching (to be
developed during the first year of the project) and, as they become ready,
materials for mother-tongue literacy teaching (to be developed during the
second year of the project) and materials for the teaching of Vietnamese as
the national language (to be developed during the third year of the project).
Each classroom should have about 15 to 20 children altogether, of whom most or
all should be ethnic minority children, and might include children at the
First, Second, and Third Grade levels, so that the teacher might put her
training to use and gain experience in teaching both in a multi-grade
situation and in bilingual education.
55.         The Experimental Part of the Classroom Implementation Phase would
begin in the second year of the project with the introduction of multi-grade
teaching tochniques and materials in 40 classrooms, one in each of the 40
expe.:imental schools. In the third year of the project, after the 40 teachers



-99-                    AM    6
Page 19 of 21
had received training in the use of materials for mother-tongue literacy
teaching, they would receive these materials and introduce mother-tongue
literacy teaching in the 40 classrooms. In the fourth year of the project,
after the 40 teachers had received training in the teaching of Vietnamese as
the national language, they would receive the appropriate materials and would
introduce the teaching of Vietnamese as the national language. As time
passed, additional teachers in the original 40 schools could begin to
introduce the new techniques in their classrooms by observing those teachers
who were already using them; for this purpose, by the fifth year of the
project, materials would be provided to all teachers in the 40 schools, so
that they could introduce the new techniques.
56.         The original 40 experimental classrooms would begin the bilingual
portion of their program in the beginning grade only. In that grade, all
instruction would be in the mother-tongue language, except for a conversation
class in spoken Vietnamese. During the next two grades, there would be a
gradual transition to Vietnamese as the language of instruction. There would
be regular supervision of the teachers and further in-service training
workshops for updating and for the introduction of the second- and third-grade
transition materials.
57.         The principals of the 40 experimental schools would keep detailed
records on attendance and student progress, according to a format furnished by
UNICEF.  This information, plus the insights gained during the training of
teachers for multi-grade teaching situations (the three workshops described
earlier), would be used to plan the expansion of the Classroom Implementation
Phase into 160 additional schools (following paragraph).
58.         Expansion Part of the Classroom Implementation Phase.  During tha
third year of the project, the program would expand beyond the original 40
schools. In each of the experimental areas, four additional schools would be
identified. These 160 schools would be selected according to the same
criteria used to select the original 40. An additional prerequisite for
initiating activities in any district would be the preparation of the district
school map and the information specified for all schools in the district. One
teacher from each of the 160 new schools would be trained in multi-grade
teaching in three workshops, the first of which would be held in the third
year of the project, before the beginning of the school year. These 160
teachers would receive training in mother-tongue literacy teaching in the
fourth year of the project, and training in the teaching of Vietnamese as the
national language in the fifth year of the project. They would also visit the
40 original schools to learn by observing those teachers who were already
teaching in an experimental classroom. Additional teachers in the 160 schools
could begin to introduce the new techniques in their classrooms by observing
those teachers who were already using them; for this purpose, by the sixth
year of the project, materials would be provided to all teachers in the 160
schools, so that they could introduce the new techniques.



-100-
Page 20 of 21
The Construction Phase
59.         Multi-grade teaching would be introduced In the original 40
classrooms during the second year of the project. At the end of that school
year. In the third year of the project, an evaluation would be made of the 40
experimental schools and of the extent to which they had been successful in
introducing multi-grade teaching.  Mhis evaluation would be concurrent with
the third teacher-training workshop for multi-grade teaching. Success in
introducing multi-grad. teaching would need to be demonstrated before
proceeding with construction activities.
60.         In preparation for the evaluation and for the tiird workshop, the
principals of the 40 experimental schools would redo their school maps,
showing how many classrooms were now used, how many grades they now offered,
places where multi-grade teaching was needed, and the condition of existing
facilities. These maps would be presented to the RCEKE and would be used to
select the Initial construction sites for the project. Construction would
start in the third year of the project where an experimental classroom had
functioned well for a year. Construction would begin in up to 40 schools
(assuming multl-grade teaching had been successfully introduced in all 40).
In the fourth year of the project, this process would be repeated for the 160
schools included in the Expansion Part of the Classroom Implementation Phase.
In the fifth and sixth year of the project, construction would continue at
other schools selected according to the same criteria or at other sites having
population clusters Including at least 20 children aged six through eleven who
lived more than 5 kilometers from an existing school or affiliate.
61.         As mentioned above, the Construction Phase would include, first,
the repair and rehabilitation of existing classrooms, the replacement of
seriously dilapidated classrooms which are beyond repair, and the construction
of now classrooms, if necessary, in sites selected according to agreed
criteria. It would also include the provision of latrines and water systems,
as necessary, in all schools benefiting from civil works under this component.
Finally, It would include the provision of essential school furniture
(including desks, chairs, blackboards, and storage cupboards) in all schools
benefiting from civil works under this component.
The Evaluation Phase
62.         Global information on the impact of inputs provided under the
component would be gained through the National Sample-Based Education
Assessment being supported under the Project. Under this activity, an
Assessment Specialist and a Statistical Data Analyst would visit Viet Nam
regularly during the first through the sixth years of the project to assist
the MOET in carrying out national assessments of primary-school children in
Viet Nam. The exercise would assess educational achievement among children
th-oughout Viet Nam in two subjects, Vietnamese and mathematics, and in two
gX -  - Gradt  3 and Grade 5. The sample 'ould include pupils from the eight
ethnic minorities aselected for inclusion in the component. The assessment
would be carried out every two years, i.e., three times during the
implementation of the Project. The first assessment would take place at the
end of the first year of the project, i.e., before the pedagogical aspects of



-101-
ANNEX 6
Page 21 of 21
the component had been introduced, and would therefore provide information on
the pre-project situation among these eight ethnic groups. The second and
third assessments, to take place at the end of the third and fifth years of
the project, would provide information on the impact of the pedagogical
interventions in some of the schools benefiting from the component as well as
control data from ethnic minority schools in the same areas not benefiting
from the component.
63.         In addition, more specific information on the impact of inputs
provided under the component would be gained through the Monitoring and
Evaluation Activity supported under the Project. Training, software,
hardware, and limited expatriate expertise provided under this component would
enable Vietnamese education managers to establish a systematic capacity to
monitor and evaluate the progress of government programs and projects in
primary education towards educational goals and objectives, including the
qualitative and quantitative impact of the component. An Assistant Project
Manager for Project Monitoring and Evaluation (part-time) would be respGnsible
for collecting information on project achievements, including information on
the progress of the component. This information would be reviewed (together
with other information on progress in project implementation) every year at
the Annual Review in October and would also be used as an input to the annual
work plan. An initial evaluation of multi-grade teaching would form part of
the Mid-Term Review scheduled for 1997, and a final evaluation of multi-grade
teaching, mother-tongue literacy teaching, and the teaching of Vietnamese as
the national language would be undertaken as an input to the Final Review at
the completion of the project. The results of these evaluations would guide
further work on ethnic minority education in Viet Nam, whether financed by the
Government or by external donors.

SOCIALIST REPUBLIC OF VIET NAM
PRIMARY EDUCTION PROJECT
INFORMATION TO BE INCLpDED IN SCHOOL MAPS
Under the project, detailed school maps would be prepared for each
district selected to receive civil works under the project. These maps would
indicate each school site and not only each school1'; i.e., for each school,
the map should show both the central school site and all affiliate school
sites. The statistics accompanying each school map should indicate:
(i)   The name of each school and, on a separate line, the name of each
school site (central and affiliate) belonging to that school;
(ii) The number of classes and the enrollment, by grade, at each s2h2ol
site (central and affiliate);
(iLi) The number of teachers, by grade, at each school site;
(iv) The number of children net in school aged 0-5, aged 6-10, and aged
11-14, at each site;
(v)   At each site, the number of classrooms in good condition, the
number needing repairs, and the number needing to be replaced
completely; and
(f)   The distance between the school site and the closest complete
school (i.e., a school site offering all five grades).
l'    In Viet Nam, a 'school" is defined as a central school and its affiliates,
if any; i.e., there may be several sites where children attend classes, but if
all are under the control of one principal, the several sites together are
considered only one school.

SOCIALIST REPUBLIC OF VIET NAM
PRIMARY EDUCATION PROJECT
CIVIL WORKS IN THE MEKONG DELTA
1.          One-fourth of all Vietnamese live in the Mekong Delta, making it
one of the most densely populated regions of the country. During project
preparation, the following agreed criteria were used to identify potential
sites in the Mekong Delta which would benefit from the construction program:
1.    Schools so seriously dilapidated that they are hazardous.
2.    Impermanent, low-lying schools that are flooded during a
substantial part of the school year.
3.    Rudimentary schools, particularly temporary thatch-roofed
structures without walls.
4.    Incomplete affiliate schools which are more than 5 kilometers from
an existing full primary school and cannot offer all five grades
for want of space.
5.   Schools using triple-shifting because of insufficient classroom
space.
6.    Population clusters living more than 5 kilometers from a school
that include at least 30 children aged 6-11.
7.    Other temporary or thatch-roofed structures in low-income areas.
2.          Construction choices for the Mekong Delta would be based on these
criteria. Accordingly, about 1,998 classrooms would be repaired and 2,973
would be replaced/expanded. This represents 18% of total need for repair and
replacement/expansion in each project province in the Mekong delta. Care
would be taken to provide flood-resistant classrooms as necessary and
technical assistance would be provided to develop a maintenance manual
appropriate for schools in the Mekong Delta. Suitable furniture (including
desks, chairs, blackboards, and storage cupboards) would be provided to
project schools where it does not already exist. Since most schools in the
Mekong Delta are located in the midst of areas of standing water during the
rainy season, special attention has to be given to health and environmental
considerations. Technical assistance would be provided under the project to
help design environmentally appropriate technologies and procedures for: (a)
construction and maintenance of about 5,000 suitable latrines (18% of need),
(b) provision of safe drinking water at about 500 sites (18t of need), and (c)
disposal of garbage. All schools benefiting from civil works under the
project also would be provided training in their maintenance.

SOCIALIST REPUBLIC OF VIET NAM
PRINARY EDUCATION PROJECT
CIVIL WORKS IN THE MOUNTAINOUS AREAS
1.          Viet Nam presently has 36 provinces which are regarded as wholly
or partially mountainous. Although sparsely populated, they cover the bulk of
the geographic area. Since it would be impossible to cover such a wide area
in the current project, the Government has identified eight out of the 36
provinces to participate in the project. These provinces (Son La, Hoa Binh,
Lang Son, Yen Bai, Lao Cai, Gia Lai, Kon Tum, and Ninh Thuan) were selected
because they contain a large number of ethnic minority inhabitants and UNICEF
has already been active in these areas. During preparation the following
criteria were used to identify proposed sites for school rehabilitation or
construction in the sparsely populated mountainous provinces:
(a)   Population clusters having a large number of children from one or
more of the eight selected ethnic minorities.
(b)   Rudimentary or temporary facilities, particularly thatch-roofed
structures without walls.
(c)   Areas where the population to be served is in the lowest 10% in
terms of per capita income.
(d)   Incomplete affiliate schools which are more than 5 kilometers from
an existing school and which at present can not offer all five
grades, either because of insufficient physical space or because
of the absence of a teacher skilled in multi-grade teaching.
(e)  Areas accessible by road.
(f)   Schools with staff willing to participate in project-supported
pedagogical activities targeted at ethnic minority children.
2.          In total, the construction component for the mountainous areas
would comprise: (a) repair of about 1,289 existing classrooms and
replacement/expansion of about 2,253 dilapidated classrooms (about 25% of the
total need per participating province); (b) provision of 5,666 suitable
latrines to schools (40% of needs); (c) provision of suitable furniture where
it does not already exist; (d) provision of water systems at some 567 school
sites (40% of needs).  All schools benefiting from civil works under the
project also would be provided training in the maintenance of the facilities.

SOCIALIST REPUBLIC OF VIET NAM
PRIMARY EDUCATION PROJECT
STUDY TOURS AND FELLOWSHIPS FOR EDUCATION MANAGERS
1.    Vietnamese education managers at various levels--national, provincial,
district, and school--would undertake various kinds of study tours abroad to
improve their ability to manage Viet Nam's primary-education system.
2.    A small number of study tours (30) would be to industrialized countries
so that selected higher-level Vietnamese education managers at the national
and provincial level could become aware of recent developments in education
management theory and practice. A few education planners at the national
level would take short courses (1-2 months) in education management and
planaing at institutions such as the International Institute of Education
Planning in Paris. Other national and provincial education managers would be
assigned to a Ministry of Education or a Provincial Education Service in an
industrialized country for one month, in order to observe how high-level
education managers use school-mapping to make decisions regarding (a) the
creation of new schools; (b) the addition of classrooms to existing schools;
(C) the planning of future school enrollment and the allocation of children to
schools; and (d) the scheduling of classes so that all classrooms and
specialized facilities are used efficiently.
3.    All candidates for short courses or study tours to industrialized
countries should have the following gualifications: (a) ten years of
experience as an education manager at the national or provincial level; (b) a
basic fluency in either English or the language of the country to be visited;
and (c) an age of 35 to 50 years.
4.   A larger number of study tours would be to nearby countries, so that
selected Vietnamese education managers at the national, provincial, district,
and school level could see how these countries are presently managing their
primary-education systems and schools and how they dealt, at earlier stages in
their development, with education management problems similar to those Viet
Nam is presently facing.
5.   Of the study tours to nearby countries, 110 would be for a duration of
one month and would be for (a) selected Ministerial and provincial education
managers and (b) selected Ministerial and provincial education planning and
statistics officers. The education managers would observe how managerial
decisions are made in regard to the creation ot new schools, the distribution
of children among existing schools, the development of rational school
networks, and effective supervision of education systems. The education
planning and statistics officers would visit education planning and statistics
units in nearby countries to see the kinds of data and information collected
and how these data are collected, verified, processed, and used.
6.    Candidates for the one-month study tours to nearby countries should have
the following galiflcations: (a) ten years of experience as an education



-113-                   ANNE  12
Page 2 of 3
manager at the Ninisterial, provincial, or district level; (b) a basic fluency
in either English or the language of the country to be visited; and (c) an age
of 35 to 50 years.
7.    Up to 500 of the study tours to nearby countries would be for a duration
of two weeks and would be for selected district education managers and
selected primary-school principals. These scholarships would allow these
managers to observe (a) successful experiences with multi-graded teaching, (b)
successful bilingual education practices; (c) successful school scheduling
practices; (d) school records and school accounts; (e) the use of educational'
resources for distance education, including radio and correspondence; (f)
successful teacher supervision practices; (g) teacher evaluation practices;
and (h) pupil evaluation practices.
8.    Candidates for the two-week study tours to nearby countries should have
the following qualifications: (a) five years of experience as a district
education manager or school principal; and (b) an age of 30 to 50 years.
9.    Since there have been few opportunities to use the most commonly used
international languages in Viet Nam in recent years, short in-service
refresher courses in English and French (6-8 weeks) would be organized, before
the departure of the candidates, in Hanoi, Hue, and Ho Chi Ninh City for
candidates for the short courses or study tours to industrialized countries
and for candidates for the one-month study tours to nearby countries. All
three cities have Foreign Language Institutes which have adequate capacity
(facilities and staff) to offer these short courses. Candidates for the
shorter study tours to nearby countries would make use of interpreters; both
the Ministry of Education and Training and the Provincial Education Services
have an adequate nuaber of interpreters for this purpose.
10.  Upon their return to Viet Nam, these education managers would give
semunars at the national, provincial, and district Levels, to disseminate the
information, observations, and new working methods learned during the study
tours. In particular, those education managers who have benefited from study
tours on school mapping, the rationalization of school networks, multi-graded
teaching, and bilingual education would share their findings with their
colleagues.
11. Short grainin; courses would also be organized for executive secretaries
and for planning, finance, and statistics officers in the Ministry of
Education and Training and in the Provincial Education Services to enable them
to learn how to use the new software and hardware provided under the project.
12.  The study tours and training courses for the Educational Management
Component are summarized below:
(a) Scholarships (covering round-trip air travel, subsistence, internal
transportation, and incidentals) to industrialized countries.
Trainees: Ministerial and selected provincial education managers
and planners.
Number:    30.
Duration: 1-2 months.



-114-                    AM    12
Page 3 of 3
(b) Scholarships to nearby countries such as Thailand, Malaysia, and the
Philippines.
Trainees:  Ministerial and provincial education managers;
Ministerial and provincial education planning and
statistics officers.
Number:    110.
Duration:  I month.
(c) Scholarships to nearby countries such as Thailand, Malaysia, or the
Philippines.
Trainees: District education managers; selected primary-school
principals.
Number:    500.
Duration:  2 weeks.
(d) National and regional seminars within Viet Nam.
Trainees:  Primary-school principals.
Number:    12,000.
Duration:  2 weeks.
(e) In-service refresher languages courses in English or French for
candidates for courses abroad.
Trainees:  Candidates for courses abroad (1-2 months) whose present
fluency in English or French (depending on the country of
destination) requires improvement.
Number:    Up to 140.
Duration:  6-8 weeks.
(f) On-site training courses on new equipment.
Trainees: Executive secretaries and planning, finance, and
statistics officers in the Ministry of Education and
Training and in the Provincial Education Services.
Number:    About 150.
Duration:  1-5 days.

SOCIALIST REPUBLIC OF VIET NAM
PRMD=  EDUCATION PROJECT
SAMPLE-EASED NATIONAL ASSESSMENT OF PRIMARY EDUCATION IN VIET NWM
Introduction
1.          This annex includes a description, schedule, and plan for a high-
quality sample-based national assessment in Viet Nam which would (a) yield
reliable data for monitoring and evaluating the educational impact of the
Primary Education Project and (b) establish a valuable, long-term monitoring
device for improve Viet Nam's education system.
Basic Requirements for the Assessment
2.          The following are the basic requirements for carrying out the
assessment successfully:
(a)   Educational achievement would be assessed in two subjects:
Vietnamese and mathematics;
(b)   The assessment would take place every two years, e.g., in
1994, 1996, 1998 etc.;
(c)   Achievements would be assessed in two grades:  Grade 3 and
Grade 5;
(d)   The assessment would be carried out nationally (i.e., not
limited to certain regions only);
(e)   The sample would include pupils from eight ethnic minori-
ties, who would also be assessed in Vietnamese and mathemat-
ics (but not in their ethnic minority languages);
(f)   The assessment would be administered by specially trained
staff (i.e., not by the pupils' teachers);
(g)   The results would be scored in Hanoi;
(h)   Two microcomputers would be available for drawing the sample
and conducting data analyses;
(i)   Computer programs would be purchased or leased for sampling,
data entry, and data analyses;
(j) Technical assistance would be made available for training
and assistance;
(k)   Reports would be provided to the Ministry of Education and
Training (MOET), policy-makers (e.g., the State Planning
Committee), administrators, teachers, and the public;



-118-                   ANNEX 14
Page 2 of 7
(1)   Trends would be measured over time to show improvements (or
declines) in educational achievement;
(m)  The tests would reflect minimum learning level objectives
and would be kept secure; and
(n)   Information on pupil, teacher, and school backgrounds would
be collected via questionnaires, and assessment results
would be related to pupil performance.
Tasks
3.          Assessment Development.  Tests would be based on minimum learning
level objectives. Test questions would be written by committees of teachers
and experts, who would write one and one half times as many questions (150%)
as eventually needed, so that questions giving equivocal results during the
pro-testing could be discarded. Manuals would be written containing direc-
tions for administering the assessments; scoring guides would also be pre-
pared. Questions would be pre-tested in a small number (25-50) of schools.
Questions would then be revised on the basis of pre-test results. Final
tests, manuals, and scoring guides would then be assembled.
4.          SaMoLe Selection.  A list of all primary or basic-education
schools in the country would be prepared, with the primary-school enrollment
at each school. The list would be separated into "strata." Schools and
alternate schools would be selected rardomly. The selected schools would be
asked to prepare lists of all pupils in Grades 3 and 5. Students would then
be randomly selected from each school. Excluded student procedures would be
developed. Results would be "weighted' after administration of the assess-
ment.
5.          Administration of the Assessment.  Administrators (25-50) would be
selected and trained. The people responsible for the assessment would insist
that the assessment be administered using rigorous, standardized procedures.
Quality-control procedures would be developed for use at 25% of the schools.
6.          Scoring.  Professional scorers would be selected and trained to
score the tests using scoring guides.  The people resF,nsible for the assess-
ment would insist on standardized procedures for scoring. Duplicate scoring
of 50% of the results would be use to ensure quality control.
7.          Preparation of Data Base (Central Processing Unit Program).  A
data base would be prepared by entering pupils' responses and data from the
pupil, teacher, and school questionnaires. A quality control data base would
also be prepared.
8.          Data Analyses. The following analyses would be carried out:  (a)
an item analysis of the results for every question; (b) bias analyses (gender;
region; ethnic minority group); (c) a percent correct analysis; (d) sub-group
analyses (gender; rural/urban; type of teacher; etc.); and (e) analyses of
relationships with family. home, and school factors.
9.          Renorting of Findings.  An analysis of the findings would be
carried out by a committee of experts, teachers, and administrators. The



-119-                   ANNEX 14
Page 3 of 7
report(s) would then be written and published. A press conference would be
called to announce the publication of the report.

Minorities/Mountain Areas Component.
The other four Sociolinguistic Researchers will work out of the Ethnic
Minority Implementation Unit for the South in Wha Trang, where they will
report to the Deputy Assistant Project Manager for the Ethnic Minorities/
Mountain Areas Component. Most of their work, however, will be carried out in
one or more of the three Central and Southern provinces selected for inclusion
in the Ethnic Minorities/Mountain Areas Component.
Reguired Oualifications
The Sociolinguistic Researchers should:
1.    be at least 25 years old;
2.    have a training in sociolinguistic surveying methods;
3.    have experience in ethnic minority education and local culture in Viet
Nam;
4.    speak one of the eight ethnic minority languages selected for inclusion
in the Ethnic Minorities/Mountain Areas Component with native fluency,
and also speak Vietnamese; and
5.    be able to work well with their national colleagues, including ethnic
minority peoples, and international counterparts.

Duties and Responsibilities

Minorities/Mountain Areas Component to determine language groups, populations,
locations, dialect differences, and other sociolinguistic features that should
be taken into account in developing educational materials for children
speaking the eight languages selected for inclusion in the Ethnic Minorities/
Mountain Areas Component.
TiminiL and RDration of Servige

Year 1 and will work for a period of 4 months each.

Terms of Reference: School Mappers (2 individuals)(Vietnamese)
Hierarchical ResDonsibility
The two School Mappers will work in Hanoi with the (expatriate)
Specialist in School Mapping in the Ministry of Education and Training's
Department of Finance and Planning, where they will report to the Director.
Reguired Oualifications
The School Mappers should:
1.    be at least 25 years old;
2.    have at least some training in education and school mapping;
3.    have experience in educational research and graphic representation;
4.    speak Vietnamese (a knowledge of English and one or more ethnic minority
languages would be useful); and
5.    be able to work well with their national colleagues, minority peoples,
and international counterparts.
Duties and Resgonsibilities
The School Mappers shall assist in doing school mapping in all the 24
provinces selected for inclusion in the project. This activity will help the
Government, Parents' Associations, and potential donors target investments to
the neediest areas, making better use of scarce education resources. It will
also enable the Ministry of Education and Training to develop a long-term plan
for priority investments in education. The exercise should include the
following activities:
1.    Map the existing physical infrastructure in the education sector,
showing classrooms in good condition, those that need repair and the
extent of repairs needed (roof; walls; floor), and those that need to be
replaced entirely.
2.    Undertake a census of existing school furniture and teaching aids,
including data on numbers of children who do not have a complet set of
textbooks.



-153-                  AM  is
Pag 30 of 46
3.    Show on the map the present and projected numbers of enrollments.
4.    Determine the number of children living In the area shown on the map who
are ngt envolled In school, and indicate how far away they live from the
nearest classroom.
S.    Elaborate plans, by district, for maklng more rational use of existing
school facilities.
6.    Calculate (and show on.the map) the needs for now classrooms as well as
repair and replacement of existing classrooms.
7.    Calculate gross and net enrollment ratios by coune, district, and
province at each level of education.
8.   Determine where and to what extent multi-graded teaching methods are
required.
Timing and Duration of Servlee
The School Kappers will work on school mapping continually throughout
the six years of the project.

SOCIALIST REPUBLIC OF VIET NAM
PRI1ARY EDUCATION PROJECT
CONSTRUCTION COMPONENT
Terms of Reference: Specialist in Appropriate School Construction
Technologies (1 individual)(expatriate)
Hierarchical Responsibility
The Specialist in Appropriate School Construction Technologies will work
in Hanoi in the Ministry of Education and Training's School Research and
Design Institute, where he/she will report to the Director (Assistant Project
Manager for Construction). Three of the Institute's local staff will be
assigned to work on a full-time basis with the Specialist.
Reauired Ouslifications
The Specialist in Appropriate School Construction Technologies should:
1.   be at least 45 years old;
2.   have at least a university diploma in architecture or civil engineering,
with special training in environmentally and technologically appropriate
school construction technologies;
3.   have at least 15 years of experience in school construction in a
developing country, preferably in Southeast Asia (experience in building
schools in regions subject to floods and/or typhoons would be highly
desirable);
4.   be fluent in English (knowledge of Vietnamese would be useful); and
5.   be able to work well with his/her national counterparts and have a
pragmatic, cost-conscious approach to determining appropriate solutions
for construction of schools in a resource-constrained society.
Duties and Responsibilities
The Specialist in Appropriate School Construction Technologies will
undertake a series of activities designed to enable the Ministry of Education.
Provincial Education Services, District Education Services, and school
Parents' Associations to use their money more effectively in providing and
maintaining education infrastructure. (The exercise should also provide
useful results for other infrastructure sectors.) Specifically, he/she will
have the following duties and responsibilities:



-162-                  AMEX 15
Page 39 of 46
1.    Study and develop appropriate construction technologies needed to build
tyDhoon-resistant schools, particularly in the cities along Viet Nam's
coast, which is subject to typhoons. (Many schools built recently by
the education authorities and by external donors have been destroyed by
typhoons, although some other buildings in these areas have lasted for
centuries.)
2.    Study and develop appropriate construction technologies needed to build
flood-resistant schools, particularly in tle Mekong Delta, which is
subject to annual flooding. (The present practice of building "tempo-
rary" schools which need major repairs every year and replacement every
second or third year is not cost-effective.)
3.    Study and develop appropriate construction solutions consistent with
local resources, and combining reliance on local materials and
traditional building techniques with the findings of contemporary
research, for other parts of Viet NaM (in particular, for the mountain
areas and for other urban areas, i.e., those not subject to typhoons).
4.    Develop suitable maintenance procedures and maintenance manuals
appropriate for different types of schools and different parts of the
country (urban and rural areas in the North, Center, and South; Mekong
Delta; coastal areas; mountain areas; etc.), setting forth appropriate
routine and preventive maintenance procedures to be undertaken by school
personnel.
5.    Elaborate a short training course (to be taught by Vietnamese staff of
the School Design and Construction Institute) in appropriate routine and
preventive maintenance procedures for different types of construction,
to be offered to principals and teachers in all schools benefiting from
civil works under the project (and, later, to principal and teachers of
other schools as well).
Timing and Duration of Service
The Specialist in Appropriate School Construction Technologies will
begin his/her work in Project Year 1 and will work for a period of 3 months on
the first activity mentioned above, 3 months on the second activity, 3 months
on the third activity, and 3 months on the fourth and fifth activities.



-163-                  ANNEX 15
Page 40 of 46

developing country, preferably in Southeast Asia;
4.    be fluent in English (knowledge of Vietnamese would be useful);
5.    be able to work well with his/her national counterparts and have a
pragmatic, cost-conscious approach to determining appropriate solutions
for school sanitation in a resource-constrained society; and
6.   have a demonstrated desire to protect and conserve the environment.
Duties and Resonasibilities
The work of the Specialist in School Sanitation will enable school
managers to provide a healthier environment to school children, in both
existing and new schools, particularly schools in the Nekong Delta, where
annual flooding takes place and where many schools are located in the midst of
areas of standing water, but also schools in mountain areas and schools in
crowded urban areas. He/she will have the following duties and
responsibilities:



-164-                   ANNE  15
Page 41 of 46
1.    Design appropriate technologies for construction and maintenance of
suitable latrines in those areas of the Mekong Delta subject to annual
flooding.
2.    Develop appropriate procedures for disposal of garbage in those areas of
the Mekong Delta subject to annual flooding.
3.    Design appropriate technologies for provision of safe drinking water in
those areas of the Mekong Delta subject to annual flooding.
4.    Test and develop environmentally appropriate procedures for sRraving fox
noxious insects (e.g., mosquitoes and termites) in those areas of the
Mekong Delta or the mountains subject to such pests.
5.    Develop suitable maintenance procedures and maintenance manuals
appropriate for latrines and water supply in different parts of the
country (urban and rural areas in the North, Center, and South; Mekong
Delta; mountain areas; etc.), setting forth appropriate routine and
preventive maintenance procedures to be undertaken by school personnel.
6.    Elaborate training courses for school principals and teachers in the
appropriate maintenance of these facilities and the implementation of
the new procedures. These courses should include specific guidance to
teachers on how to demonstrate to students (a) the proper use and
maintenance of latrines and (b) other routine measures to keep the
school environment clean and healthy.
Timing and Duration of Service
The Specialist in School Sanitation will begin his/her work in Project
Year 1 and will work for a period of 9 months.

SOCIALIST REPUBLIC OF VIET NA1
PRtIMARY EDUCATION PROJE£
TESHNICAL ASSISTANCE
MONITORING AND ASSESSE   COMQN
Terms of Reference: Assessment Specialist (1 individual)(expatriate)
Hierarchical Responsibility
The Assessment Specialist will work in Hanoi in the Ministry of
Education and Training's Department of General Education, where he/she will
report to the Director.
Required Oualifications
The Assessment Specialist should:
1.    be at least 35 years old;
2.    have at least a university degree in education, statistics, or a related
social science and also have had special training in techniques of
sampling and educational assessment;
3.   have at least 10 years of experience in the field of educational
assessment, preferably at least part of it in one or more developing
countries;
4.    have a broad understanding of the potential and limitations of census-
and sample-based assessments and testing programs;
5.    have a knowledge of (a) the various possible designs of instruments to
use for assessments and testing programs and (b) the kinds of data and
contextual information they can yield;
6.   understand how assessment projects can be developed, organized, and
implemented;
7.    be fluent in English (knowledge of Vietnamese would be useful); and
8.    be able to work well with his/her national counterparts but have
sufficient strength of personality to insist that the exercise be
carried out in a manner which will not compromise the results.
Duties and Responsibilities
Working in tandem with the Statistical Data Analyst, the Assessment
Specialist will have the following duties and responsibilities:



-166-                    ANNEX 15
Page 43 of 46
1.    Explain in detail to the Vietnamese educational authorities the
procedures and activities to be undertaken for a national assessment of
primary education.
2.    Oversee the collection of information on pupil, teacher, and school
backgrounds using questionnaires.
3.    Assist in organizing committees of teaciers and experts to write test
questions, manuals containing directions for administering the assess-
ment, and scoring guides.
4.    Oversee the pre-testing of the test questions in a small number of
schools and the revision of the test questions on the basis of the pre-
testing results.
5.    Oversee the preparation of final test questions, manuals containing
directions for administering the assessment, and scoring guides.
6.   Organize the preparation of a list of all primary or basic-education
schools in the country and oversee the separation of the schools on the
list into 'strata." Oversee the random selection of schools and
alternate schools for the assessment, the preparation of lists of all
pupils in Grades 3 and 5 in the selected schools, and the random
selection of students in each selected school.
7.    Assist in selecting and then train 25-50 test administrators. Insist
that the assessment be administered using rigorous, standardized
procedures. Develop quality-control procedures for use at 25% of the
schools.
S.    Assist in selecting professional scorers, and train them to score the
tests using scoring guides. Insist on standardized procedures for
scoring.
9.    Assist in preparing a data base by entering pupils' responses and data
from the pupil, teacher, and school questionnaires.
10.   Oversee the carrying out of the following analyses:  (a) an item
analysis of the results for every question; (b) bias analyses (gender;
region; ethnic minority group); (c) a percent correct analysis; (d) sub-
group analyses (gender; rural/urban; type of teacher; etc.); and (e)
analyses of relationships with family, home, and school factors.
11.   Teach a committee of experts, teachers, and administrators how to
analyze the findings. Oversee the writing and publishing of a report(s)
and the calling of a press conference to announce the publication of the
report.
Timing and Duration of Service
There will be 19 visits of varying duration to Viet Nam over a six-year
period by one or the other of the two specialists, according to the following
schedule:



-167-                   ANNEX 15
Page 44 of 46

-190-                        &MEX  is
Page 1 of 15
SOCIALIST REPUBLIC OF VIET NAM
PRIMARY EDUCATION PROJECT
PROJECT MANAGEMENT
1.        National Level.  A National Project Director', assisted by a
National Project Manager assigned to the project full-time, would coordinate
the implementarion of all project-related activities. The National Project
Director would receive advice and assistance from a newly-formed Consultative
Council, comprising representatives of agencies outside the MOET such as the
State Planning Committee (SPC), the Ministry of Finance (MOF), and the State
Bank of Viet Nam. He/she would be assisted by a National Project Management
Committee composed of directors of all MOET departments participating in the
project. In addition to overall coordination of project implementation, the
National Project Management Committee would be responsible for liaison with
other government agencies and IDA.
2.        The full-time National Project Manager would be responqible for
day-to-day oversight of all aspects of project implementation. He/she would
be supported by seven Assistant Project Managers and three Deputy Assistant
Project Managers, as follows:
(a) An internationally-recruited Assistant Project Manager for
Administration and Finance (full-time), to oversee (inter alLa)
project accounting and auditing, reporting requirements, ICB
procurement, and processing of withdrawal applications.
(b) An Assistant Project Manager for Project Monitoring and Evaluation
(full-time), who would be responsible for preparing the Annual Work
Program, organizing the Annual Reviews and Project Completion
Reporting exercises, collecting information on project
achievements, and organizing the sample-based assessments.
(c) An Assistant Project Manager for Ethnic Minorities (full-time),
based in Hanoi, assisted by a full-time Deputy Assistant Project
Manager based in Nha Trang, who would be responsible for overseeing
implementation of specific pedagogical interventions targeted at
ethnic minorities (See Chart 3: Management Structure for the
Ethnic Minority Component).
(d) An Assistant Project Manager for Construction (full-time), based in
Hanoi, assisted by a Deputy Assistant Project Nanager for
Construction (full-time), based in Ho Chi Minh City, to oversee LCB
procurement in urban areas and generally the implementation of the
project's construction components in the North and the South,
respectively.
The current First Vice-Minister of the MOET has been nominated.



-191-                         ANNEX  18
Page 2 of 15
(e)  An Assistant Project Manager for Textbooks (part-time), supported
by a Deputy Assistant Manager for Textbooks (part-time), to be
filled by the Director of the EPH in Hanoi and the Director of the
branch office of the EPH in Ho Chi Minh City, respectively.
(f)  An Assistant Project Manager for Human Resources and Training
(full-time), based in Hanoi, responsible for organizing and
coordinating the study tours and fellowships as well as the local
training foreseen under the project.
(g)  An Assistant Project Manager for Teacher Training (part- time),
based in Hanoi, responsible for organizing the teaching aids
competitions and the primary teacher training research supported
under the project.
3.        The National Project Manager and the Assistant Project Managers
would together constitute a Project Coordination Unit (PCU). The MOET has
agreed to assign a definite hierarchical and bureaucratic position to this
Unit within the organizational structure of the Ministry. Although the PCU
would initially coordinate the implementation of only the IDA-assisted
project, the MOET's intention is that this unit would eventually be
responsible for overseeing implementation of all foreign-assisted projects.
4.        Provincial Level.  A Provincial Project Coordinator, attached to
the Provincial Education Service, would coordinate and oversee project
activities within the province in question. His/her primary responsibility
would be implementing the project; accordingly, the incumbent would be
relieved of other responsibilities. Duties would include: (a) undertaking M
Dost review of School Committee plans; (b) ensuring delivery of nationally-
procured equipment to districts and Primary Teacher Training School(s); (c)
supervising (through the Provincial Education Service's Construction Unit)
construction activities in the province; (d) ensuring timely provision of
funds from provincial budgets for the project; (e) ensuring the flow of funds
from the national to the district level; and (f) following agreed criteria,
selecting staff from the Provincial Education Service to participate in
project-sponsored training. A Provincial Management Committee (composed of
the director of the Provincial Teacher Training School, other members of the
Provincial Education Service involved in project implementation, and other
appropriate officials of the province, including a representative of the
Provincial Financial Service) would provide assistance to the Provincial
Project Coordinator.
5.        District Level.  A District Project Coordinator, attached to the
District Education Service, would coordinate and oversee project activities
within the district in question. His/her primary responsibility would be
implementing project; accordingly, the incumbent would be relieved of other
responsibilities. Duties would include: (a) supervising civil works in the
district (if any); (b) reviewing/approving School Committee plans; (c)
ensuring delivery of equipment to the district's primary schools; (d) ensuring
timely provision of funds from district budgets for the project; (e) ensuring
flow of funds from the provincial to the school level; and (f) following
agreed criteria, selecting staff from the district's Education Service and
primary schools to participate in project-sponsored t-mining. The District
Project Coordinator would be assisted by a District Manak,qment Committee



-192-                        AM    is
Page 3 of 15
composed of school principals involved in project implementation and other
appropriate officials of the district, including a representative of the
District Financial Service. In addition, the District Project Coordinator
would be assisted by a part-time project monitoring expert responsible for
collecting information on project achievements and preparing periodic reports
to be submitted to the District Management Committee.
6.        Scg.l jLevl.  At the school level, principals would be assisted
(as is presently the case) by a School Committee chaired by the principal and
including representatives of the Parents' Association and the local People's
Committee (commune People's Committee, in rural areas; neighborhood People's*
Comaittee, in urban areas). Where sufficiently large population clusters
exist without a school, local initiative would form an appropriately-
constituted New School Committee on the basis of information received from the
District education authorities.
7.        Each School Committee (whether existing or New) would prepare a
plan for the repair, rehabilitation, or construction to be undertaken under
the project. These plans would include:
(a) Designs. Designs for proposed civil works would be prepared by
private (or public) architects on the basis of national norms for
school construction supplied by the RDICS.
(b) Estirated costs.
(c) A proposed financing plan which would include and reflect the local
contributions (from Parents' Association and/or the local People's
Committee).
(d) A proposed implementation schedule.
(e) An estimate of probable recurrent costs resulting from project-
supported activities (including salaries of any new teachers
required), a proposal for financing additional recurrent costs, and
a commitment by the local People's Committee and the Parents'
Association to providing the funds required.
(f) Plans for schools in ethnic minority areas (and mountainous areas
with sufficient population and no school) would be prepared by the
existing or New School Committees with the help of district
education authorities and would include the following additional
elements:
(g) Number of primary-age children in the catchment area by gender and
ethnic affiliation.
(h) Proposals for multi-grade teaching and resulting number of new
classrooms needed (if any).
(i) Proposals for mother-tongue literacy and for teaching Vietnamese as
the national language, as relevant.



-193-                         Am         la
Page 4 of 15
8.        All such plans would be submitted to the respective District
Mnagement Committee for review and approval; in particular, the District
Management Committee would ensure that the proposed designs conforted to
national norms. Following approval, copies of the plans would be submitted to
the respective Provincial Management Committee and the Project Coordination
Unit (either the head office in Hanoi or the branch office in Ho Chi Minh
City) for ^ post review and eventual review by IDA supervision missions.
9.        During project preparation, IDA and UNICEF worked closely with
RDICS staff to develop cost-effective designs for schools and latrines, and
these designs should become the basis for the new construction. The
Government agreed that all civil works would be carried out based upon the
preparation, submission, and approval of a satisfactory plan, and that these
plans would be based upon designs reviewed by IDA during project preparation.
In the case of Phase Three civil works (in mountainous areas), these plans
must also reflect the phasing of additional pedagogic inputs which would
precede construction.
10.       Finally, each school would have a site manager, consisting of the
principal or head teacher. This individual would represent the school during
project implementation in order to: (a) minimize the impact of construction
on the learning environment; (b) receive, inspect, and accept shipments of
books, materials, and furniture on behalf of the school; (c) schedule staff
training (especially in multi-grade or bi-lingual teaching techniques); (d)
interact with provincial, district, and local authorities to maximize impact
of inputs on education activities; and (e) -collect school-level data for use
by Assistant Project Manager for Monitoring and Evaluation.

SOCIALIST REPUBLIC OF VIET NAM
PRIMARY EDUCATION PROJECT
FLOW OF FUNDS AND ACCOUNTING FORMATS
1.    It had previously been proposed that the flow of funds within Viet Nam
take place through the network of the State Bank of Viet Nam, which had its
head office in Hanoi and branches in every province and district. Since
October 1, 1990, however, the State Bank no longer functions as a commercial
bank, i.e., handling customers' accounts. These accounts have been
transferred to newly created commercial banks (Agricultural Banks at the
district level, the Industrial Banks at the provincial level, and the Foreign
Trade Bank at the national level) set up under the new banking regime. The
State Bank retains its regulatory and supervisory roles, and also handles all
government accounts.
2.    The NOET and the Provincial Education Service of Hanoi have accounts in
the State Bank's head office in Hanoi. Every other Provincial Education
Service has its account in the Industrial Bank in the provincial capital.
Each District Education Service has an account in the Agricultural Bank in the
district capital; the district's primary schools also have accounts in this
bank. A transfer from an account in the head office of the State Bank in
Hanoi to an account in an Industrial Bank in a provincial capital can take up
to five days; from the Industrial Bank to-an Agricultural Bank in a district
capital, about two days; and from one account in an Agricultural Bank (e.g.,
that of the District Education Service) to another account in the same bank
(e.g., that of a primary school), less than an hour.
3.    A school principal can withdraw money from the school's account in the
district's Agricultural Bank by writing a check, and does so to make large
payments to a receiver who also has A bank account. For smaller payments
(e.g., for normal small-scale civil works and for teachers' monthly salary
payments), however, the school accountant normally withdraws money in cash
from the account at the district's Agricultural Bank and makes payments in
cash.
4.    In October of each year (as is presently the case), the MOET would
request the MOF to include in the expenditures budget for the forthcoming year
the full amount of funds necessary to cover national contributions to project
implementation expenditures. The MOF would also include in its revenue budget
the amount expected to be recovered from IDA in reimbursements during that
year. After further elaboration in the MOP, the draft budget would be
discussed by the SPC (as is presently the case) and then submitted to the
National Assembly for approval, which normally occurs in December. After
January 1, the MOET would be authorized to start making expenditures according
to the approved budget allocations, drawing on its account in the State Bank
of Viet Nam in Hanoi. Funds needed for expenditures at the lower levels which
are to be financed by the national government would be periodically
transferred to the accounts of the respective Provincial Education Services in
the Industrial Banks in those provinces, using the normal budgetary transfer
procedures outlined above. Funds needed for expenditures at the school level
would be periodically transferred, first, to the accounts of the respective
District Education Services in the Agricultural Banks in those districts, and
then to the accounts of the respective primary schools in the same banks, also
using the normal budgetary transfer procedures.
